Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
The Third Division of Possibles: Immaterial Beings
Imam Razi presents the third category of possibles, the theologians' argument against it, and his logical critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third category of possible beings (immaterial entities), the theologians' argument against their existence, and Imam Razi's critique showing why sharing negative attributes does not imply equality of essence.

Core Idea
Sharing negative attributes does not mean sharing the same essence.

Imam Razi continues his classification of possibles by introducing a third category: beings that are neither bodies nor accidents. He notes that most philosophers accept such beings while theologians reject them. In this passage, he presents the theologians' strongest argument and then dismantles it by clarifying a logical point about negative vs. positive attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third category
Read the opening definition: the third category is 'the thing that is neither a body nor subsistent in a body.' Understand that 'mutaḥayyiz' refers to a body occupying space, and 'qāʾim bil-mutaḥayyiz' refers to an accident.
This category includes immaterial souls, intellects, and angels.
2
Understand the theologians' argument
Theologians argue that if such a being existed, it would be equal to God in not being a body or accident, implying equality in essence, which would confuse the Necessary and the possible. Pay attention to the logical chain.
3
Grasp Razi's critique about negative concepts
Razi calls the argument weak. He points out that equality in a negative concept (like 'not being a body') does not necessitate equality in essence. Every two different essences share many negations; that is trivial.
Negative concepts are too broad to determine identity.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Razi hints that even equality in positive attributes does not entail equality in essence. This opens the door for affirming immaterial beings distinct from God without compromising divine uniqueness.
This is a key move in defending philosophical theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث من أقسام الممكنات : الشيء الذي لا يكون متحيزا ولا يكون قائما بالمتحيز. واعلم أن جمهور الفلاسفة يثبتون هذا القسم. وجمهور المتكلمين ينكرونه. وأقوى دلائل المنكرين أن قالوا لو حصل موجود سوى الله تعالى بهذه الصفة، لكان ذلك الموجود مساويًا لذات الله تعالى في أنه ليس بمتحيز، ولا قائم بالمتحيز. ولو حصلت المساواة في هذه الصفة لحصلت المساواة في تمام الماهية ولو حصلت المساواة في تمام الماهية لزم القول بأنه إما أن يكون الواجب ممكنا أو يكون الممكن واجبًا . وكل ذلك محال. واعلم أن هذه الحجة ضعيفة، وذلك لأن الاستواء في كونه ليس بمتحيز ولا حال في المتحيز، استواء في مفهوم سلبي. والاستواء في المسلوب، لا يوجب الاستواء في الماهية ؛ لأن كل ماهيتين مختلفتين فلا بد وأن يتشاركا في سلب كل ما عداهما عنهما وإذا بطلت هذه المقدمة سقطت هذه الحجة. بل نقول : الاستواء في الصفات الثبوتية، لا يوجب الاستواء في الماهية؛ لأن
The third division of the divisions of possibles: the thing that is neither a body (mutaḥayyiz) nor subsistent in a body (qāʾim bil-mutaḥayyiz). Know that the majority of the philosophers affirm this division, while the majority of the theologians (mutakallimūn) deny it. The strongest proof of those who deny it is that they said: If there existed an other-than-God with this attribute, then that existent would be equal to the essence of God in that it is not a body nor subsistent in a body. And if equality in this attribute occurred, then equality in the complete essence (al-māhiyya) would occur; and if equality in the complete essence occurred, then it would necessarily follow that either the Necessary (al-wājib) is possible or the possible is necessary. All of that is impossible. Know that this argument is weak. That is because equality in being neither a body nor an accident in a body is equality in a negative concept (mafhūm salbī). And equality in what is negated does not necessitate equality in the essence; because every two different essences must share in the negation of everything other than themselves from each other. And if this premise is invalidated, this argument falls. Rather we say: equality in positive attributes (ṣifāt thubūtiyya) does not necessitate equality in the essence; because...
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage introduces the third category of possible things (al-mumkināt): entities that are neither bodies (mutaḥayyiz) nor accidents (qāʾim bil-mutaḥayyiz). Imam Razi notes that most philosophers (falāsifa) affirm the existence of such entities (e.g., immaterial intellects, souls), while most theologians (mutakallimūn) deny them. He then presents the strongest argument from the theologians: if such a being existed besides God, it would share with God the negative attribute of being neither a body nor an accident. From that it would follow that they share in the complete essence (al-māhiyya), which would then entail confusion between the Necessary (al-wājib) and the possible (al-mumkin) — i.e., either the Necessary becomes possible or the possible becomes necessary, both absurd. Razi then critiques this argument, calling it weak. He explains that equality in a negative concept (mafhūm salbī) — i.e., both lack certain attributes — does not imply equality in essence. For any two different essences, they must share the negation of everything that is not them (i.e., they both are not what the other is, but that is trivial). Therefore, the premise that equality in a negative attribute implies equality in essence is false, and the argument collapses. He then begins to add a further point: even equality in positive attributes (ṣifāt thubūtiyya) does not require equality in essence — the text cuts off here, but Razi likely argues that two different essences can share some positive attributes without being identical in essence.

Key Terms
متحيز (mutaḥayyiz)
قائم بالمتحيز (qāʾim bil-mutaḥayyiz)
ماهية (māhiyya)
واجب (wājib)
ممكن (mumkin)
سلبي (salbī)
ثبوتي (thubūtī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two beings share a negative attribute (e.g., not being a body), they must share the same essence.
Correction: Imam Razi explains that negative concepts are too broad; many distinct essences can share the same negation without being identical.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi consider the theologians' argument against immaterial beings weak?
A) Because it assumes philosophers cannot prove their existence
B) Because equality in negative concepts does not entail equality in essence
C) Because God's essence is completely unknowable
D) Because the argument leads to circularity

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi shows that equality in negative attributes does not entail equality in essence, thus defending the possibility of immaterial beings distinct from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'not a body', do you secretly think that this makes you somewhat like Him because you also are not a body (in your soul)? How does Razi's point about negative attributes not determining essence challenge that assumption, and what concrete change in your awe of God will you make today?
?
Think of an attribute you share with God—like mercy, knowledge, or existence. Have you ever used that shared attribute to feel entitled to His favor or to compare yourself to Him? Razi argues that even positive attributes don't make essences equal. What specific thought or action will you change to reassert God's absolute uniqueness in your heart?
?
This lesson shows that the theologians' fear of immaterial beings leading to a compromised tawḥīd was based on a flawed premise. Have you ever held a belief about God out of fear of shirk, only to realize it was too restrictive? Are you willing to let go of that fear and trust in the deeper logical safeguards Razi provides, even if it makes you intellectually uncomfortable?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

Three Proofs That Things Differ by Their Essences
Razi presents three arguments for intrinsic difference of essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's three arguments demonstrating that some things differ intrinsically by their essences, not by added attributes.

Core Idea
Difference must ultimately be intrinsic; otherwise we face infinite regress or circularity.

Razi begins by establishing a foundational principle: whenever two things differ, that difference cannot go on forever through added attributes. He offers three distinct ways (wujūh) to prove that we must arrive at things that are different by themselves. Each argument targets the same conclusion from a different angle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Razi asks: if two things differ, what makes them different? If it is an added attribute, that attribute itself must be different from the other. This leads to a chain.
This is the starting point of the first wajh.
2
First way: avoiding infinite regress
Follow the first argument that either the added attribute is different or not, and see why it forces an intrinsic difference.
The argument ends with the necessity of ending at two things that differ by themselves.
3
Second way: thought experiment of equal essences
Imagine two equal essences that acquire different qualities. The essences stay equal; the qualities differ by themselves.
This shows that the qualities do not derive their difference from the essences.
4
Third way: essence vs. quality
An essence and its quality must differ; that difference is due to the essence itself, not to something else.
This argument uses the relation between a substance and its attribute.
5
Conclusion
Thus we must accept that some things are essentially different, not by added attributes.
This conclusion is the foundation for the next lesson on God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زائد لكان ذلك الزائد إما أن يكون مخالفًا للآخر أو لا يكون، فإن لم يكن مخالفا للآخر امتنع كونه سببًا لأن يصير غيره مخالفًا للآخر، وإن كان مخالفا للآخر وجب أن تكون مخالفته لأجل شيء آخر ، ولزم إما التسلسل وإما الدور وهما محالان، فثبت أنه لا بد من الانتهاء إلى شيئين يختلفان لنفسهما لا لأمر زائد.

الوجه الثاني : وهو أن الذاتين إذا كانتا متساويتين من كل الوجوه ثم قامت بكل واحدة منهما صفة مخالفة للصفة القائمة بالذات الأخرى، فعند هذا الفرق بقيت الذاتان كما كانتا متساويتين والصفات كما كانتا مختلفتين وما كانتا متساويتين لا تنقلبان البتة مختلفتين وما كانتا مختلفتين لا تنقلبان البتة متساويتين، وعند هذا يظهر أن المختلفتين لا يعقلان إلا أن يكونا مختلفتين لذاتيهما، وأما فرض أمرين يختلفان أمر زائد على ذاتيهما فذلك غير معقول.

الوجه الثالث : أنا إذا فرضنا ذاتا وقامت بها صفة فالذات من حيث إنها هي يجب أن تكون مخالفة لتلك الصفة وإلا لم تكن إحداهما بأن تكون موصوفة والأخرى بأن تكون صفة أولى من العكس، وإذا كانت تلك الذات مخالفة لتلك الصفة كانت مخالفة تلك الذات لتلك الصفة لنفس الذات لا لأمر زائد. فثبت بهذه الوجوه أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بأمور يخالف بعضها بعضا لأنفسها وذواتها، حيث هي هي لا باعتبار صفة قائمة بها.

وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : قولنا : " الله تعالى مخالف لخلقه لذاته المخصوصة " المراد منه ما ذكرناه ولخصناه.

وإذا لخصنا محل النزاع فنقول : الدليل على أنه تعالى مخالف لخلقه لذاته المخصوصة أنه لو كانت ذاته من حيث إنها تلك الذات مساوية لسائر الذوات - وقد عرفت أن الأشياء المتساوية في تمام الحقيقة يجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها ما صح على الآخر، وإذا كان كذلك كان اختصاص ذاته بصفته المخصوصة، وعدم اختصاصه بصفات المحدثات أمرًا جائزا ، فترجح ذلك الجائز على سائر الجائزات، إن لم يكن لأمر فقد ترجح الممكن لا عن مؤثر، وهو محال، وإن كان لأمر عاد الطلب في اختصاص ذاته بذلك الأمر، فيلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل، وهما محالان، فثبت أن القول بكون ذاته سبحانه وتعالى مساوية لسائر الذوات في كونه ذاتا يفضي إلى أحد هذه المحالات فكان القول به محالا وبالله التوفيق.
... would be something additional. That additional would either be different from the other or not. If it is not different from the other, it is impossible for it to be a cause for something else becoming different from the other. If it is different from the other, then its difference must be due to something else, leading either to infinite regress or circularity, both of which are impossible. So it is established that one must end at two things that differ by themselves, not by an additional thing.

The second way: That when two essences are equal in every respect, and then each is characterized by a quality different from the quality characterizing the other, then at this difference the two essences remain as they were, equal, and the qualities as they were, different. What were equal never become different, and what were different never become equal. Hence it is evident that different things cannot be conceived unless they are different in their very essences. To suppose two things that differ by something additional to their essences is unintelligible.

The third way: If we suppose an essence characterized by a quality, the essence, qua itself, must be different from that quality; otherwise, it would not be more fitting for one to be the qualified and the other the quality rather than the reverse. If that essence is different from that quality, then the difference of that essence from that quality is due to the essence itself, not to something additional. So by these ways it is established that one must acknowledge things that differ from one another by themselves and their essences, as they are in themselves, not in virtue of a quality inherent in them.

Once you understand this, we say: Our statement 'God Most High is different from His creation by His particular essence' means what we have mentioned and summarized.

Now that we have summarized the point of dispute, we say: The proof that He Most High is different from His creation by His particular essence is that if His essence, qua that essence, were equal to other essences—and you know that things equal in every respect must have the same properties—then its being characterized by its particular quality and not by the qualities of contingent things would be a contingent matter. If that contingent matter were preferred over other possibilities without a cause, then a possible would be preferred without a determiner, which is impossible. If it were due to a cause, then the search would revert to its essence's being characterized by that cause, leading either to circularity or infinite regress, both impossible. Thus, the view that His essence (glorified and exalted) is equal to other essences in being an essence leads to these impossibilities, so that view is impossible. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a composite argument: it first gives three ways (wajh) to prove that difference must ultimately be intrinsic to things, not due to added attributes, and then applies this to establish God's essential difference from creation. The first way (incomplete at the start) argues that if two things differ by something additional, that additional either is or is not different from the other; if not, it cannot cause difference; if it is, its own difference would require another cause, leading to infinite regress or circularity. Therefore, difference must be intrinsic. The second way presents a thought experiment: two equal essences each acquire different qualities; the essences remain equal while the qualities differ; thus the qualities differ by themselves, not by essence. The third way argues between essence and quality: since an essence and its quality are different, that difference is due to the essence itself, not to an added attribute. After concluding that intrinsic differences exist, Razi applies this to God: if God's essence were equal to other essences, then His particular attributes would be contingent, requiring a cause for that contingency, leading to regress or circularity; thus God's essence is unique. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum of the opponent's position.

Key Terms
ذات (essence)
مخالفة (difference)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is proving that all things differ by essence, but he is specifically arguing that some differences (like that between God and creation) are essential, not accidental.
Correction: Razi's conclusion is that we must acknowledge things that differ by themselves, but he does not claim everything differs essentially; rather, he establishes the possibility of essential difference, which he will then apply to God.

Checkpoint
In the second way (الوجه الثاني), what happens when two equal essences are characterized by different qualities?
A) The essences become different from each other
B) The qualities become equal
C) The essences remain equal and the qualities remain different
D) Both essences and qualities become different

Key Takeaway
Razi establishes that difference must ultimately be intrinsic to avoid infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is essentially different from everything else, what does that mean for your desire to 'be like God' in power or control? Are you trying to take on something that isn't yours?
?
Razi shows that difference must be intrinsic or else we face endless justifications. When you look at your own identity, are you relying on external things (job, status, relationships) to define who you are, or do you know your intrinsic nature as a created being before your Creator?
?
The third proof says an essence must be different from its own quality by itself. How does that challenge the way you distinguish between your 'self' and your shortcomings? Do you accept that your flaws do not change who you are in God's sight?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

Proving God's Essential Difference from Creation
Razi applies the intrinsic difference principle to God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi applies the principle of intrinsic difference to prove that God's essence is uniquely different from all creation.

Core Idea
If God's essence were like other essences, His attributes would be contingent, leading to absurdity.

Having established that some differences are intrinsic, Razi now applies this to the most important case: the difference between God and His creation. He argues that if God's essence were equal to other essences in its quiddity, then His special attributes would be accidental, requiring a cause, and that cause would need another cause, leading to impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Recall the principle
We have learned that some things differ by themselves. Now we apply this to God.
This lesson builds directly on the previous one.
2
State the proposition
Razi says God is different from creation by His particular essence. This means His essence is unique.
This is the thesis being proven.
3
Follow the reductio
Assume God's essence is equal to other essences. Then His attributes would be contingent on a cause. If no cause, it's arbitrary; if a cause, it leads to regress.
The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Conclusion
Thus, God's essence must be essentially different from all creation.
This is the conclusion Razi draws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زائد لكان ذلك الزائد إما أن يكون مخالفًا للآخر أو لا يكون، فإن لم يكن مخالفا للآخر امتنع كونه سببًا لأن يصير غيره مخالفًا للآخر، وإن كان مخالفا للآخر وجب أن تكون مخالفته لأجل شيء آخر ، ولزم إما التسلسل وإما الدور وهما محالان، فثبت أنه لا بد من الانتهاء إلى شيئين يختلفان لنفسهما لا لأمر زائد.

الوجه الثاني : وهو أن الذاتين إذا كانتا متساويتين من كل الوجوه ثم قامت بكل واحدة منهما صفة مخالفة للصفة القائمة بالذات الأخرى، فعند هذا الفرق بقيت الذاتان كما كانتا متساويتين والصفات كما كانتا مختلفتين وما كانتا متساويتين لا تنقلبان البتة مختلفتين وما كانتا مختلفتين لا تنقلبان البتة متساويتين، وعند هذا يظهر أن المختلفتين لا يعقلان إلا أن يكونا مختلفتين لذاتيهما، وأما فرض أمرين يختلفان أمر زائد على ذاتيهما فذلك غير معقول.

الوجه الثالث : أنا إذا فرضنا ذاتا وقامت بها صفة فالذات من حيث إنها هي يجب أن تكون مخالفة لتلك الصفة وإلا لم تكن إحداهما بأن تكون موصوفة والأخرى بأن تكون صفة أولى من العكس، وإذا كانت تلك الذات مخالفة لتلك الصفة كانت مخالفة تلك الذات لتلك الصفة لنفس الذات لا لأمر زائد. فثبت بهذه الوجوه أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بأمور يخالف بعضها بعضا لأنفسها وذواتها، حيث هي هي لا باعتبار صفة قائمة بها.

وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : قولنا : " الله تعالى مخالف لخلقه لذاته المخصوصة " المراد منه ما ذكرناه ولخصناه.

وإذا لخصنا محل النزاع فنقول : الدليل على أنه تعالى مخالف لخلقه لذاته المخصوصة أنه لو كانت ذاته من حيث إنها تلك الذات مساوية لسائر الذوات - وقد عرفت أن الأشياء المتساوية في تمام الحقيقة يجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها ما صح على الآخر، وإذا كان كذلك كان اختصاص ذاته بصفته المخصوصة، وعدم اختصاصه بصفات المحدثات أمرًا جائزا ، فترجح ذلك الجائز على سائر الجائزات، إن لم يكن لأمر فقد ترجح الممكن لا عن مؤثر، وهو محال، وإن كان لأمر عاد الطلب في اختصاص ذاته بذلك الأمر، فيلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل، وهما محالان، فثبت أن القول بكون ذاته سبحانه وتعالى مساوية لسائر الذوات في كونه ذاتا يفضي إلى أحد هذه المحالات فكان القول به محالا وبالله التوفيق.
... would be something additional. That additional would either be different from the other or not. If it is not different from the other, it is impossible for it to be a cause for something else becoming different from the other. If it is different from the other, then its difference must be due to something else, leading either to infinite regress or circularity, both of which are impossible. So it is established that one must end at two things that differ by themselves, not by an additional thing.

The second way: That when two essences are equal in every respect, and then each is characterized by a quality different from the quality characterizing the other, then at this difference the two essences remain as they were, equal, and the qualities as they were, different. What were equal never become different, and what were different never become equal. Hence it is evident that different things cannot be conceived unless they are different in their very essences. To suppose two things that differ by something additional to their essences is unintelligible.

The third way: If we suppose an essence characterized by a quality, the essence, qua itself, must be different from that quality; otherwise, it would not be more fitting for one to be the qualified and the other the quality rather than the reverse. If that essence is different from that quality, then the difference of that essence from that quality is due to the essence itself, not to something additional. So by these ways it is established that one must acknowledge things that differ from one another by themselves and their essences, as they are in themselves, not in virtue of a quality inherent in them.

Once you understand this, we say: Our statement 'God Most High is different from His creation by His particular essence' means what we have mentioned and summarized.

Now that we have summarized the point of dispute, we say: The proof that He Most High is different from His creation by His particular essence is that if His essence, qua that essence, were equal to other essences—and you know that things equal in every respect must have the same properties—then its being characterized by its particular quality and not by the qualities of contingent things would be a contingent matter. If that contingent matter were preferred over other possibilities without a cause, then a possible would be preferred without a determiner, which is impossible. If it were due to a cause, then the search would revert to its essence's being characterized by that cause, leading either to circularity or infinite regress, both impossible. Thus, the view that His essence (glorified and exalted) is equal to other essences in being an essence leads to these impossibilities, so that view is impossible. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a composite argument: it first gives three ways (wajh) to prove that difference must ultimately be intrinsic to things, not due to added attributes, and then applies this to establish God's essential difference from creation. The first way (incomplete at the start) argues that if two things differ by something additional, that additional either is or is not different from the other; if not, it cannot cause difference; if it is, its own difference would require another cause, leading to infinite regress or circularity. Therefore, difference must be intrinsic. The second way presents a thought experiment: two equal essences each acquire different qualities; the essences remain equal while the qualities differ; thus the qualities differ by themselves, not by essence. The third way argues between essence and quality: since an essence and its quality are different, that difference is due to the essence itself, not to an added attribute. After concluding that intrinsic differences exist, Razi applies this to God: if God's essence were equal to other essences, then His particular attributes would be contingent, requiring a cause for that contingency, leading to regress or circularity; thus God's essence is unique. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum of the opponent's position.

Key Terms
ذات (essence)
صفة (attribute)
مخالفة (difference)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
دور (circularity)

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what would follow if God's essence were equal to other essences?
A) God's attributes would be necessary
B) God's attributes would be contingent
C) God would have no attributes
D) God's essence would be composite

Key Takeaway
God's essence is necessarily unique and different from all creation by itself, not by an added attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking of God as essentially similar to creation, just greater in power or knowledge? How does this proof challenge that assumption, and what would it mean for your worship if you truly internalized His uniqueness?
?
When you pray, do you feel a sense of awe that the One you are addressing is not just 'another being' but an essence utterly unlike anything else? How can you cultivate this awareness in your daily life?
?
If you had to explain to a friend why God cannot be compared to any created thing, would you rely on this logical argument or just say 'He is different'? How would mastering this proof change how you defend your faith?

Three Proofs for the Equality of All Essences
The opponent presents three proofs that all essences are equal in being essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's thesis that all essences are equal in being essences and the three proofs he offers to support it.

Core Idea
All essences share a single concept; distinction must come from attributes.

The opponent in the debate begins by asserting that all essences are equal in their essence-hood. To defend this claim, he provides three distinct proofs, each demonstrating that the concept of essence is uniform across all things. This lays the groundwork for his argument that distinction arises from attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's thesis
Start by reading the opening statement: all essences are equal in being essences, so distinction must be by attributes.
This is the claim we will see proven in three ways.
2
Examine the first proof
The first proof uses the division of essences into necessary and possible. Notice that the division itself implies a common concept shared by both divisions.
This is a logical argument from classification.
3
Examine the second proof
The second proof considers that the concept of essence remains constant whether we think of an eternal or originated being. This indicates a single meaning.
Focus on the constancy of the concept.
4
Examine the third proof
The third proof rests on the exhaustive division of the known into essence and attribute. If the concept of essence were not one, the division wouldn't be exhaustive.
This proof appeals to sound reason's testimony.
5
Synthesize the three proofs
Review how each proof supports the same conclusion: all essences are equal qua essences.
The three ways are cumulative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الخصم : بأن جميع الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يكون امتياز بعضها عن البعض بسبب الصفات والأحوال. وأما بيان المقدمة الأولى فيدل عليه وجوه :

الأول : أنه يصح تقسيم الذوات إلى الواجب وإلى الممكن ومورد التقسيم مشترك بين القسمين.

والثاني: أنا إذا اعتقدنا ذاتا فسواء اعتقدنا قديمًا أو محدثًا أو واجبًا أو ممكنا ، فإن اعتقاد كونه ذاتا لا يزول ولا يتبدل وهذا يدل على أن المفهوم من الذوات واحد في كل المواضع.

والثالث : أنا نقول : المعلوم إما ذات وإما صفة وصريح العقل يشهد بأن هذا التقسيم منحصر ، ولولا أن المفهوم من كون الذوات ذاتا أمر واحد، وإلا لم يكن هذا التقسيم منحصراً، فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات.

وأما المقدمة الثانية - وهي أنه لما كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يكون امتياز بعض الذوات عن بعض بسبب الصفات - فذلك لأنه لا شك أن الله تعالى متميز عن سائر الموجودات وثبت أن ذاته مساوية لسائر الذوات وما به الامتیاز غیر ما به المشاركة، فوجب أن يكون امتياز ذاته عن سائر الذوات بأمور زائدة على الذات، يكون بالصفة.

لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون امتياز ذوات المحدثات ذاته اختصاص ذوات المحدثات بصفات ثبوتية وامتياز ذاته سبحانه عن ذوات المحدثات بسبب سلب تلك الصفات ذاته؟

لأنا نقول : الذات من حيث إنها ذات لو كانت مستقلة بنفسها في التحقق غنية الصفات لزم جواز أن ينقلب ذات السواد بياضًا، وذات البياض سوادًا وذات الواجب عرضًا حالا في المحل، وبالعكس، وكل ذلك محال، وإن لم تكن الذات من حيث إنها ذات مستقلة إلا الصفات وجب أن يكون امتياز ذات الحق تعالى مع عن غيره بسبب ثبوت الصفات لا بسبب سلب الصفات.
The opponent argued: All essences are equal in being essences. If so, then distinction of some from others must be due to attributes and states. As for the demonstration of the first premise, it is indicated by ways:

First: It is valid to divide essences into the necessary and the possible, and the locus of division is common to both divisions.

Second: If we conceive of an essence, whether we conceive it as eternal or originated, necessary or possible, the conception of its being an essence does not disappear or change. This indicates that the concept of essences is one in all places.

Third: We say: The known is either an essence or an attribute, and sound reason testifies that this division is exhaustive. If the concept of being an essence were not one, this division would not be exhaustive. So by these ways it is established that essences are equal in being essences.

As for the second premise—that since it is so, distinction of some essences from others must be due to attributes—that is because there is no doubt that God Most High is distinct from all other existents, and it is established that His essence is equal to all other essences, and what distinguishes is other than what is shared. So His essence's distinction from other essences must be due to things additional to the essence, namely attributes.

It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible that the distinction of contingent essences is by their being characterized by positive attributes, and the distinction of His essence (glorified) from contingent essences is due to the negation of those attributes from His essence?

To which we say: If the essence, qua essence, were independent in existence and free of attributes, then it would be possible for the essence of blackness to become whiteness, the essence of whiteness to become blackness, and the essence of the Necessary to become an accident inhering in a substrate, and vice versa—all of which are impossible. If the essence, qua essence, is not independent except through attributes, then the distinction of His essence (the Truth, exalted) from others must be due to the affirmation of attributes, not due to negation of attributes.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's full argument. The opponent first states his thesis: all essences are equal in being essences, so distinction must come from attributes. He then offers three proofs for the first premise: (1) the division of essences into necessary and possible shows a common concept; (2) the concept of essence remains constant whether we think of eternal or originated, indicating a single meaning; (3) the exhaustive division of the known into essence and attribute requires a single concept of essence. Then the opponent presents the second premise: since God is distinct and His essence is equal to others, distinction must be by attributes (the distinguishing factor is other than the shared factor). The opponent then addresses a potential objection: why not say that contingent essences are distinguished by positive attributes and God by negation? The opponent's answer is that if essences were independent of attributes, all sorts of absurd transformations would be possible; if they are not independent, then distinction must be by positive attributes, not by negation. This is a self-contained argument supporting the opponent's view that God's distinction is by attributes.

Key Terms
ذات (essence)
صفة (attribute)
امتياز (distinction)
تقسيم (division)
مفهوم (concept)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first proof (الوجه الأول) for the equality of essences?
A) The constancy of the concept of essence across different objects
B) The exhaustive division of the known into essence and attribute
C) The division of essences into necessary and possible
D) The impossibility of essences changing

Key Takeaway
The opponent establishes that all essences are equal in being essences through three independent logical proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all essences are equal in being essences, how do you treat others based on their attributes (wealth, status) rather than their shared humanity? Will you change your perception after this lesson?
?
The passage says the concept of essence remains unchanged whether thought of as eternal or originated. Consider your own self: do you sometimes feel your identity shifts with your actions? How does this truth affect your accountability before God, knowing your essence is fixed in His knowledge?

From Equality to Distinction: Why Attributes Are Necessary
The opponent argues that distinction of essences must be by attributes and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponent argues that distinction between essences must be due to attributes, and how he responds to the objection that God's distinction could be by negation.

Core Idea
What distinguishes cannot be what is shared; distinction must come from added attributes.

Having established that all essences are equal in essence-hood, the opponent now argues that the distinction of God's essence from others must be due to attributes. He then addresses a key objection: perhaps contingent essences are distinguished by positive attributes while God is distinguished by their negation. His response shows that essences cannot be independent of attributes; otherwise absurd transformations would follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the second premise
The opponent states: since essences are equal, what distinguishes them must be something else, i.e., attributes. Read carefully the logic: God is distinct, His essence is equal to others, so distinction is by something additional.
Note the key principle: 'ما به الامتياز غير ما به المشاركة'.
2
Understand the objection
The objection proposes an alternative: perhaps contingent essences are distinguished by positive attributes, while God's distinction is by negation of those attributes.
This objection tries to avoid adding attributes to God.
3
Follow the response
The opponent's response has two parts: if an essence were independent of attributes, absurd changes would be possible; if not independent, then distinction must be by positive attributes, not negation.
The response uses a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The opponent concludes that God's distinction must be by the affirmation of attributes, not by negation.
This supports the Ash'ari view that God has real attributes.

?

Razi's Refutation of Equality of Essences
Razi refutes the opponent's first premise by applying the same logic to attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the opponent's claim of equality of essences by applying the same logic to attributes and reinterpreting 'essence' as a negative concept.

Core Idea
Razi turns the opponent's argument against them by showing it leads to infinite regress with attributes.

In this passage, Razi delivers a sharp refutation of the opponent's first premise. He does not accept that essences are equal in a positive sense, and he demonstrates that the opponent's three proofs would apply equally to attributes, forcing an infinite regress. Additionally, he redefines 'essence' as a negative concept, undermining the opponent's foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi begins with 'We do not accept' (لا نسلم), signaling a rejection.
2
Identify the three proofs and their application to attributes
Razi mentions that the opponent's three proofs apply equally to attributes. Consider what those proofs might be (e.g., division, constancy of concept, exhaustive division) and how applying them to attributes leads to the conclusion that attributes are equal in being attributes.
3
Trace the consequence: infinite regress
If attributes are equal in being attributes, then their distinction would require further attributes, which would also be equal, leading to an infinite regress. Understand why this is a problem for the opponent.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the opponent's logic leads to an unacceptable conclusion.
4
Grasp the redefinition of 'essence' as a negative concept
Razi argues that the concept of 'being an essence' is merely self-subsistence, which is independence from a substrate—a negative concept. This means there is no positive shared reality that would necessitate equality.
Negative concepts do not imply a positive common essence.
5
Synthesize the refutation
Combine the two strands: the application to attributes leading to infinite regress, and the redefinition of essence as negative. How do these two together refute the opponent's claim?
Razi uses both logical consequence and conceptual analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب: لا نسلم أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات، والوجوه الثلاثة التي عولتم عليها قائمة بعينها في الصفات فيلزمكم أن تكون الصفات متساوية في كونها صفات، فيلزمكم أن يكون امتياز بعض الصفات عن بعض بصفات أخرى، ويلزم التسلسل، وأيضًا: فالمفهوم من كونها ذوات كونها أمورًا قائمة بأنفسها، والقيام بالنفس عبارة عن الاستغناء عن المحل، وهو مفهوم سلبي، ولا نزاع في كون هذا
The answer: We do not accept that essences are equal in being essences. The three proofs you relied upon apply equally to attributes, so you would be required to hold that attributes are equal in being attributes, and then you would be required to hold that distinction of some attributes from others is by other attributes, leading to infinite regress. Moreover, the concept of being essences is that they are things subsisting by themselves, and self-subsistence means independence from a substrate, which is a negative concept, and there is no dispute that this...
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is Razi's direct refutation of the opponent's first premise. Razi does not accept that essences are equal in being essences. He points out that the opponent's three proofs (based on division, constancy of concept, and exhaustive division) apply equally to attributes. If the same reasoning were applied to attributes, one would have to say that attributes are equal in being attributes, and then distinction between attributes would require further attributes, leading to an infinite regress. Furthermore, Razi adds that the concept of 'being an essence' is negative: it means self-subsistence, i.e., independence from a substrate. Since this is a negative concept, it does not imply a positive shared reality that would necessitate equality. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: Razi undermines the opponent's foundation by showing its consequences and reinterpreting the meaning of 'essence' as a negative notion.

Key Terms
ذات (essence)
صفة (attribute)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
قيام بالنفس (self-subsistence)
مفهوم سلبي (negative concept)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is denying any commonality among essences, e.g., that they share nothing at all.
Correction: Razi does not deny that essences share the concept of self-subsistence; he denies that this shared concept is a positive, real equality that necessitates further properties.

Checkpoint
What consequence does Razi draw from applying the opponent's three proofs to attributes?
A) Attributes are equal in being attributes
B) Attributes do not exist
C) Distinction of attributes from each other requires other attributes, leading to infinite regress
D) Attributes are self-subsistent

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the equality of essences by showing the opponent's logic leads to infinite regress when applied to attributes, and by defining 'essence' as a negative concept that implies no positive shared reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you tend to define yourself or others by what you are not (negative concepts) rather than by positive attributes? How does Razi's point about 'essence' as a negative concept challenge you to reconsider your self-understanding? Will you commit to actively defining your identity by positive, God-given attributes?
?
Have you ever accepted an argument that seemed logical without checking whether it leads to absurdity when applied consistently? Think of a specific belief you hold. Can you trace its consequences to see if it leads to an infinite regress or contradiction? What will you do if it does?
?
When you interact with people, do you sometimes treat them as if they are essentially the same, ignoring their unique qualities? How does Razi's rejection of equality of essences remind you to honor the distinct attributes of each person? How will you change your attitude starting today?

Divisibility Implies Contingency: Razi's First Proof
Razi proves God is not a body via divisibility-contingency syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's syllogistic proof that God cannot be a body because every body is divisible, and divisibility entails contingency.

Core Idea
If something is divisible, it depends on its parts—and dependence is the mark of contingency.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi begins his case for God's transcendence by tackling the notion that God could be a body. His first argument is a crisp syllogism: every body is divisible, and everything divisible is contingent; therefore, a necessary being cannot be a body. In this lesson, we walk through the logical steps of that proof and see why it rests on the relationship between parts and whole.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the gist of Razi's argument. Notice how he states his conclusion early, then unpacks the premises.
The proof is structured as a conditional syllogism.
2
Identify the two key premises
Razi relies on two premises: (1) every body is divisible, and (2) everything divisible is contingent. Trace how he justifies each—the first is based on rejecting atomism, the second on the part-whole relation.
The first premise is not argued here; it will be defended later.
3
Understand why composition implies contingency
The second premise is unpacked: a divisible thing is composite, a composite depends on its parts, and each part is other than the whole. This dependence on something else is exactly what makes it contingent.
Contingent (mumkin al-wujud li-dhatihi) means existence is not from itself.
4
Connect to God's necessity
God is necessary (wajib al-wujud), so He cannot be contingent. Therefore, He cannot be divisible and hence cannot be a body. That's the conclusion Razi wants.
This proof is just one of several for divine incorporeality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السابعة في أن الله تعالى ليس بمتحيز الحجة الأولى ويدل عليه وجوه كل متحيز منقسم وكل منقسم ممكن الوجود لذاته، فكل متحيز فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته، وهذا يستلزم أن كل ما لا يكون ممكن الوجود لذاته وجب أن لا يكون متحيزا ، أما أن كل متحيز فهو منقسم لذاته فهذه بناء على نفي الجوهر الفرد، وسيأتي الكلام عليه في هذه المسألة، وأما أن كل منقسم لذاته فهو ممكن لذاته، وذلك لأن كل منقسم فهو مركب وكل مركب فهو مفتقر في تحققه إلى تحقق كل واحد من أجزائه، وكل واحد من أجزاء الشيء غير ذلك الشيء، فكل منقسم فهو مفتقر في تحققه إلى غيره وكل مفتقر في تحققه إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته، فثبت أن كل منقسم فهو ممكن لذاته.
The seventh issue: That Allah Most High is not a body. The first proof: And it is indicated by [several] considerations: Every body is divisible, and every divisible is possible in itself. So every body is possible in itself. This entails that whatever is not possible in itself must not be a body. As for [the claim] that every body is divisible in itself, this is based on the denial of the indivisible atom, and the discussion of it will come in this issue. As for [the claim] that every divisible in itself is possible in itself, that is because every divisible is composite, and every composite is dependent for its realization upon the realization of each one of its parts, and each one of the parts of a thing is other than that thing. So every divisible is dependent for its realization upon another, and everything dependent for its realization upon another is possible in itself. Thus it is established that every divisible is possible in itself.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is the first proof that God is not a body (mutahayyiz). Razi argues by syllogism: every body is divisible, and everything divisible is contingent (possible in itself), therefore every body is contingent. Since God is necessary (not contingent), He cannot be a body. He then explains the two premises: first, that every body is divisible — this assumes the denial of atomism (al-jawhar al-fard), which will be discussed later. Second, that everything divisible is composite, and a composite depends on its parts for its existence. Since each part is distinct from the whole, the whole depends on something other than itself. Dependence on another is a mark of contingency. Therefore, anything divisible is contingent. Hence God, who is necessary, cannot be divisible, and so cannot be a body.

Key Terms
متحيز
منقسم
ممكن الوجود لذاته
مركب
جوهر فرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes atomism is false, but Razi states it is based on denying atomism, which is a separate claim.
Correction: Razi openly acknowledges that the first premise (every body is divisible) stands on the rejection of indivisible atoms; he does not assume atomism. He promises to argue against atomism later.

Checkpoint
What is the intermediate step that connects divisibility to contingency?
A) Divisibility implies being a body.
B) Divisibility implies composition, and composition implies dependence on parts.
C) Divisibility implies being in space.
D) Divisibility implies having dimensions.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that because every body is divisible and therefore contingent, the necessary God cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that everything composite depends on its parts and is therefore contingent. You too are composite—body and soul, strengths and weaknesses. Which part of your life do you most treat as if it were self-sufficient, as if it did not depend on Allah at every moment? What would it look like to consciously depend on Him for that part today?
?
Razi says: 'Everything that depends on another is possible in itself.' You depend on oxygen, food, sleep, loved ones, your own organs. When was the last time you felt the fragility of that dependence? Did it drive you to the One who depends on nothing, or did you try to secure yourself against fragility on your own? Be honest: where are you pretending to be necessary when you are only possible?
?
The conclusion is that God cannot be a body because He is not possible. But you are possible—every breath is a gift. If you truly internalized that you are contingent and that God alone is necessary, what one concrete habit would change in your next 24 hours? Name it and commit to it before you close this lesson.

Second Proof: God Is Not a Body
Razi proves God is not a body using temporal origin of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's second proof establishes that God cannot be a body because all bodies are originated and God is eternal.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would be created—impossible for the Eternal.

Imam Razi now presents a second argument for God's transcendence over physicality. The proof is concise but rests on an earlier conclusion that all bodies are originated. By linking God's nature to that established principle, Razi shows that attributing a body to God leads to a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the flow: premise, conditional, contradiction, conclusion.
Notice how Razi uses the phrase 'if...then...' to build the argument.
2
Identify the key premise
The proof depends on the claim that 'every body is originated'. Ask yourself: Is this a self-evident truth or something that needs proof? Razi refers back to an earlier discussion.
The premise is not proven here; it is assumed from a prior argument.
3
Trace the logical structure
The argument is a conditional syllogism: If God were a body, He would be originated. But God cannot be originated (because He is eternal). Therefore, God cannot be a body.
This is a classic modus tollens: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is not just that God is not a body, but that such a notion is impossible. Consider what this implies about God's nature.
This proof reinforces tawhid and divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أنا ذكرنا في مسألة حدوث الأجسام أن كل متحيز فهو محدث فلو كان الباري تعالى متحيزا لكان محدثًا ، لكنه يمتنع أن يكون محدثًا فيمتنع أن يكون متحيزا.
The second proof: We mentioned in the issue of the origination of bodies that every body is originated. So if the Exalted Creator were a body, He would be originated. But it is impossible for Him to be originated, so it is impossible for Him to be a body.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This is a concise second proof that God is not a body. Razi references a prior discussion (the issue of the origination of bodies) where it was established that every body is originated (hadith). The argument is straightforward: if God were a body, He would share the property of being originated. But God cannot be originated because He is eternal. Therefore, He cannot be a body. This proof relies on the earlier proof that all bodies are originated, which is a standard kalam argument.

Key Terms

محدث
باري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof tries to prove that all bodies are originated within this very passage.
Correction: Razi explicitly references an earlier discussion where the origination of bodies was proven. This proof assumes that conclusion and builds on it.

Checkpoint

Razi's second proof shows that God cannot be a body because that would make Him originated, contradicting His eternality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof says God cannot be originated. When you sin, do you act as if you can hide from an originated being, or do you truly believe you stand before the One who never began? What one habit will you change to live in that awareness?

Third Proof: Bodies Are Finite in Magnitude
Razi proves God is not a body because all bodies have finite magnitude and are thus originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof that God is not a body, which argues that all bodies are finite in magnitude and therefore originated, so God cannot be a body.

Core Idea
Finitude of magnitude implies origination, so God cannot be a body.

Razi presents a concise syllogism to demonstrate that God is not a body. He draws on a previously established principle: everything finite in magnitude is originated. Since bodies are finite, they are originated, and the Creator is not originated—hence He is not a body. This proof avoids reliance on composition and instead uses finitude as a sign of contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: premises followed by conclusion.
The passage is a single sentence, so pay attention to the flow.
2
Identify the syllogism
Break the argument into its three parts: major premise, minor premise, and conclusion. Map each to a line in the translation.
The major premise is 'everything finite in magnitude is originated'; the minor is 'every body is finite'; the conclusion is 'every body is originated'.
3
Reflect on the key premise
Ask: why does finitude imply origination? Razi assumes this from a prior argument about the origination of bodies. Think about how this differs from proofs based on composition.
This premise is not self-evident; it depends on an earlier discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة وذكرنا في مسألة حدوث الأجسام أن كل متحيز فهو متناهي المقدار، وكل متناهي المقدار فهو محدث ينتج أن كل متحيز فهو محدث، والباري تعالى ليس بمحدث، فلا يكون متحيزا.
The third proof: And we mentioned in the issue of the origination of bodies that every body is finite in magnitude, and everything finite in magnitude is originated. This yields that every body is originated. The Exalted Creator is not originated, so He is not a body.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
The third proof builds on a different property of bodies: every body has finite magnitude (mutanahi al-miqdar). Razi references a prior discussion that established that everything with finite magnitude is originated (because finitude implies temporal origin). The syllogism: all bodies are finite in magnitude; all finite things are originated; therefore all bodies are originated. Since God is not originated, He is not a body. This proof avoids relying on composition or divisibility and instead uses finitude as a sign of origination.

محدث

Correction: Razi explicitly references a prior discussion (mas'alat huduth al-ajsam) where the premises were established. The proof assumes those conclusions; it is not circular but relies on earlier arguments about the origination of bodies.

Fourth Proof: God Is Not a Body
Razi proves God is not a body via shared essence argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof from Imam Razi that God cannot be a body, based on the premise that all bodies share the same complete essence and thus any differentiation requires an external cause.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would need an outside cause to be special—impossible.

Imam Razi presents a tightly reasoned proof that God cannot be a body. The argument rests on a key premise established earlier: all bodies share the same complete essence. From this, he shows that if God were a body, He would require an external factor to account for His divine attributes—a clear contradiction of His necessary existence. Let's follow the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key premise
The proof begins with a premise: all bodies (متحيزات) are equal in their complete essence (تمام الماهية). This means that, as a species, they share the same quiddity. Make sure you grasp this foundational claim.
This premise was established earlier in the discussion on proving the existence of the Maker.
2
Apply the premise to God
If the Exalted Creator were a body, His essence would be identical to the essence of all other bodies. Logically, there would be no intrinsic difference between God and any other body.
3
See the necessary consequence
If God were just another body, then His being distinguished by divine attributes (like being Creator, eternal, etc.) would require an external specifying factor (مرجح ومخصص). But such a need is impossible for the Necessary Being. Therefore, God cannot be a body.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming God is a body leads to an impossible consequence, so the assumption is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة ذكرنا في مسألة إثبات الصانع أن المتحيزات متساوية في تمام الماهية، فلو كان الباري تعالى متحيزا لكانت ماهيته مساوية لسائر الماهيات في تمام الماهية، ولو كان كذلك لافتقر في اختصاصه بصفات الإلهية إلى مرجح ومخصص، وذلك محال فالقول بكونه متحيزا محال.
The fourth proof: We mentioned in the issue of establishing the Maker that bodies are equal in the completeness of essence. So if the Exalted Creator were a body, His essence would be equal to the rest of essences in completeness of essence. And if that were the case, He would need a preponderating and specifying factor for His being distinguished by divine attributes, and that is impossible. So the statement that He is a body is impossible.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
The fourth proof is based on the premise that all bodies share the same complete quiddity (tamam al-mahiya); they are identical in essence. This was established in a previous discussion on proving the existence of the Maker. If God were a body, His essence would be identical to other bodies in complete essence. Then, to be distinguished by divine attributes (such as being the Creator, eternal, etc.), God would require a separate factor (murajjih wa mukhasis) to specify Him over other bodies. But God is necessary in Himself and cannot depend on anything external for specification. Therefore, God cannot be a body. This argument uses the principle that if multiple individuals share the same essence, any differentiation must come from an external cause.

Key Terms
متحيز
تمام الماهية
مرجح
مخصص
صفات الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The premise that all bodies share the same essence means they are exactly identical in every respect, including accidents and individual characteristics.
Correction: The premise refers to the species essence (تمام الماهية) of 'body', not accidental properties. Bodies differ in accidents (like size, color) but share the same specific quiddity as bodies.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's fourth proof, why would God need a specifying factor if He were a body?
A) Because all bodies are created.
B) Because bodies are identical in complete essence, so any distinction requires an external cause.
C) Because bodies have parts.
D) Because bodies are contingent.

Key Takeaway
Because all bodies share identical essence, God as a body would require an external cause for His divine attributes—impossible, so God is not a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself imagining God as if He were a being with limits, like a body? How does this proof challenge that mental image, and what must you do to purify your concept of God?
?
If all bodies share the same essence, then nothing physical can be ultimately special on its own. How does this truth expose the emptiness of your attachment to material status or appearance? What will you do to shift your focus to the Creator alone?
?
God needs no external cause to be who He is, yet you constantly rely on others' approval or circumstances to define your identity. How does this proof confront your self-worth, and what concrete change in your daily life will you make to reflect God's self-sufficiency?

Fifth Proof: A Body Cannot Possess All Knowledge and Power
Razi argues that a body cannot have a single knowledge and power over all its parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God cannot be a body because a single attribute cannot inhere in multiple parts.

Core Idea
A single attribute cannot be located in multiple places, so a divisible body cannot have God's unitary knowledge and power.

Imam Razi presents the fifth of his twelve proofs for God's incorporeality. He argues that if God were a body, He would be divisible, and then His single knowledge and power would have to exist in all parts simultaneously — which is impossible. This concise argument relies on a key metaphysical principle about the inherence of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the precise wording of Razi's argument sink in before analyzing.
2
Understand the logical structure
The proof is a conditional: if God were a body, He would be divisible. Then a single knowledge and power would have to subsist in all parts, which is impossible. Trace this logical chain.
The impossibility is due to a principle about attribute inherence.
3
Reflect on the key principle
The core principle is that a single attribute cannot inhere in multiple, separate loci. Ask yourself why that is and how it applies to God.
This principle is crucial for many kalam arguments about divine simplicity.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Note that this proof does not deny God's omnipresence. It specifically targets the idea that a composite body could host unitary attributes across its parts.
Omnipresence is not the same as inherence in parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : لو كان جسما لكان منقسماً ، فإما أن يقوم علم واحد وقدرة واحدة بجميع تلك الأجزاء، وهو محال لامتناع حلول الصفة الواحدة في المحال.
The fifth proof: If He were a body, He would be divisible. Then either a single knowledge and a single power would subsist in all those parts, but that is impossible because a single attribute cannot inhere in multiple locations.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This fifth proof argues that if God were a body, He would be divisible (since all bodies are divisible). If He were divisible, then His knowledge and power—which are single and unitary—would have to subsist in all parts of His body. However, a single indivisible attribute cannot inhere in multiple, separate places simultaneously; an attribute that is one cannot be wholly present in each part. Therefore, God cannot be a body. This argument relies on the principle that a unitary attribute cannot be divided across parts. The text appears to be cut off, but the core argument is clear.

Key Terms

منقسم
علم واحد
قدرة واحدة
حلول الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this proof denies God's omnipresence or His ability to be present everywhere.
Correction: This proof does not deny God's omnipresence; it denies that a single attribute can be located in multiple physical parts as would be required for a composite deity. Omnipresence is a different concept.

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge and power cannot exist in a body.
B) A single attribute cannot inhere in multiple separate locations.
C) Bodies cannot have attributes.

Key Takeaway
A single attribute cannot inhere in multiple parts, so God, who has unitary attributes, cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you face a trial, do you find yourself thinking that God is 'only' merciful or 'only' just at that moment? This proof shows that His attributes are indivisible. How can you today, in a concrete situation, rely on both His mercy and justice together instead of separating them in your mind?

First Proof: Every Body is Divisible and Thus Contingent
Imam Razi presents the first proof that Allah is not a body, based on divisibility leading to contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first proof that Allah is not a body, based on the contingency of all bodies.

Core Idea
Bodies are divisible, thus contingent; therefore Allah, being necessary, cannot be a body.

Imam Razi opens the first proof with a clear logical chain: every body is divisible, and every divisible is contingent. This leads to the conclusion that bodies are contingent, so the Necessary Being cannot be a body. In this lesson, we will unpack the two key premises and follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the full Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice that Razi uses the term 'mutaḥayyiz' for body and 'munqasim' for divisible.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument has two main premises: (1) every body is divisible, and (2) every divisible is contingent. Find where these are stated.
The second premise is justified further in the passage.
3
Understand the justification for premise 2
Razi explains that divisibility implies composition, and composition implies dependence on parts, which is the mark of contingency. Trace this reasoning.
Dependence on others (muftaqir ila ghayrihi) is a key concept.
4
Apply the conclusion
Confirm that the conclusion—every body is contingent—leads to the result that Allah, who is necessary, cannot be a body.
This is a standard argument in kalam to establish divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى ويدل عليه وجوه كل متحيز منقسم وكل منقسم ممكن الوجود لذاته، فكل متحيز فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته، وهذا يستلزم أن كل ما لا يكون ممكن الوجود لذاته وجب أن لا يكون متحيزا ، أما أن كل متحيز فهو منقسم لذاته فهذه بناء على نفي الجوهر الفرد، وسيأتي الكلام عليه في هذه المسألة، وأما أن كل منقسم لذاته فهو ممكن لذاته، وذلك لأن كل منقسم فهو مركب وكل مركب فهو مفتقر في تحققه إلى تحقق كل واحد من أجزائه، وكل واحد من أجزاء الشيء غير ذلك الشيء، فكل منقسم فهو مفتقر في تحققه إلى غيره وكل مفتقر في تحققه إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته، فثبت أن كل منقسم فهو ممكن لذاته.
The first proof: and it is indicated by [several] aspects: Every body (mutaḥayyiz) is divisible (munqasim), and every divisible is possible of existence in itself (mumkin al-wujud li-dhatihi). Thus every body is possible of existence in itself. And this necessitates that everything that is not possible of existence in itself must not be a body. As for the [claim] that every body is divisible in itself, this is based on the negation of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), and discussion will come on this in this issue. And as for [the claim] that every divisible in itself is possible in itself, that is because every divisible is composite (murakkab), and every composite is dependent in its realization (muftaqir fi tahqaqihi) upon the realization of each one of its parts, and each one of the parts of a thing is other than that thing. So every divisible is dependent in its realization upon something other than itself, and everything dependent in its realization upon something other than itself is possible in itself. Thus it is established that every divisible is possible in itself.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that Allah (exalted) is not a body. The argument proceeds in steps: First, it states that every body (mutaḥayyiz) is divisible (munqasim), and every divisible is possible in itself (mumkin li-dhatihi). Therefore every body is possible. Since Allah is necessary in Himself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi), He cannot be a body. The proof then addresses two subclaims: (1) that every body is divisible, which is based on denying the atom (jawhar fard) – to be discussed later; (2) that every divisible is possible, because divisibility implies composition, and composition implies dependence on parts, and dependence on others is the mark of contingency. Thus the link from body to contingency is established.

Key Terms
متحيز
منقسم
ممكن الوجود لذاته
واجب الوجود لذاته
مركب
مفتقر
جوهر فرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes the existence of indivisible atoms (jawhar fard).
Correction: Razi explicitly notes that the proof builds on the negation of the indivisible atom; the divisibility of bodies is taken as a starting point.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is the relationship between being a body (mutaḥayyiz) and being possible in itself (mumkin li-dhatihi)?
A) Every body is necessary in itself.
B) Every body is possible in itself.
C) Every body is timeless.
D) Every body is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that every body is contingent, so Allah, being necessary, cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you stand in prayer, do you ever unconsciously picture Allah as having a form or direction? After this proof, what specific thought will you use to correct that image the next time it comes?
?
The proof says that every composite depends on its parts. In your life, have you made any created thing—like a person, a job, or a habit—essential to your well-being, as if it were necessary? Will you identify one and remind yourself today that only Allah is necessary?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is not confined by space or composition, how does that change the way you seek nearness to Him? What is one action you can take today that reflects this transcendence in your worship?

Second Proof: Allah is Not a Body (Temporal Origination of Bodies)
Imam Razi presents the second proof that Allah is not a body based on the origin of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof from Imam Razi that Allah cannot be a body because every body is temporally originated.

Core Idea
Every body is originated; Allah is eternal, so He has no body.

Imam Razi presents a succinct second proof for Allah's transcendence. Building on the earlier established principle that every body (mutaḥayyiz) is temporally originated (muḥdath), he argues that if Allah were a body, He would be originated—an impossibility for the eternal Creator. This lesson unpacks this logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Review the premise about bodies
The proof starts with an already-established claim: every body is originated. Ensure you understand this premise before proceeding.
2
Apply the premise to Allah
Consider the conditional: if Allah were a body, He would be originated. What does this imply about Allah's nature?
3
Reach the conclusion
Since Allah cannot be originated, He cannot be a body. Reflect on how this proof protects divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أنا ذكرنا في مسألة حدوث الأجسام أن كل متحيز فهو محدث فلو كان الباري تعالى متحيزا لكان محدثًا ، لكنه يمتنع أن يكون محدثًا فيمتنع أن يكون متحيزا.
The second proof: We mentioned in the issue of the temporal origination of bodies that every body (mutaḥayyiz) is originated (muḥdath). So if the Creator (al-Bari) – exalted – were a body, He would be originated. But it is impossible for Him to be originated, so it is impossible for Him to be a body.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents a very concise second proof. It relies on an earlier established principle from the discussion on the temporal origination of bodies: every body is temporally originated (muḥdath). Since Allah is eternal and not originated, He cannot be a body. The proof is straightforward and valid if the premise that all bodies are originated is accepted.

Key Terms

محدث

A) Every body is eternal.
B) Every body is originated.
C) Every body is composite.
D) Every body is possible.

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates that since every body is temporally originated, and Allah is eternal, Allah cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that Allah cannot be limited by space or time. Do you ever imagine Allah as being 'up there' or 'in a certain direction' during prayer? How can you train yourself to remember He is beyond all physical concepts?
?
The proof relies on the truth that every body comes into existence and is temporary. In what areas of your life are you still clinging to temporary things as if they will last forever? What would it mean for you to truly live as if only Allah is eternal?
?
If Allah is not a body, then He sees, hears, and knows without organs. Do your actions reflect a belief that He is fully aware of you at every moment, even when no human is watching? What is one change you can make today because of that awareness?

Allah’s Transcendence Beyond Bodily Quiddity
Imam Razi's fourth proof against divine corporeality, using the equality of bodies in quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if Allah were a body, He would need an external specifier to be uniquely divine, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If Allah were a body, He would need a specifier — but the Necessary Being needs no specifier.

Imam Razi presents the fourth proof against divine corporeality. Building on the earlier principle that all bodies are identical in their essential quiddity (māhiyya), he argues that if Allah were a body, His quiddity would be no different from any other body. To then be uniquely divine, He would require an external cause to specify Him — a dependence incompatible with being the Necessary Being. This succinct argument demonstrates that corporeality contradicts divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once in Arabic, then the literal translation, to grasp the argument's flow.
Note the terms 'māhiyya', 'murajjiḥ', and 'mukhaṣṣiṣ'.
2
Identify the key premise
The proof rests on the premise that all bodies are equal in their complete quiddity. Why is that important for the argument?
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
Trace the 'if... then' logic: If Allah were a body → His quiddity equals others → He needs a specifier → impossible → so He is not a body.
4
Ask: what does this imply about Allah?
Reflect on the conclusion: Allah cannot be a body at all. This is a core tenet of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة ذكرنا في مسألة إثبات الصانع أن المتحيزات متساوية في تمام الماهية، فلو كان الباري تعالى متحيزا لكانت ماهيته مساوية لسائر الماهيات في تمام الماهية، ولو كان كذلك لافتقر في اختصاصه بصفات الإلهية إلى مرجح ومخصص، وذلك محال فالقول بكونه متحيزا محال.
The fourth proof: We mentioned in the issue of proving the Maker that bodies (al-mutaḥayyizāt) are equal in the entirety of quiddity (tamām al-māhiyya). So if the Creator – exalted – were a body, His quiddity would be equal to the other quiddities in the entirety of quiddity. And if that were the case, He would be dependent in His particularity with the attributes of divinity (ṣifāt al-ilāhiyya) upon a preponderator (murajjiḥ) and a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ). That is impossible. So the statement that He is a body is impossible.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This fourth proof relies on an earlier principle: all bodies share the same essential quiddity (māhiyya). If Allah were a body, His quiddity would be identical to that of other bodies. But then to be distinguished as the unique divine being, He would require an external cause to specify Him (murajjiḥ/mukhaṣṣiṣ). That dependence is impossible for the Necessary Being. Therefore He cannot be a body.

Key Terms
ماهية
مرجح
مخصص
صفات الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief that Allah is a body (tajsim).
Correction: This proof shows that if Allah were a body, He would require a specifier to be divine, which is impossible. Therefore, Allah cannot be a body, and any anthropomorphic interpretations must be metaphorical.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what would happen if Allah's quiddity were equal to other bodies?
A) He would be eternal.
B) He would need a specifier for divinity.
C) He would be divisible.
D) He would be composite.

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot be a body because He would need an external specifier to be uniquely divine, contradicting His necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah is beyond any form or physical limitation, or does your heart still imagine Him in some shape? What would change in your worship if you realized He needs nothing—not even a location—to be God?
?
If Allah needed a cause to be unique, He would not be worthy of your absolute trust. Yet you sometimes act as if His mercy depends on your actions. How does this proof expose that hidden dependence in your heart?
?
This proof shows that no external reality gives Allah His divinity—He is what He is by Himself. Are you content to worship a God who owes nothing to anyone, or do you secretly wish He fit your expectations? Take a moment to repent of any mental image you have made of Him.
