Fifth Proof: One Knowledge in Multiple Parts is Impossible
Razi presents a proof by contradiction against divine corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's fifth proof that Allah cannot be a body because it would entail either an impossible inherence of a single attribute in multiple loci or polytheism.

Core Idea
If Allah had parts, either one attribute would be in all parts (impossible) or each part would be a god (polytheism).

Imam Razi continues his series of arguments for divine transcendence. This fifth proof targets the idea that Allah could be a physical body. By examining the logical consequences of divisibility, he shows that corporeality leads to absurdities that contradict the oneness of Allah. Let's explore this powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the logical flow of the proof.
2
Identify the two options
Notice the two possibilities Razi considers: a single attribute in all parts, or each part having its own attributes. Understand why each is impossible.
3
Connect to tawhid
Reflect on how this proof reinforces the oneness of Allah and refutes anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : لو كان جسما لكان منقسماً ، فإما أن يقوم علم واحد وقدرة تلك الأجزاء، وهو محال لامتناع حلول الصفة الواحدة في المحال المتعددة، وإما أن يقوم بكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء علم على حدة، وقدرة على حدة، فيكون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء موصوفا بجميع صفات الإلهية، فكان هذا تصريحًا بإثبات الآلهة الكثيرة، وذلك محال على ما سيأتي بيانه.
The fifth proof: If He were a body, He would be divisible. Then either a single knowledge and power would subsist in all those parts – which is impossible because a single attribute cannot inhere in multiple loci – or each part would have its own knowledge and power separately, making each part described with all the attributes of divinity. That would be an explicit affirmation of many gods, and that is impossible, as will be explained later.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This proof considers the implications of Allah being a body with parts. Since a body is divisible, it would have parts. Two possibilities: (1) A single knowledge and power reside in all parts simultaneously – impossible because a single attribute cannot be present in multiple locations. (2) Each part has its own knowledge and power, making each part a divine being – that entails polytheism, which is false. Therefore Allah cannot be a body.

Key Terms
علم
قدرة
صفات الإلهية
آلهة كثيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah could be a body with parts if the parts share a single attribute.
Correction: A single attribute cannot simultaneously inhere in multiple loci; also, separate attributes in each part would imply multiple gods.

Checkpoint
In the fifth proof, what is the first option that is rejected?
A) Each part has its own knowledge.
B) One knowledge in all parts.
C) No parts exist.
D) Parts are infinite.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of a single attribute inhering in multiple parts, combined with the absurdity of multiple gods, proves that Allah cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's knowledge cannot be divided, what does it mean for you when you try to keep some areas of your life hidden from Him? How does this verse prove that every part of your being is completely known by a single, undivided knowledge?
?
This proof shows that polytheism is logically absurd. Is there any person, ambition, or attachment that you have allowed to share the status of 'lord' in your heart? Examine your priorities honestly—where do you see a tendency to set up rivals?
?
The logic here forces you to accept that Allah cannot have parts. How does this truth confront your own tendency to compartmentalize your faith—giving some aspects full devotion while neglecting others? Will you let this proof reshape your life into a single undivided submission?

The Sixth Proof: The Dilemma of Corporeality
Imam Razi presents a dilemma proving that Allah cannot be a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing a body to Allah leads to a logical contradiction, as demonstrated by Imam Razi's sixth proof.

Core Idea
If Allah were a body, He would either be composite or temporal—both impossible.

Imam Razi presents a powerful dilemma: if Allah were a body, He would share essential properties with all bodies. From that shared basis, either He has a distinguishing attribute or He does not. Both paths lead to impossibilities. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the dilemma sink in.
Pay attention to the phrase 'إما أن يكون مباينا وإما أن لا يكون'.
2
Trace the two branches of the dilemma
Identify the first branch: if there is an essential difference, then composition follows, leading to contingency. Identify the second branch: if no essential difference, then all properties of bodies apply, leading to temporality.
Note how each branch ends with an impossibility for Allah.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
Ask yourself: Why is composition incompatible with Necessary Existence? Why is temporality incompatible with divinity? Connect back to earlier lessons on necessity and contingency.
This shows the elegance of the argument: a single shared premise leads to two equally absurd conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : لو كان جسما ومتحيزا لكان مساويًا الأجسام في كونه متحيزا وجسماً، وبعد هذا إما أن يكون مباينا في أمر ذاتي وإما أن لا يكون، فإن باينها في أمر ذاتي كانت الجسمية التي بها المشاركة مغايرة لذلك الأمر الذي حصلت به المباينة فكانت ذاته مركبة من جزأين بهما قوام حقيقته، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته ، فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، ممكن الوجود لذاته، هذا خلف، وأما إن لم يباين هذه الأجسام المحدثة في وصف ذاتي أصلاً، لزم من حدوث هذه الأجسام وقبولها للعفونة والفساد، أن يكون كل جسم كذلك ـ تعالى الله عنه علوًا كبيرا.
The sixth proof: If He were a body and an extended entity (mutaḥayyiz), He would be equal to bodies in being extended and a body. After that, either He differs from them in an essential attribute or He does not. If He differs from them in an essential attribute, then the corporeality (al-jismiyya) by which [He] participates [with bodies] would be different from that attribute by which the difference occurs, so His essence would be composed of two parts by which the reality of His quiddity is constituted. And everything that is like that is possible of existence in itself. Then it would follow that the Necessary of Existence in Himself would be possible of existence in Himself – a contradiction (khalaf). And if He does not differ from these originated bodies in any essential attribute at all, then from the temporal origination of these bodies and their susceptibility to decay and corruption, it follows that every body is like that – exalted is Allah above that with great exaltation.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This is a dilemma argument. If Allah were a body, He shares corporeality with other bodies. Then either He has an essential attribute that distinguishes Him from them, or He does not. If He does have a distinguishing attribute, then His essence comprises two aspects: the shared corporeality and the unique attribute. That composition makes Him contingent (mumkin), but He is necessary – contradiction. If He has no distinguishing attribute, then He shares all properties with other bodies, including temporality and decay. That is impossible for Allah. So in either case, being a body is impossible.

Key Terms
جسم
متحيز
ذاتي
مباين
مركب
ممكن
واجب
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that Allah could be a body different from other bodies without composition — a unique body with no parts.
Correction: The proof shows that any essential difference from other bodies implies that His reality consists of both the common corporeality and that difference, which is a composition of at least two attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, what follows if Allah had an essential difference from other bodies?
A) He would be simple and indivisible.
B) He would be composed of parts and thus contingent.
C) He would be temporal and subject to decay.
D) He would be a unique body without composition.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's sixth proof decisively shows that the concept of a corporeal God leads to logical inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah during your prayers, do you find yourself picturing a form or shape? How does this logical proof challenge that tendency, and what specific step will you take to purify your mental image of Allah?
?
This proof shows that if Allah had any composition, He would be contingent. Are there any aspects of your understanding of Allah that implicitly treat Him as if He were composed of parts (e.g., prioritizing one attribute over another)? How will you correct that in your heart?
?
If Allah were a body, He would be limited like His creation. In what ways have you subconsciously limited Allah by comparing Him to human qualities? Write down one concrete change in your worship that reflects the absolute transcendence established by this lesson.

Seventh Proof: Composition Requires an External Composer
Imam Razi gives the seventh proof: if Allah were a body composed of parts, composition would need an external composer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's seventh proof that Allah cannot be a body because any composition of parts would require an external composer.

Core Idea
If Allah were a body, His parts would need an external arranger — impossible for the Creator.

In this proof, Imam Razi demonstrates that if Allah had a body composed of parts, those parts would need an external cause to arrange them. He considers two possibilities: the parts are identical in nature, or they are different. In both cases, the arrangement is contingent and requires a composer, which contradicts Allah's self-sufficiency. Let's examine the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Start by recognizing that if Allah were a body, He would be composed of parts. This is the conditional premise of the proof.
The proof assumes for argument that Allah is a body and shows it leads to contradiction.
2
First branch: equal parts
If the parts are equal in quiddity, their arrangement (e.g., which part is upper or lower) is arbitrary and depends on a particularizer and preponderator. Thus the body depends on an external composer.
3
Second branch: different parts
If the parts are different, they still share one nature. Positions like left and right are interchangeable, so assembly is contingent and needs an external unifier.
4
Conclusion
In both cases, the body is dependent on an external composer. Therefore, such a being cannot be the independent Creator of the world.
This is the core of the proof: composition implies dependence, which is incompatible with divinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : لو كان جسما كان مركبا من الأجزاء وتلك الأجزاء إما أن تكون متساوية في الماهية أو مختلفة في الماهية، فإن كان الأول فكما الفوقاني أن يكون فوقا، وصح في السفلاني أن يكون فوقا فاختصاص كل واحد منهما بما له من الترتيب والوضع يكون أمرًا جائزا ومفتقرًا إلى المخصص والمرجح، فيكون ذلك الجسم لا محالة مفتقرًا إلى المركب والمؤلف، وما كان كذلك امتنع يكون صانعا للعالم، وأما الثاني وهو أن تكون تلك الأجزاء مختلفة بالماهية، وكل تلك الأجزاء المفروضة تكون له طبيعة واحدة وخاصية واحدة، فالذي يصح أن يكون ممسوس يمين الجزء الفرد من تلك الأجزاء صح أيضا أن يكون ممسوس يساره وبالضد، وإذا كان كذلك كان التفرق على تلك الأجزاء جائزا، وإذا كان كذلك فتلك الأجزاء قد تركبت واجتمعت مع جواز أن تكون متفرقة متباينة، ومتى كان الأمر كذذلك افتقرت في تألفها وتركبها إلى المؤلف والمركب، وكل ذلك على خالق كل العالم محال.
The seventh proof: If He were a body, He would be composed of parts. Those parts are either equal in quiddity or different in quiddity. If the first [i.e., equal], then just as the upper part can be 'upper' and it is valid for the lower part to be 'upper', then the particularity of each one with its own arrangement and position would be a contingent matter dependent upon a particularizer and preponderator. Thus that body would inevitably be dependent upon a compounder and assembler. And that which is like that cannot be the Maker of the world. As for the second [i.e., the parts are different in quiddity], all those supposed parts would have one nature and one property. So what is valid to be touched by the right side of the individual part from those parts is also valid to be touched by its left side, and vice versa. If that is the case, then separation among those parts is possible. If that is the case, then those parts have come together and assembled while it is possible for them to be separate and distinct. And whenever the matter is like that, they become dependent for their unification and composition upon a unifier and compounder. And all that is impossible for the Creator of the entire world.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
The seventh proof analyzes the composition of a body. The parts can be either equal in quiddity or different. If equal, then the arrangement of parts (e.g., upper/lower) is arbitrary and would need a specific reason and a composer. That dependence is incompatible with being the independent Creator. If the parts are different in quiddity, they still share a common nature, and the positions of parts (e.g., which part is right or left) are contingent, so the assembly would need an external assembler. In both cases, the body is dependent and cannot be the Creator.

Key Terms
أجزاء
ماهية
ترتيب
وضع
مخصص
مرجح
مركب
مؤلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the seventh proof, if the parts of a body are equal in quiddity, what does the arrangement of parts depend on?
A) The essence of the parts.
B) A particularizer and preponderator.
C) The necessity of the parts.
D) The indivisibility of the body.

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof shows that any composite being is dependent on an external composer, so Allah, the Creator, cannot be composite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Since every composite thing depends on an external composer, what parts of your life do you treat as self-sufficient? Where are you acting as if you don't need the Creator every moment?
?
The proof shows that even the arrangement of parts requires a preponderator. How does this humble your view of your own choices? Do you acknowledge that your ability to choose is a gift from the Independent One?
?
If Allah cannot be composed of parts, then He is utterly simple and beyond imagination. Are you accidentally worshipping a god limited by your own mental images? How will you guard against forming a mental picture of Allah in your worship today?

The Collapse of Arguments Without Divine Transcendence
Imam Razi gives a reductio proof against corporeality of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses a reductio argument to show that if Allah could be a body, we would lose the ability to deny the divinity of created objects like the sun and moon.

Core Idea
If Allah could be a body, sun-worship becomes irrefutable.

Imam Razi presents a striking proof: the very argument we use to reject the divinity of the sun and moon depends on their being bodies. If we allow that Allah could also be a body, that argument vanishes. This lesson walks through that logical move and its powerful conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof in Arabic and translation
Read the passage once in Arabic, then the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then... because...'.
The proof is short but dense.
2
Identify the key terms
Spot terms like 'جسمية' (corporeity), 'تناهي' (finitude), 'إلهية' (divinity). These are the building blocks of the argument.
3
Understand the reductio structure
The proof assumes the opposite of what we want to prove (that Allah could be a body) and shows it leads to an absurd consequence (we cannot deny sun/moon divinity).
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since the consequence is false, the assumption is false: Allah cannot be a body.
The proof strengthens tawhid and divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أنه لو لم تكن الجسمية والتناهي في المقدار مانعا من الإلهية لتعذر القدح في الهية الشمس والقمر ؛ لأنه لا سبيل لنا إلى القدح في إلهية واحد منهما ، إلا لكونه جسما مركبًا من الأجزاء متناهيا في القدر، فإن جوزنا كون الإله تعالى جسمًا متناهيا انسد هذا الطريق فلا يمكننا القدح في الهية الشمس والقمر ولما كان ذلك باطلاً علمنا أن القول بأن الإله تعالى جسم قول باطل.
The second proof: That if corporeity and finitude in magnitude were not an impediment to divinity, then it would be impossible to refute the divinity of the sun and the moon. Because we have no way to refute the divinity of either of them except that it is a body composed of parts, finite in magnitude. If we allow the deity – exalted – to be a finite body, then this way would be closed, and we would not be able to refute the divinity of the sun and the moon. And since that is false, we know that the statement that the deity – exalted – is a body is a false statement.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This proof takes a different angle. It points out that the only reason we can deny that the sun or moon are gods is because they are bodies – composite, finite. If we admit that Allah could be a body, then the same objection would not apply to the sun or moon, and we would have no argument to show they are not gods. Since it is obvious that they are not gods, we conclude that Allah cannot be a body either.

Key Terms
جسمية
تناهي المقدار
إلهية
شمس
قمر
قدح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this proof is about proving the sun and moon are not gods directly.
Correction: No, the proof assumes that is already obvious. It uses that common knowledge to argue that if Allah were a body, the same reasoning would not hold, so Allah cannot be a body.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's proof, what is the only basis we have to refute the divinity of the sun and moon?
A) They are created in time.
B) They are bodies composed of parts and finite.
C) They are not worshipped by Muslims.
D) They are subordinate to Allah.

Key Takeaway
If Allah could be a body, the argument against sun and moon worship would vanish; since that consequence is false, Allah cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to allow any human-like attribute to Allah in your heart, what consequence would that have for your worship? Can you identify one thought or analogy about Allah that you must repent from today?
?
The sun and moon are magnificent creations, yet worshiping them is false. What created thing do you find yourself admiring more than the Creator, and what step will you take to redirect that reverence?
?
Imam Razi shows that if Allah were a body, the argument against idolatry collapses. In your own life, where have you made an idol of your own desires by attributing to them a kind of absolute authority? Commit to one concrete act of obedience that puts Allah above that desire.

The Sixth Argument: God Cannot Be a Body (Dilemma of Composition)
Imam Razi gives the sixth argument against God being a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth proof from Imam Razi against Allah being a body, which uses a dilemma to show that assuming God is a body leads to the absurd conclusion that He is either composite/contingent or originated/corruptible.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would be either composite or corruptible—both impossible.

Imam Razi continues his series of arguments against corporealism with a logical dilemma. This proof cuts both ways: whether God differs from bodies or not, the assumption that He is a body leads to a contradiction with His necessary existence. As you read the passage, trace the two horns of the dilemma and see how each ends in absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage first without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the concise, logical style of Imam Razi.
The passage is one continuous argument; pay attention to the structure of 'if... then... either... or...'.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The argument splits into two possibilities: (1) God differs from other bodies in an essential attribute, or (2) He does not. Find where each horn is discussed.
The first horn leads to composition and contingency; the second leads to origination and decay.
3
Understand the contradiction in each horn
For the first horn, composition implies contingency, which contradicts God's necessary existence. For the second horn, if God shares all essential attributes with originated bodies, He must also be originated and corruptible.
Both conclusions are impossible for God, so the premise that God is a body must be false.
4
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider why this dilemma is effective: it forces the opponent to choose between two unacceptable outcomes, both derived from the single assumption of corporeality.
No third option is available because the division is exhaustive (either essential difference or no difference).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : لو كان جسما ومتحيزا لكان مساويا للأجسام في كونه متحيزا وجسما، وبعد هذا إما أن يكون مباينا في أمر ذاتي وإما أن لا يكون، فإن باينها في أمر ذاتي كانت الجسمية التي بها المشاركة مغايرة لذلك الأمر الذي حصلت به المباينة فكانت ذاته مركبة من جزأين بهما قوام حقيقته، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، ممكن الوجود لذاته، هذا خلف، وأما إن لم يباين هذه الأجسام المحدثة في وصف ذاتي أصلا، لزم من حدوث هذه الأجسام وقبولها للعفونة والفساد، أن يكون كل جسم كذلك — تعالى الله عنه علوا كبيرا.
The sixth proof: If He were a body and spatially extended, He would be equal to bodies in being spatially extended and a body. After this, either He differs from them in a essential matter or He does not. If He differs in an essential matter, then the body-ness (al-jismiyya) by which there is participation is other than that matter by which the distinction occurs; thus His essence would be composed of two parts by which the constitution of His reality subsists, and everything that is like that is possible of existence in itself, so it would follow that He is Necessary of existence in Himself and possible of existence in Himself — this is a contradiction. And if He does not differ from these originated bodies in any essential attribute at all, then from the origination of these bodies and their susceptibility to decay and corruption, it would follow that every body is like that — exalted is Allah above that, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof (al-ḥujja al-sādisa) against the claim that Allah is a body (jism). The argument is a dilemma. Assume God is a body like other bodies. Then either He differs from them in some essential attribute (amr dhātī) or He does not. If He differs, then the body-ness (al-jismiyya) that He shares with them is distinct from the attribute that distinguishes Him. This implies His essence is composed of two parts, meaning He is contingent (mumkin al-wujūd). But God is necessarily existent (wājib al-wujūd) — contradiction. If He does not differ at all, then since all bodies are originated (ḥāditha) and subject to decay, God would also be originated and corruptible, which is impossible. Thus, the assumption that God is a body leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
جسم
متحيز
مباينة
مشاركة
تركيب
ممكن الوجود
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument only works if God is identical to other bodies; if He differs, the argument fails.
Correction: The argument addresses both cases. If God differs essentially, composition leads to contingency. If He does not differ, origination leads to imperfection. Both are impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What does the sixth argument conclude if God (as a body) differs from other bodies in an essential attribute?
A) God would be unique and transcendent.
B) God's essence would be composed of two parts, making Him contingent.
C) God would be identical to other bodies.
D) God would be incorruptible.

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument uses a dilemma to show that assuming God is a body leads to either composition and contingency or origination and decay, both impossible for the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is completely beyond any composition or limitation, what would change about how you approach Him in your daily prayers? How would your sense of awe deepen? Commit to pausing before each prayer this week to reflect on His absolute transcendence.
?
The argument shows that any concept of God as a body leads to absurdities. Yet many people still imagine Allah in limited ways. Do you have any subtle mental images or anthropomorphic ideas about Allah? Picture a specific one. Resolve to replace it with a proper understanding from this lesson.
?
Consider the fact that everything that is composed is dependent and contingent. This includes your own body and soul. How does the absolute necessity of Allah, in contrast, humble you? Write down one area of your life where you act as if you are independent, and commit to acknowledging your contingency to Allah today.

The Seventh Argument Against Corporealism: Composition Implies Dependence
Imam Razi presents the seventh argument: composition of parts implies dependence on an assembler.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's seventh argument that if God were a body composed of parts, He would depend on an external assembler, which is impossible for the Creator.

Core Idea
A composite body can never be self-sufficient; it always needs an assembler.

This lesson presents Imam Razi's seventh proof that God cannot be a body. The argument examines what it means for something to be composed of parts. Whether parts are equal or different in essence, their arrangement or combination requires an external cause. This refutes corporealism by showing that a composite being cannot be the independent Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the flow of the argument come through.
2
Understand the two cases
The argument splits into two possibilities: parts equal in essence or parts different in essence. Notice how each case leads to the same conclusion.
3
Analyze the first case
If parts are equal, any part could occupy any position. Thus the actual arrangement needs a specifier, making the body dependent.
This is the core of the first branch.
4
Analyze the second case
If parts are different, each part has a unique nature, but still the same part could be on the right or left, so assembly is contingent and requires an assembler.
5
Draw the conclusion
Both cases show dependence on an external assembler, so a composite body cannot be the self-sufficient Creator. This refutes corporealism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : لو كان جسما كان مركبا من الأجزاء وتلك الأجزاء إما أن تكون متساوية في الماهية أو مختلفة في الماهية، فإن كان الأول فكما صح في الفوقاني أن يكون فوقا، وصح في السفلاني أن يكون فوقا فاختصاص كل واحد منهما بما له من الترتيب والوضع يكون أمرا جائزا ومفتقرا إلى المخصص والمرجح، فيكون ذلك الجسم لا محالة مفتقرا إلى المركب والمؤلف، وما كان كذلك امتنع يكون صانعا للعالم، وأما الثاني وهو أن تكون تلك الأجزاء مختلفة بالماهية، وكل تلك الأجزاء المفروضة تكون له طبيعة واحدة وخاصية واحدة، فالذي يصح أن يكون ممسوس يمين الجزء الفرد من تلك الأجزاء صح أيضا أن يكون ممسوس يساره وبالضد، وإذا كان كذلك كان التفرق على تلك الأجزاء جائزا، وإذا كان كذلك فتلك الأجزاء قد تركت واجتمعت مع جواز أن تكون متفرقة متباينة، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك افتقرت في تألفها وتركبها إلى المؤلف والمركب، وكل ذلك على خالق كل العالم محال.
The seventh proof: If He were a body, He would be composed of parts. Those parts are either equal in essence or different in essence. If the first, then just as it is valid for the upper part to be above, and valid for the lower part to be above, so the specification of each one with its order and position would be a contingent matter dependent on a specifier and preferrer; thus that body would inevitably depend on a combiner and assembler, and whatever is like that cannot be the maker of the world. As for the second, that those parts are different in essence, and each of those supposed parts has one nature and one property, then whatever is valid to be touched by the right hand of that individual part is also valid to be touched by its left hand, and vice versa. If so, separation of those parts is possible. If so, those parts have combined and come together while it is possible for them to be separate and distinct. Whenever the matter is like that, they depend in their composition and assembly on an assembler and combiner. All of that is impossible for the Creator of the entire world.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof (al-ḥujja al-sābiʿa). It also relies on the assumption that God is a body composed of parts. The parts are either equal in essence (mutasāwiya fī al-māhiyya) or different. In the first case, if all parts have the same essence, then any part could occupy any position; their actual arrangement requires a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) and preferrer (murajjiḥ), so the body depends on an external assembler. In the second case, if the parts differ in essence, each part has a unique nature; yet even then, the same part could be on the right or left, so separation and combination are possible, again requiring an assembler. Thus a composite body cannot be the self-sufficient Creator. This refutes anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
مركب
أجزاء
ماهية
مخصص
مرجح
مؤلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if parts are equal, God could simply will the arrangement, avoiding dependence.
Correction: The argument shows that even will would be a specification, which itself requires a specifier, still implying contingency.

Checkpoint
In the seventh argument, what does the case of parts equal in essence imply about the body?
A) The parts would necessarily be arranged perfectly without need for a specifier.
B) The body would depend on a specifier for its arrangement, so it cannot be the Creator.
C) The body would be simple and indivisible.
D) The parts would have different natures.

Key Takeaway
The seventh argument demonstrates that any composite being, whether its parts are identical or distinct, relies on an external assembler, and therefore cannot be the self-sufficient Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are made of body, soul, thoughts, and roles—a composition. Who assembles them into a coherent self? Are you the assembler, or do you rely on a self-sufficient Creator to hold you together? If you try to be your own assembler, what does that reveal about your view of God?
?
The argument proves that nothing composite can be self-sufficient. Look at any aspect of your life you've been treating as independent—your career, relationships, or even your worship. Where have you been acting as if these are self-standing, forgetting that they all depend on God?
?
If God were composite, He would be like a machine needing an assembler. Why do you still trust in your own composite plans and strategies as if they do not need God's specification and assembly? Will you today ask the Self-Sufficient One to be the assembler of your affairs?

The Second Argument: Corporealism Would Allow Sun and Moon as Gods
Imam Razi argues that if corporealism were true, one could not refute the divinity of the sun and moon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's second argument against corporealism: that if Allah were a body, one could not refute the divinity of the sun and moon.

Core Idea
If Allah were a body, you could not deny the divinity of the sun or moon.

Imam Razi presents a logical argument that targets the very core of corporealism. He shows that accepting a bodily God would destroy the only basis we have for rejecting the divinity of created bodies like the sun and moon. This forces a choice between two unacceptable outcomes, proving that Allah cannot be a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'
2
Identify the logical structure
This is a reductio ad absurdum proof. Identify the premise, the conditional, the consequence, and the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Ask yourself: why is it absurd to consider the sun or moon divine? How does that absurdity force us to reject corporealism?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أنه لو لم تكن الجسمية والتناهي في المقدار مانعا من الإلهية لتعذر القدح في الهية الشمس والقمر؛ لأنه لا سبيل لنا إلى القدح في إلهية واحد منهما، إلا لكونه جسما مركبا من الأجزاء متناهيا في القدر، فإن جوزنا كون الإله تعالى جسما متناهيا انسد هذا الطريق فلا يمكننا القدح في الهية الشمس والقمر ولما كان ذلك باطلا علمنا أن القول بأن الإله تعالى جسم قول باطل.
The second proof: That if body-ness and finitude in magnitude were not an impediment to divinity, then it would be impossible to reject the divinity of the sun and moon. For we have no way to reject the divinity of either of them except because it is a body composed of parts, finite in magnitude. If we allow that the exalted God is a finite body, this path is blocked, and we cannot reject the divinity of the sun and moon. And since that is false, we know that the statement that the exalted God is a body is a false statement.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage gives another proof (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) against corporealism. It is a reductio ad absurdum: the only way to deny divinity to created bodies like the sun and moon is that they are finite bodies composed of parts. If we allow that God Himself is a finite body, then the same objection would apply to Him, and we would have no grounds to reject the divinity of the sun or moon. Since it is absurd to consider them divine, it follows that God cannot be a body.

Key Terms
جسمية
تناهي
إلهية
قدح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument is merely rhetorical or emotional, not a strict logical proof.
Correction: It is a formal reductio ad absurdum: it assumes the opposite (God is a body) and derives an absurd consequence (sun and moon could be divine), thereby disproving the assumption.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, why can we reject the divinity of the sun and moon?
A) Because they are luminous.
B) Because they are bodies composed of parts and finite.
C) Because they move.
D) Because they are created by God.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi proves that Allah cannot be a body because accepting corporealism would make it impossible to deny divinity to any finite body, including the sun and moon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you found yourself unable to deny that a finite thing like the sun or a worldly object is worthy of ultimate reverence, what would that reveal about your concept of Allah? Are there any 'suns' or 'moons' in your life—people, status, wealth—to which you ascribe ultimate significance, effectively treating them as divine? What would it take to tear them down today?
?
Imam Razi's argument exposes the absurdity of limiting Allah to a body. But do you, in your private thoughts or during worship, unconsciously imagine Allah with human-like limitations—like being in a certain direction, having a shape, or being comparable to creation? Right now, before you finish this reflection, what concrete step can you take to purify your understanding of Allah's transcendence?
?
The proof hinges on the fact that if corporealism were true, you could not even deny the divinity of the sun. Have you ever considered that your own small compromises in tawheed—like fearing creation more than the Creator or seeking fulfilment from finite things—are, in effect, a form of that same error? What is one attachment you will cut off today to make your tawheed absolute?

Disagreement Proves Non-Badihiyya
Imam Razi argues that the premise that every two existents must be either penetrating or separate in direction is not self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the premise used to affirm direction for Allah is not self-evident, using the criterion that self-evident truths cannot be denied by a large group of rational people.

Core Idea
Self-evident truths cannot be denied by the majority of rational people.

Imam Razi begins the eighth issue of his work by tackling the claim that Allah is in a direction. Some scholars argue that every two existents must either be interpenetrating or separate in direction, and that this is self-evident. Razi counters by showing that this premise fails the test of self-evidence: if it were truly self-evident, it would not be rejected by the vast majority of intelligent thinkers. In this lesson, we will examine this first argument and understand how disagreement undermines the claim of badihiyya.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim of direction-affirmers
Read the first part of the passage where Imam Razi reports the claim: every two spatially extended existents must either be interpenetrating or separate in direction. The affirmers claim this is necessary and self-evident knowledge.
2
Grasp Razi's criterion for self-evidence
Imam Razi argues that if a premise were truly self-evident (badihi), it would be impossible for a large group of rational people to unanimously deny it. This is a key logical rule in kalam.
Self-evident truths are those that are immediately known without proof, and disagreement indicates they are not self-evident.
3
Apply the criterion to the premise
Notice that Razi points out that only the Hanabila and Karramiyya affirm direction, while everyone else rejects the premise. Since the majority of intelligent rational people disagree, the premise cannot be self-evident.
This does not prove the opposite conclusion; it only shows that the premise is not a starting point of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثامنة في أنه تعالى ليس في مكان ولا في جهة. اعلم أن كثيرا ممن يثبت كونه تعالى في الجهة يزعم أن كل موجودين متحيزين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر مثل العرض والجوهر، أو يكون مباينا عن الآخر في الجهة مثل موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما قائما بالنفس، ويدعي أن العلم بأن كل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكونا على أحد هذين القسمين علم ضروري، فإن العلم بامتناع وجود موجودين لا يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر ولا مباينا عنه في الجهة علم ضروري بديهي لا يقبل الشك. واعلم أنا نحتاج إلى أن نبين أن هذه المقدمات ليست بديهية، والذي يدل عليه: الحجة الأولى أن العلوم البديهية لا يجوز وقوع الاختلاف فيها للجمع العظيم، فلو كان وقوع هذه المقدمة بديهيا لامتنع إطباق الجمع العظيم على إنكاره، ونرى جمهور الأذكياء من العقلاء متفقين على بطلان هذه المقدمة، فإن إثبات الجهة لله تعالى لم يقل به إلا الحنابلة والكرامية، وأما كل من سواهم فهم متفقون على أن ذاته سبحانه وتعالى منزه عن الاختصاص بالحيز والجهة فظهر أن هذه المقدمة ليست بديهية.
The eighth issue: That He, exalted, is not in a place nor in a direction. Know that many of those who affirm that He, exalted, is in a direction claim that every two spatially extended existents must either be that one pervades the other like accident and substance, or be separate from the other in direction like two existents each subsisting by itself. And they claim that the knowledge that every two existents must be in one of these two categories is necessary knowledge. For the knowledge of the impossibility of two existents existing where neither pervades the other nor is separate from it in direction is necessary, intuitive knowledge that admits no doubt. Know that we need to show that these premises are not intuitive. What indicates that: The first proof: Intuitive sciences do not permit disagreement among a great multitude. If this premise were intuitive, it would be impossible for a great multitude to unanimously deny it. And we see the majority of intelligent rational people agreeing on the falsity of this premise. For affirmation of direction for Allah, exalted, has only been held by the Hanabila and Karramiyya. As for everyone else, they agree that His essence, glorified and exalted, is transcendent of being particularized by space and direction. So it appears that this premise is not intuitive.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage begins the eighth issue (al-masʾala al-thāmina): that Allah is not in a place or direction. First, Imam Razi reports the claim of those who affirm direction: they say that every two spatially extended existents must either be interpenetrating (one pervading the other like accident and substance) or separate in direction (like two self-subsistent substances). They claim this is self-evident (ḍarūrī badīhī). Razi then states that we must show these premises are not self-evident. The first proof (al-ḥujja al-ūlā) is that self-evident truths cannot be disputed by a large group of people. Yet we see that the majority of intelligent rational people reject this premise; only the Hanabila and Karramiyya affirm direction for Allah. Everyone else agrees that Allah transcends space and direction. Thus the premise is not self-evident.

Key Terms
جهة
بديهي
ضروري
ساري
مباين
حيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi is using consensus (ijma') as a proof that the opposite is true.
Correction: Razi is only using the disagreement to disprove the claim of self-evidence, not to establish the truth of the opposite position.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi argue that the premise about two existents is not self-evident?
A) Because many people reject it.
B) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran.
C) Because it is contradictory.
D) Because it is based on sense perception.

Key Takeaway
A premise cannot be self-evident if the majority of rational people disagree with it; thus the premise that every two existents must be either penetrating or separate is not a starting point for knowledge about Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah or religion as 'obviously true' simply because it was common among those around you? How does Razi's argument that self-evident truths cannot be denied by the majority challenge you to examine the basis of your own convictions? What steps will you take to test whether your beliefs are truly self-evident or merely inherited?
?
This lesson shows that even intelligent people can disagree on fundamental premises. Reflect on a time when you were certain about a religious matter but later learned that other rational people held a different view. Did that shake your certainty? How can Razi's method—using disagreement to deny self-evidence, not to prove the opposite—teach you both intellectual humility and confidence in reasoned argument?
?
Razi argues that if a premise were truly self-evident, it would be immune to widespread denial. In your own spiritual life, are there areas where you are afraid to examine your assumptions because you might find them lacking? Will you commit to honestly evaluating the foundations of your faith, trusting that truth can withstand scrutiny?

Second Argument Against Direction: The Common Humanity of Different Individuals
Imam Razi uses the concept of humanity shared by individuals to show essence does not entail location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's second argument against direction: that the concept of humanity does not entail a specific location or measure, and what this implies for the divine essence.

Core Idea
Humanity is shared but location is not – essence does not imply place.

Imam Razi presents a second rational argument to show that Allah is not confined to a direction. He uses a simple observation about human beings: people share the same humanity despite having different bodies and locations. This reasoning helps us see that the essence of something can exist without being tied to a specific place.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence, but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the shared and distinguishing attributes
The passage mentions Zayd, Amr, and Khalid. Identify what they share (humanity) and what distinguishes them (height, color, location). Understand the distinction between these.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Follow the logical conclusion
The argument concludes that humanity as such does not have a specific measure or location. Ask yourself: why does this follow?
The premise is that what is shared is different from what distinguishes.
4
Avoid a common misconception
Some think this argument denies all divine attributes. But it only denies that essence entails spatial determination. Reflect on this distinction.
The common misunderstanding is addressed in the checkpoint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أن صريح العقل يشهد بأن زيدا وعمرا وخالدا يشتركون في معنى الإنسانية ويمتاز كل واحد منهم عن الآخر بطوله وقصره وسواده وبياضه، وما به المشاركة مغاير لما به المباينة، فإذن مفهوم الإنسان من حيث إنه إنسان مغاير للطول والقصر، ولكونه هنا وهناك، ولكونه أسود أو أبيض ثم الإنسان من حيث إنه إنسان إما أن يكون له قدر معين وحيز معين، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك، والأول باطل، وإلا لما كان مشتركا فيه بين الأشخاص ذوات الأحياز المختلفة، والمقادير المختلفة، فثبت أن الإنسان من حيث إنه إنسان ليس له قدر معين ولا حيز معين ولا
The second proof: That pure reason witnesses that Zayd, Amr, and Khalid share in the meaning of humanity (insāniyya) and each is distinguished from the other by his tallness, shortness, blackness, and whiteness. That by which there is participation is other than that by which there is distinction. So the concept of a human, insofar as he is a human, is other than tallness and shortness, and than being here and there, and than being black or white. Then the human insofar as he is a human either has a specific measure and specific location, or does not. The first is false, otherwise it would not be shared among individuals having different locations and different measures. So it is established that the human insofar as he is a human does not have a specific measure nor a specific location, nor
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) that God is not in a direction. It uses an analysis of the concept of humanity (insāniyya). People like Zayd, Amr, and Khalid share the meaning of humanity but differ in height, color, and location. That which is shared (al-mushāraka) is different from that which distinguishes (al-mubāyana). Therefore, the concept of a human as human is separate from particular measures and locations. Since the concept of a human does not inherently include a specific measure or location, it is possible for a human to exist without being tied to a particular space. By analogy, the divine essence can exist without being in a direction. The argument is cut off at the end of the page.

Key Terms

مشاركة
مباينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God has any attributes at all.
Correction: It only denies that essence entails spatial determination; other attributes are not negated.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why can the concept of a human be shared by individuals of different sizes and locations?
A) Because humanity is an accident that can change.
B) Because the essence of humanity does not include specific size or location.
C) Because all humans are essentially the same.
D) Because location is part of the definition of humanity.

Key Takeaway
The essence of humanity does not include a specific location or measure, which supports the idea that the divine essence is also free from direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that what makes you truly human is shared, not your accidental traits. In your daily life, do you treat people based on their essence or their accidents? Where do you need to repent of judging by appearance or status?
?
If the divine essence is even more free from location than humanity, then God is not confined to a mosque, a direction, or a place. Does your worship reflect that truth, or have you been limiting your awareness of God to particular times and spaces? What will you change today to seek Him everywhere?

Objection and Answer on Externality and Direction
Objection about two external existents requiring direction and the answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi responds to the objection that two external existents must have direction, by distinguishing between conceptual possibility and actual existence.

Core Idea
Conceivability does not prove actual existence; it only shows that the intellect can grasp it.

This lesson tackles a subtle objection: if two things exist externally, they must either permeate or be separate in direction. The objector uses this to argue against a directionless Creator. Razi's reply is a masterclass in theological method – he separates the question of conceivability from the question of actual existence. Read carefully to see how he defends divine transcendence without overstepping rational boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Notice the flow: statement, objection, answer.
The start 'شكل معين' may be a continuation from previous page.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection: 'if two existents are external, one must permeate or be separate in direction.' Understand why this seems to challenge a directionless Creator.
3
Understand Razi's distinction
Razi says: 'We do not use this to prove external existence, only to show conceivability.' Distinguish between 'conceivable' and 'actually exists externally'.
This is the key logical move.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why is this distinction important for defending divine transcendence? It shows that the intellect can accept a being without direction, even if it cannot prove its external existence by this argument alone.
5
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you grasped the main point: the purpose of the proof is not to prove external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثامنة : شكل معين، وإذا كان كذلك فالتفتيش قد أخرج من المحسوس ما ليس بمحسوس. فكيف يبعد في خالق كل المحسوسات أن يكون منزها عن الشكل والقدر والحيز ؟ فإن قيل : الإنسان من حيث إنه إنسان لا وجود له في الخارج، بل في العقل، ونحن ندعي أن كل موجودين في الخارج فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما ساريًا في الآخر أو مباينا منه في الجهة فأين أحد المتباينين عن الآخر ؟ قلنا : نحن لا نستدل بهذا الكلام على وجود موجود في الخارج منزه عن المقدار والجهة بل غرضنا منه أنه يمكننا أن نعقل أمرًا من الأمور من أن العقل لا يثبت له تعالى جهة ولا قدرًا، وقد ثبت لنا بهذا الدليل هذا القدر، وإذا لم يكن تصور مثل هذا الموجود مستبعدا في العقل، فبعد ذلك ادعاء أن هذا الموجود هل هو موجود خارج الذهن أو لا يكون موقوف على دليل منفصل.
The Eighth Question: A specific form, and if it is such, then investigation has extracted from the sensible what is not sensible. So how is it far-fetched that the Creator of all sensible things is transcendent above form, measure, and space? If it is said: 'Man insofar as he is man has no existence in the external, but only in the mind, and we claim that every two existents in the external must be such that one permeates the other or is separate from it in direction; so where is one of the two separate things from the other?' We say: 'We do not use this statement to prove the existence of an existent in the external transcendent above measure and direction; rather our purpose in it is that we can conceive a matter of matters such that the intellect does not affirm for Him, exalted, a direction or a measure, and this much has been established for us by this proof. And if the conception of such an existent is not far-fetched in the intellect, then afterwards the claim that this existent exists outside the mind or not depends on a separate proof.'
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer within the eighth question of the work. The author first states that investigation has extracted from the sensible world what is not sensible, so it is not far-fetched that the Creator of all sensible things is transcendent above form, measure, and space. The objector then raises a problem: man as a concept has no external existence, but you claim that every two external existents must either permeate each other or be separate by direction. So where is one separate from the other? The author answers that they are not using this argument to prove the existence of a transcendent being in the external world; rather, their goal is to show that the intellect can conceive of something for which it does not affirm direction or measure. This much is established by the proof. If the conception of such an existent is not intellectually impossible, then whether it actually exists externally is a separate question requiring a different proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that a directionless being cannot exist because external existents must have spatial relations.
Correction: Razi shows that even if external existents require spatial relations, the argument only aims to show that a directionless being is conceivable, not that it actually exists externally. Actual existence requires a separate proof.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the purpose of the proof that mentions conceiving something without direction?
A) To prove that such a being exists externally
B) To show that the intellect can conceive of it, not to prove its external existence
C) To deny that any being can be without direction
D) To prove that all existents must have direction

Key Takeaway
Razi defends divine transcendence by showing that the intellect can conceive of a being without direction, but whether such a being exists externally is a separate question to be proven by other arguments.

?

Third Proof: Imagination Cannot Picture Its Own Form
Third proof that not every existent must have a form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the imagination's inability to conceive its own form proves that existence does not require having a physical shape, supporting divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The imagination exists but cannot picture itself — form is not necessary for existence.

Imam Razi presents a subtle but powerful proof for God's transcendence. He invites us to reflect on our own cognitive faculties: the imagination can picture many things, but it fails to picture itself. This failure reveals that existence and form are not inseparable. Let us examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: premise, two limitations, conclusion.
2
Follow the logical steps
Identify the four parts: (1) we perceive visible things by sight, (2) imagination cannot imagine a form for sight, (3) imagination cannot imagine its own form, (4) therefore existence does not require form. Trace how each step builds on the previous.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if something as real as the imagination can exist without a mental image of itself, why should the Creator need a physical form? This is the core of the argument.
Connect to the broader theme of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : أنا ندرك المبصرات بالقوة الباصرة، ثم الخيال لا يمكنه أن يتخيل للقوة الباصرة كيفية وشكلا بل الخيال يستحضر المتخيلات أشكالاً وصورًا، ويعجز عن أن يستحضر لنفسه صورة وشكلاً ، فذات الوهم والخيال من أصدق الدلائل على أنه لا يجب أن يكون لكل موجود صورة وشكل ؛ لأنه لا صورة عند الخيال من نفسه ولا شكل نفسه.
The Third Proof: We perceive visible things by the power of sight, then the imagination cannot imagine for the power of sight a quality and a form; rather the imagination summons imagined things as shapes and forms, and it is incapable of summoning for itself an image and a form. So the essence of the estimative faculty and imagination is among the truest proofs that it is not necessary that every existent have an image and a form, because the imagination has no image of itself and no form of itself.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is the third proof (al-hujja al-thalitha) for God's transcendence above form and shape. The argument proceeds by analogy: we perceive visible objects through the power of sight, but the imagination (al-khayal) cannot conceive of the power of sight itself as having a quality or shape. The imagination can summon mental images of things that have shapes and forms, but it is utterly unable to form an image of itself. Thus, the essence of the estimative faculty and imagination (dhat al-wahm wa al-khayal) is among the clearest evidences that not every existent must possess an image or a form. Since the imagination itself exists without any mental image of itself, it proves that existence does not require having a form. This supports the idea that the Creator can exist without being confined to a shape.

Key Terms

خيال
وهم
صورة
شكل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims the imagination does not exist or that it is not real.
Correction: The proof uses the existence of imagination to show that form is not a necessary condition for existence; it does not deny its reality.

Checkpoint
What does the third proof use to show that not every existent must have a form?
A) The power of sight cannot see itself
B) The imagination cannot imagine its own form
C) The intellect cannot conceive of formlessness
D) The estimative faculty is separate from the body

Key Takeaway
The imagination's inability to picture itself demonstrates that existence does not require having a physical form, supporting divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You can imagine countless things, but you cannot imagine your own imagination. Does this limit unsettle you? What does it reveal about your tendency to equate reality with what you can picture? Will you now accept that the most real things—your own mind, your soul, your Creator—may be beyond your mental images?
?
When you try to think of God, do you catch yourself imposing a form, even vaguely? This proof shows that existence does not require form. Will you let go of the need to picture God and instead worship Him as He is: beyond shape, beyond imagination?
?
If your own imagination exists without being able to picture itself, what does that say about your own existence? Are there parts of yourself—your thoughts, your spirit—that you cannot fully picture? How will this truth change the way you approach your own identity and your relationship with the Unseen?

Fourth Proof: Concepts Need No Direction
Fourth proof that two concepts can be conceived without spatial relation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concepts of negation and affirmation do not require spatial relations, and how this paves the way for conceiving of God without location.

Core Idea
Mental concepts can exist without spatial relations.

Imam Razi continues his proofs for divine transcendence by examining our most basic logical ideas. He takes the concepts of negation and affirmation — the building blocks of all judgment — and asks whether the intellect can demand that these concepts be related in space. His answer reveals a fundamental principle: mental conception does not depend on direction or location. This is a crucial step in freeing our understanding of God from spatial categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once. Notice how Razi begins with a self-evident truth and then poses a question.
The passage is short but dense; take your time with each phrase.
2
Identify the self-evident principle
Razi states that it is self-evident that negation and affirmation cannot both combine and cannot both be removed. This is the law of non-contradiction and excluded middle.
Reflect on why this is called 'the clearest of self-evident sciences.'
3
Analyze the question about spatial relation
Razi asks: can the intellect require that the meaning of negation either permeate the meaning of affirmation or be separate from it by direction? Recognize that this is demanding a spatial relationship between two mental concepts.
The terms 'sārī' (permeating) and 'mubāyin' (separate) are key.
4
Understand the impossibility
Razi declares that such a judgment is impossible in itself (mumtaniʿ li-dhātihi). Concepts are not physical objects; they do not occupy space.
This shows that spatial relations are not necessary for conception.
5
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, it is permissible to conceive two concepts in the mind without any spatial relation. This proof supports the possibility of conceiving God without location.
This is one of several proofs Razi offers for divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة أن أجلى العلوم البديهية الأولية أن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، وهذا العلم لا بد وأن يكون مسبوقا بتصور معنى النفي والإثبات فهل يتمكن العقل من أن يقول : إن معنى النفي لا بد وأن يكون ساريا في معنى الإثبات أو مباينا عنه بالجهة والحيز ، بل هذا الحكم ممتنع لذاته ؟ فعلمنا أن استحضار متصورين في العقل بحيث لا يكون أحدهما ساريًا في الآخر أو مباينا عنه بالجهة أمر جائز.
The Fourth Proof: The clearest of the self-evident primary sciences is that negation and affirmation do not combine and are not both removed. And this knowledge must be preceded by a conception of the meaning of negation and affirmation. So can the intellect say that the meaning of negation must permeate the meaning of affirmation or be separate from it by direction and space? Rather this judgment is impossible in itself. So we know that bringing to mind two conceptions in the intellect such that neither one permeates the other nor is separate from it by direction is permissible.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is the fourth proof (al-hujja al-rabi'a) demonstrating that it is possible to conceive of two things without one being in the other or separate by direction. The author starts with a self-evident truth: negation and affirmation (al-nafy wa al-ithbat) cannot both be true at once (they are contradictory) and cannot both be false (they are exhaustive). This knowledge itself requires that we already conceive the meanings of negation and affirmation. Now, can the intellect demand that the meaning of negation must either 'permeate' (sarī) the meaning of affirmation or be 'separate' from it by direction and space? No, that judgment is intrinsically impossible (mumtani' li-dhatihi), because concepts like negation and affirmation are mental, not spatial. Therefore, we learn that it is permissible to conceive of two concepts in the mind without requiring any spatial relationship. This paves the way for conceiving of God without location.

Key Terms
النفي والإثبات
بديهي
ساري
مباين
جهة
حيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negation and affirmation are physical things that must occupy space or be related spatially.
Correction: The proof shows that these are mental concepts; demanding spatial relations between them is intrinsically impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, is it necessary that the meaning of negation be spatially related to the meaning of affirmation?
A) Yes, they must permeate each other
B) Yes, they must be separate in direction
C) No, that judgment is impossible in itself
D) No, but only because they are opposites

Key Takeaway
Concepts like negation and affirmation can be conceived without spatial relations, proving that mental conception does not require direction or location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you unconsciously imagine a direction or location? How does this proof challenge that habit? Will you actively train your mind to conceive of God without spatial assumptions starting today?
?
This proof uses the concepts of negation and affirmation—basic mental operations. Have you ever reflected on the fact that your own thoughts are not constrained by space? What does that tell you about the nature of your soul, and how might that change the way you approach prayer?
?
The intellect cannot demand spatial relations between concepts. Is there an area of your belief where you have tried to confine God to a place or direction? What step will you take tonight to correct that misconception in your heart?

The Fifth Proof: Logical Division and the Conceivable Third Possibility
Fifth proof that a third logical division is not self-evidently impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof from logical division, which demonstrates that the impossibility of a third division (neither permeation nor directional separation) is not self-evident, leaving room for divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The mind does not automatically reject a third logical possibility where God is neither in the world nor separate by direction.

This proof uses a simple logical division to challenge the assumption that God must either permeate the world or be spatially separate from it. By showing that a third possibility is not self-evidently impossible, al-Razi opens the door for a conception of God's existence without spatial relation altogether. Let's trace the steps of this argument and see why it matters for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical division presented in the first sentence.
The proof is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three logical divisions
The proof states that for any two existents, either one permeates the other, or they are separate by direction, or neither. Identify each division.
The third division is the key focus.
3
Reflect on the intellect's reaction
The author asks us to introspect: do we find an innate aversion to the third division? He says no; the intellect is undecided and seeks proof.
This shows the third division is not self-evidently impossible.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The proof concludes that the impossibility of the third division is not among self-evident truths (first principles). So a proof is needed either way.
5
Connect to divine transcendence
Apply the argument to God and the world. God is neither permeating the world nor spatially separate from it—this third option is now rationally open.
This supports God's transcendence beyond spatial categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن صريح العقل يشهد بأن كل موجودين يفرضان فإما أن يكون أحدهما ساريًا في الآخر أو مباينا عنه في الجهة أو لا ساريًا في الآخر ولا مباينا عنه بالجهة، وهذا القسم الثالث لا شك أنه حاصل بحسب القسمة العقلية، وإذا رجعنا إلى أنفسنا لم نجد البتة في عقولنا نبوة عن إثبات هذا القسم الثالث، بل نجد العقل متوقفا فيه بالنفي والإثبات طالبًا للحجة على الجزم بإثباته أو الجزم بامتناعه، فعلمنا أن امتناع هذا القسم ليس من الأوليات.
The Fifth Proof: The clear intellect testifies that for any two existents that are supposed, either one of them permeates the other or is separate from it in direction, or neither permeates the other nor is separate from it by direction. And this third division is undoubtedly attained according to the rational division. And if we reflect on ourselves, we find absolutely no aversion in our intellects to affirming this third division. Rather we find the intellect pausing in it between negation and affirmation, seeking a proof for definitively affirming it or definitively deeming it impossible. So we know that the impossibility of this division is not among the self-evident truths.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is the fifth proof (al-hujja al-khamisa). It presents a logical division: for any two supposed existents, there are three possibilities: (1) one permeates the other, (2) they are separate by direction, or (3) neither permeation nor directional separation. The third division is rationally conceivable. When we introspect, we find no innate repugnance (nubū') in our intellects against accepting this third division. Instead, the intellect is undecided, neither affirming nor denying it outright, but seeks a proof to determine whether it is actual or impossible. This shows that the impossibility of the third division is not self-evident (laysa min al-awwaliyyat). Therefore, it is possible that God exists without being in any spatial relationship with the world.

Key Terms
قسمة عقلية
ساري
مباين
أوليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fifth proof asserts that the third division (neither permeation nor directional separation) is actual for God and the world.
Correction: The proof does not claim actuality; it only argues that the impossibility of this division is not self-evident, leaving it as a rationally open possibility.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth proof conclude about the third division (neither permeation nor directional separation)?
A) It is self-evidently impossible
B) It is self-evidently possible
C) Its impossibility is not self-evident
D) It is impossible because the intellect rejects it

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of God being neither in the world nor spatially separate is not self-evident, so the intellect must seek proof before ruling it out.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a possibility about God's nature—like His being beyond space—simply because it felt strange or unfamiliar? What does this lesson's call to intellectual humility demand of you right now?
?
The proof shows the intellect pauses before the third division. In your own journey of faith, where have you jumped to conclusions about God's relationship to the world without pausing to seek proof? What would it look like for you to truly pause and seek evidence?
?
If the impossibility of God being neither inside nor outside the world is not self-evident, then your current conception of God's spatial relation to creation may be unexamined. Will you commit today to re-examine that assumption, not with blind acceptance, but with honest intellectual scrutiny?

The Mind Accepts Formlessness: The Proof from Numbers
Razi enumerates the sixth proof using quiddities of numbers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how abstract concepts like numbers demonstrate that the mind can conceive of existence without place or direction, undermining the claim that God's formlessness is impossible.

Core Idea
If the mind can conceive of 'one' without space, it can conceive of God without space.

Imam Razi presents a powerful argument from our own cognitive experience: we can think about numbers like 'one' and 'two' without imagining them in any specific location or shape. This shows that the intellect is not repelled by the idea of an existence without spatial attributes. The claim that such an existent is impossible cannot, therefore, be a self-evident truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi starts by stating a proof, then gives examples, and then draws a conclusion.
The passage is a short continuous argument.
2
Understand 'quiddities'
Identify what Razi means by 'quiddities' (māhiyyāt). These are essences or whatness of things, like the concept of numbers.
Quiddities are intellectual concepts, not physical objects.
3
Follow the reasoning
Trace the logic: we know numbers have no place or direction → the intellect conceives them without spatial attributes → therefore the intellect does not reject a being without place or direction.
This step is crucial for the conclusion.
4
Connect to the theological point
Ask: How does this argument help prove that God's existence without location is not impossible? Razi says it shows the impossibility is not self-evident.
This refutes those who say God must be in a direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : أن عقلنا يشير إلى ماهيات مع أنا نعلم بالضرورة أن ليس لتلك الماهيات أحياز، ولا جهات فإن ماهيات الأعداد مثل مسمى الواحد ومسمى الاثنين وكذا مسمى مراتب الأعداد أمور يدركها العقل ولا يمكنه أن يحكم على ماهية الواحد من حيث إنه واحد بأن مكانه هو موضع كذا ومقداره هو مقدار كذا، بل يعقل العقل هذه الماهيات ولا يعتبر معها البتة لا مقدارًا ولا شكلاً، ولا حيزا فثبت هذه الوجوه أن العقل من حيث إنه هو لا يستبعد تصور معقول حال ما لا له مكانا ولا جهة ولا قدرًا ولا شكلاً ، فثبت أن العلم بامتناع هذا الموجود ليس من العلوم البديهية.
The Sixth Proof: Our intellect points to quiddities while we know by necessity that those quiddities have no spaces and no directions. For the quiddities of numbers, such as the meaning of 'one' and the meaning of 'two', and likewise the meanings of the ranks of numbers, are things that the intellect perceives, and it cannot judge concerning the quiddity of the one insofar as it is one that its place is such-and-such location and its magnitude is such-and-such magnitude. Rather, the intellect conceives these quiddities and does not consider with them at all any magnitude, nor shape, nor space. So these aspects establish that the intellect insofar as it is does not consider it far-fetched to conceive of an intelligible that has no place, no direction, no magnitude, and no shape. Thus it is established that the knowledge of the impossibility of such an existent is not among the self-evident sciences.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is the sixth proof (al-hujja al-sadisa). The argument turns to intellectual quiddities (māhiyyāt) that are abstract, like the numbers one and two and the orders of numbers. We know necessarily that these quiddities have no spatial location or direction. When the intellect conceives of the quiddity of 'one' as such, it cannot attribute to it a specific place or magnitude. It simply understands the meaning without any consideration of size, shape, or space. Therefore, the intellect does not find it absurd to conceive of something that has no place, direction, magnitude, or shape. Consequently, the claim that such an existent (i.e., God) is impossible is not a self-evident truth. The intellect is comfortable with the concept, and thus a proof for God's existence without location is needed, not a rejection based on immediate intuition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that numbers exist in the external world without spatial attributes.
Correction: The proof does not claim that numbers exist externally; it only speaks about the conceptual quiddities (māhiyyāt) that we conceive in the mind. Numbers as concepts have no place or direction, and this mental fact is used to show that the intellect can accept formless existence.

Checkpoint

B) The concept of the number one
C) The concept of a body
D) The concept of fire

Key Takeaway
The intellect's ability to conceive abstract quiddities like numbers without spatial attributes shows that believing in a formless God is not rationally impossible, and thus the burden of proof lies on those who reject it.

?
This proof shifts the burden: the one who denies God's existence without location must prove impossibility. Have you ever dismissed this idea without evidence? Will you examine your own assumptions before rejecting the unseen?
