Refutation of Indwelling by Possibility and Objection
Razi refutes possible indwelling and begins answering an objection about conditional necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's refutation of possible indwelling and his initial response to the objection that God's essence might conditionally necessitate indwelling.

Core Idea
Possible indwelling still requires dependence, which is the essence of hulul and incompatible with God.

Imam Razi now addresses the second division: indwelling by possibility (jawaz). He argues that the very concept of indwelling entails the indweller's dependence on the locus; if that dependence is absent, the term 'indwelling' is meaningless. He then considers an objection: could God's essence necessitate indwelling conditionally upon the existence of the locus? His response begins but is cut off in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for possible indwelling
Read the Arabic from 'وأما القسم الثاني' to the end of the passage. Note the two parts: the refutation of possible indwelling, and the objection-and-response.
The response is incomplete; we only have the beginning 'We do not accept' and a suggestion of conditional necessity.
2
Understand the refutation of possible indwelling
Razi says: The intelligible meaning of indwelling is that the indweller is dependent on and in need of the locus. If that meaning is absent, it is not indwelling at all. Since possible indwelling implies no necessary dependence, it cannot be called indwelling.
This refutation is conceptual: the essence of hulul is iftiqar; without iftiqar, there is no hulul.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector says: Why not say that God's essence necessitates indwelling for itself, conditioned upon the existence of the locus? That is, the necessity is not absolute but conditional. The text gives Razi's beginning of a response: 'We do not accept' and then a question about the condition.
The response is cut off, but the direction is to show that conditional necessity still implies a kind of dependence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why is indwelling by possibility (jawaz) false?
A) Because it would make God temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God is dependent on your actions or as if He is utterly self-sufficient? In what moment today did you act as if God needed you to uphold something? What would change if you truly believed that nothing adds to God and nothing diminishes Him?
?
The passage argues that even a conditional indwelling implies dependence. Are there areas of your life where you treat God as conditionally dependent on your circumstances—praying only when you feel you need Him, or assuming He acts only when conditions are right? How does that reflect a subtle form of hulul in your heart?
?
Razi's argument cuts off before the full response. Do you trust that divine transcendence is so absolute that any hint of dependence is impossible? Or do you hold back, thinking perhaps God needs something from you? Confess one way you have treated God as needing your worship or obedience, and commit to seeing it as your need, not His.

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
5 min
Why Allah Cannot Indwell Anything
Razi divides indwelling into necessary and contingent, then refutes both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of indwelling (necessary and contingent) and why both are rationally impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
Allah cannot indwell anything because that would make Him dependent—impossible for the Necessary Being.

Imam al-Razi begins his refutation of the doctrine of indwelling (ḥulūl) by dividing the claim into two logical possibilities: that Allah indwells something necessarily, or that He does so contingently. Through clear rational arguments, he demonstrates that both alternatives lead to contradictions with Allah's necessity and self-sufficiency. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two categories
Read the first part of the passage where Al-Razi divides indwelling into necessary (wujūban) and contingent (jawāzan). Note that this division covers all possibilities.
This is a classic kalam method: exhaust all possibilities before refuting.
2
Refutation of necessary indwelling
Al-Razi gives two arguments against necessary indwelling. First, it would entail either that Allah is originated or the locus is eternal—both impossible. Second, it would make Allah dependent on the locus, but dependence implies possibility, while Allah is necessary.
3
Refutation of contingent indwelling
Examine why contingent indwelling is also false. Al-Razi argues that the very meaning of indwelling requires the indweller to be dependent on the locus. If the indwelling is contingent, that dependence is absent, so the term 'indwelling' doesn't apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : أما القول بالحلول فهو باطل لأنه تعالى لو حل في شيء لكان إما أن يحل مع وجوب أن يحل أو جواز أن يحل والأول باطل لوجهين : الأول : أن ذلك يقتضي إما حدوث الحال أو قدم المحل، وكلاهما محالان. والثاني : أنه لما حل مع وجوب أن يحل كانت ذاته مفتقرة إلى ذلك المحل، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن بالذات. وأما القسم الثاني وهو أنه تعالى يحل مع جواز أن يحل فهذا أيضًا باطل ؛ لأن المعقول من الحلول كون الحال مفتقرًا إلى المحل ومحتاجا إليه، وإذا لم يوجد هذا المعنى لم يتحقق معنى الحلول.
We say: As for the claim of indwelling (al-ḥulūl), it is false, because if the Most High indwelt something, then either He would indwell necessarily or contingently. The first is false for two reasons: The first is that it entails either the temporal origination of the indweller (al-ḥāl) or the pre-eternity of the locus (al-maḥall), both of which are impossible. The second is that when He indwells necessarily, His essence becomes dependent on that locus, and that which is dependent on another is possible in essence. As for the second division, that the Most High indwells contingently, this is also false because the intelligible meaning of indwelling is that the indweller is dependent on the locus and in need of it, and if this meaning is not present, the meaning of indwelling is not realized.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the doctrine of indwelling (ḥulūl). Imam al-Razi divides the claim into two possibilities: that God indwells something necessarily (wujūban) or contingently (jawāzan). He rejects both. For the first (necessary indwelling), he gives two arguments. First, it would entail either that the indweller (God) is originated (ḥādith) or that the locus (the thing indwelt) is eternal (qadīm), both of which are impossible for God. Second, if God indwells necessarily, His essence would be dependent on that locus, but dependence on another indicates possibility (imkān), while God is the Necessary Being. For the second (contingent indwelling), al-Razi argues that the very concept of indwelling requires the indweller to be dependent on the locus; if that dependence is absent, the term 'indwelling' does not apply. Thus, both forms are refuted.

Key Terms
الحلول
الحال
المحل
الوجوب
الجواز
المفتقر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Razi's refutation only targets Christian beliefs about the incarnation, not a general impossibility.
Correction: Razi's arguments are universal: they prove that no entity can indwell another in a way that would entail dependence of the indweller. The logic applies to any claim of divine indwelling.

Checkpoint
Why is necessary indwelling impossible according to the second reason?
A) It implies God's essence is dependent and thus possible
B) It implies the locus is God
C) It implies God is originated
D) It implies the locus is originated

Key Takeaway
Indwelling is rationally impossible because it would either make Allah dependent (impossible for the Necessary Being) or render the term meaningless.

The concept of indwelling makes God dependent on a location. Have you ever confined God to a mosque, a supplication, or a feeling? How can you repent from this subtle form of limiting the Limitless?

If you truly internalized that God is utterly independent (al-Ghanī), which habit or attachment would you be most ashamed of clinging to? What step will you take today to break that attachment?

Objection and Answer on Necessary Indwelling
Razi responds to a Christian objection about necessary indwelling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the Christian objection that God could necessarily indwell under conditional circumstances.

Core Idea
Razi defends divine transcendence by showing that necessary indwelling contradicts God's self-sufficiency.

The objection attempts to preserve the idea of divine indwelling by introducing conditions: perhaps God's essence necessitates indwelling only when the locus exists, or the locus itself necessitates God's indwelling. Razi answers by examining the core meaning of indwelling, showing that it either makes God dependent on creation or renders the term meaningless. This lesson walks through the objection and Razi's response to see how he upholds the transcendence of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the full passage from 'If it is said' to 'it is not necessary?' to understand the two versions of the objection.
The objection uses the ideas of conditional necessity and agency of the locus.
2
Identify Razi's refutation strategies
Notice that Razi addresses two variations: one where God's essence necessitates indwelling conditionally, and one where the locus necessitates it. Both are rejected.
3
Analyze the key distinction
Focus on Razi's answer: the distinction between al-ḥāl (indweller) and al-maḥall (locus) is that the indweller depends on the locus. If this dependence holds, God would depend on creation—a contradiction. If not, the term 'indwelling' does not apply.
This is the core of Razi's argument.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Consider why Razi calls the contradiction a 'khulf' (absurdity). This shows that the objection cannot stand without undermining God's necessary existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه حل مع وجوب أن يحل، قوله : يلزم إما قدم المحل أو حدوث الحال. قلنا لا نسلم ولم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن ذاته يوجب لذاته الحلول في المحل، بشرط وجود ذلك المحل، وقبل وجود المحل لم يكن شرط هذا الاقتضاء حاصلاً، فلا جرم لم يحصل هذا الحلول، وبعد وجود المحل حصل شرط هذا الاقتضاء، فلا جرم حصل هذا الحكم على سبيل الوجوب وأيضًا فلم لا يجوز يقال : المحل يوجب كون الله تعالى حالا فيه، وعند وجود هذا المحل وجب هذا الحلول، وقبل وجوده، لا يجب ؟ الجواب : الفرق بين الحال والمحل إنما يظهر من حيث إن الحال مفتقر إلى المحل والمحل غني عنه، فإن حصل هذا المعنى لزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، مفتقرًا إلى الغير، فيكون الواجب لذاته ممكن الوجود لذاته، هذا خلف هذا خلف، وإن لم يحصل هذا المعنى لم يحصل مسمى الحلول.
If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that He indwells necessarily, while his statement 'it entails either the pre-eternity of the locus or the temporal origination of the indweller'? We say: We do not accept it. Why is it not permissible to say that His essence necessitates indwelling in the locus, conditionally upon the existence of that locus, and before the locus existed the condition of this necessitation was not present, so this indwelling did not occur; and after the locus existed the condition of this necessitation occurred, so this result occurred necessarily. Also, why is it not permissible to say that the locus necessitates that God Most High be an indweller in it, and when this locus exists this indwelling becomes necessary, and before it exists it is not necessary? The answer: The distinction between the indweller (al-ḥāl) and the locus (al-maḥall) is only apparent in that the indweller is dependent on the locus and the locus is independent of it. If this meaning occurs, then the Necessary Being in essence would be dependent on another, so the Necessary Being in essence would be possible in essence — this is a contradiction. And if this meaning does not occur, then the designation of indwelling does not occur.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. A hypothetical Christian objector raises a nuanced point: Maybe God's essence necessitates indwelling only conditionally upon the existence of the locus, so that before the locus existed there was no indwelling, but after the locus exists, indwelling becomes necessary. Or alternatively, the locus itself might necessitate God to indwell it at the moment of its existence. Al-Razi answers by emphasizing the core meaning of indwelling: the indweller (al-ḥāl) is dependent on the locus (al-maḥall) and the locus is independent. If this dependence is present, then the Necessary Being would depend on another, which contradicts Its necessity. If it is absent, then the term 'indwelling' does not apply. Thus, the objection fails because it either leads to contradiction or to incoherence of the concept.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء
الشرط
الوجوب
الخلف
الحال
المحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection's conditional necessity is a valid way to avoid the dilemma of pre-eternity or origination.
Correction: Razi shows that conditional necessity fails because it either makes God dependent on creation or undercuts the meaning of indwelling.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction Razi uses in his answer?
A) Between necessary and contingent being
B) Between the indweller and the locus in terms of dependence
C) Between eternal and originated
D) Between essence and attribute

Key Takeaway
Razi preserves divine transcendence by demonstrating that necessary indwelling either implies contradiction or incoherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi's Refutation of Union (al-Ittiḥād)
Razi presents a trilemma refuting the logical possibility of union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the doctrine of union (al-ittiḥād) is logically incoherent according to Imam al-Razi's trilemma.

Core Idea
Union is logically impossible: two cannot become one while remaining distinct.

Imam al-Razi refutes the claim that God could unite with Christ or any created being. He presents a simple but devastating trilemma: no matter what happens to the two things supposed to unite, the result is not a genuine union. This argument relies on the basic logic of identity and difference. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: Razi states the claim, then lists three possibilities, then concludes.
The key terms are الاتحاد (union), البقاء (remaining), الفناء (perishing).
2
Understand the trilemma
Identify the three logical possibilities Razi presents. For each one, ask: does this produce a true union?
3
Analyze the third possibility
The third case is subtle: one remains, the other perishes. Razi says this cannot be union because the existent cannot be identical with the non-existent. Reflect on why this is a decisive refutation.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Razi concludes that both claims of indwelling (ḥulūl) and union are false. Consider how this argument defends divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول بالاتحاد فهو أيضًا باطل؛ لأن الشيئين إذا اتحدا فهما حال الاتحاد إن كانا باقيين فهما اثنان لا واحد، وإن عدما معًا كان الحاصل ثالثًا مغايراً لهما، وإن بقي أحدهما وفني الآخر الاتحاد أيضاً؛ لأن الموجود لا يكون عين المعدوم فثبت بما ذكرنا أن القول بالحلول والاتحاد باطل.
As for the claim of union (al-ittiḥād), it is also false, because when two things unite, at the moment of union: if both remain, then they are two, not one; if both cease to exist, then the result is a third thing different from them; if one remains and the other perishes, then [the concept of] union also is impossible, because the existent cannot be identical with the non-existent. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the claims of indwelling and union are false.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of union (ittiḥād). Al-Razi presents a trilemma using the logic of identity. When two distinct things supposedly unite, there are three logical possibilities: (1) Both remain as they were — in which case they remain two, not one, so no union has occurred. (2) Both cease to exist — then what results is a third entity distinct from the original two, so again we do not have union of the original two. (3) One remains and the other perishes — this cannot be union because the existent cannot be the same as the non-existent. Thus, all possibilities fail, establishing the impossibility of union. Al-Razi concludes that both indwelling and union are false.

Key Terms
الاتحاد
البقاء
الفناء
المغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think union means a mystical blending where two become one in essence.
Correction: Razi's argument shows that such blending is logically impossible because the components cannot lose their identity without ceasing to be themselves.

Checkpoint
What is the third possibility for union that Razi refutes?
A) Both remain
B) Both cease
C) One remains and one perishes
D) Both become a third thing

Key Takeaway
Razi proves the impossibility of union through a logical trilemma, preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before studying this lesson, did you ever imagine that a creature could somehow 'become one' with God in essence? Now that Razi has shown this is logically impossible, what must you do with any lingering thought that God is 'mixed' with His creation?

If union with God is impossible, then what is the real relationship between you and your Creator? How should this reshape how you approach Him in worship—not as seeking fusion, but as a servant to a transcendent Lord?
?
Razi's argument forces you to choose: either God is wholly other or He is not. Have you been living as if God is 'close' in a way that compromises His transcendence? What concrete change in your belief or practice will you make today to affirm His absolute separateness?

Refuting Incarnation through the Hal-Mahall Distinction
Razi refutes the incarnation argument through the hal-mahall distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses the distinction between the indwelling attribute (hal) and the locus (mahall) to refute the doctrine of incarnation (hulul).

Core Idea
If God indwells creation, He would depend on it—impossible.

In this passage, Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi addresses a key objection raised by proponents of incarnation (hulul). They argue that if a locus exists, God must indwell it. Razi's answer hinges on a precise distinction: the indwelling attribute (hal) depends on its locus, while the locus is independent. This distinction unravels the entire argument and upholds divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector asks why it is not possible that a certain locus necessitates God's indwelling in it. They claim that when the locus exists, indwelling becomes obligatory.
Notice that the objector assumes a causal relationship between locus and indwelling.
2
Understand Razi's key distinction
Razi distinguishes between the hal (indwelling attribute) and the mahall (locus). The hal depends on the mahall, while the mahall is independent. This is the heart of the refutation.
This distinction is crucial for the next step.
3
Follow the logical consequence
If the hal depends on the mahall, then if God were the hal, He would depend on creation—contradicting His necessary existence. If He does not depend, then there is no indwelling at all.
The conclusion is that the objection collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا فلم لا يجوز يقال : المحل يوجب كونه تعالى حالا فيه، وعند وجود هذا المحل وجب هذا الحلول، وقبل وجوده، لا يجب ؟
الجواب : الفرق بين الحال والمحل إنما يظهر من حيث إن الحال مفتقر إلى المحل والمحل غني عنه، فإن حصل هذا المعنى لزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، مفتقرًا إلى الغير، فيكون الواجب لذاته ممكن الوجود لذاته، هذا خلف هذا خلف، وإن لم يحصل هذا المعنى لم يحصل مسمى الحلول.
And also why is it not permissible to say: The locus necessitates that He (exalted) be indwelling in it, and when this locus exists, this indwelling becomes obligatory, and before its existence, it is not obligatory? The answer: The difference between the indwelling attribute (al-hal) and the locus (al-mahall) is only apparent from the fact that the hal depends on the mahall and the mahall is independent of it. If this meaning obtains, then the Necessarily Existent by essence (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi) would depend on another, so the Necessarily Existent by essence would be possible by essence (mumkin al-wujud li-dhatihi), this is a contradiction. And if this meaning does not obtain, then the meaning of indwelling does not obtain.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the doctrine of hulul (incarnation). The objector argues that the locus could necessitate the indwelling of God in it, so when the locus exists, indwelling is necessary. The answer clarifies the distinction between the indwelling attribute (al-hal) and the locus (al-mahall). The hal depends on the mahall, while the mahall is independent. If this dependency holds, then the self-necessary God would be dependent on another, which is contradictory. If it does not hold, then there is no indwelling at all. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
حال (al-hal)
محل (al-mahall)
حلول (hulul)
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud)
ممكن الوجود (mumkin al-wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Incarnation is possible because if a locus exists, God must indwell it.
Correction: Razi shows that indwelling would make God dependent on the locus, contradicting His necessary existence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The hal-mahall distinction shows that God cannot indwell creation because that would make Him dependent.

Refuting Ittihad: Three Logical Possibilities
Razi refutes ittihad via three logical possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the doctrine of ittihad (union between God and creation) is logically impossible through three exhaustive cases.

Core Idea
Union between two things is logically impossible in all three scenarios.

Imam al-Razi now tackles the doctrine of ittihad, which claims that God and creation can become one. He demonstrates its falsehood by analyzing what must happen when two things unite. The argument is simple but powerful: there are only three possibilities, and each leads to an absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text aloud or silently. Notice the three conditional clauses starting with 'in' (if).
The passage is concise; each condition is a separate case.
2
Identify the three cases
List the three possibilities: both remain, both cease, one remains and the other perishes. Understand the logical outcome of each.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that both hulul (indwelling) and ittihad are false. See how the three cases together make the proof airtight.
No fourth possibility exists.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and try the activity to solidify your grasp.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول بالاتحاد فهو أيضًا باطل؛ لأن الشيئين إذا اتحدا فهما حال الاتحاد إن كانا باقيين فهما اثنان لا واحد، وإن عدما معًا كان الحاصل ثالثًا مغايراً لهما، وإن بقي أحدهما وفني الآخر الاتحاد أيضاً؛ لأن الموجود لا يكون عين المعدوم فثبت بما ذكرنا أن القول بالحلول والاتحاد باطل.
As for the saying of union (ittihad), it is also false; because when two things unite, at the moment of union: if they both remain, they are two not one; if they both cease, that which results is a third thing different from them; and if one remains and the other perishes, there is no union [؟] because the existent cannot be identical to the non-existent. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the doctrines of indwelling and union are false.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage refutes the doctrine of ittihad (union between God and creation) by analyzing the three logical possibilities of what happens when two things unite. The three cases are exhaustive and all lead to absurdity: if both remain after union, they are still two; if both cease to exist, a third thing emerges different from both; if one remains and the other perishes, there is no union because the existent cannot be identical to the non-existent. Therefore, both hulul and ittihad are false.

Key Terms
اتحاد (ittihad)
باقيان (baqiyan)
عدما (adam)
فني (fani)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ittihad is possible if one thing absorbs another, like water mixing with milk.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even in such mixing, the components remain or a third substance appears; true union where two become one without remainder is impossible.

Key Takeaway

Refutation of Claims about Power and Honorific Sonship
Razi dismisses two Christian claims regarding Jesus Christ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi dismisses two Christian claims about Jesus' creative power and honorific sonship.

Core Idea
Creation power belongs to God alone; honorific titles are scriptural, not rational.

Al-Razi refutes two Christian assertions: that Jesus had creative power, and that being called 'son' is honorific. He states the first is false because creation is exclusive to God, and the second becomes a scriptural debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Razi addresses two separate claims in a concise manner.
The passage is very compact; each claim gets one sentence.
2
Identify the two claims
List the two Christian claims that al-Razi refutes. The first is about power to create, the second about the honorific title 'son'.
This helps you see the structure of the refutation.
3
Understand the refutation method for each claim
For the first claim, note that al-Razi says it is false because only God has power to create. For the second, note that he categorizes it as a scriptural (sam'i) issue, implying it is not a rational argument.
The second claim is not dismissed as false but deferred to scriptural discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول بأنه تعالى خلق فيه القدرة على خلق الجسم فهذا أيضًا باطل لأنا سنبين أنه لا قدرة لأحد على الإيجاد إلا الله تعالى. وأما القول بأنه تعالى سماه ابنًا على سبيل التشريف فهذا البحث يصير سمعيًا.
As for the saying that He (exalted) created in him (Jesus) the power to create bodies, this is also false, for we will show that no one has power to create except God. And as for the saying that He (exalted) called him a son by way of honor, this discussion becomes scriptural (sam'iy).
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage dismisses two Christian claims. First, the claim that God created in Jesus the power to create bodies is rejected because creation is exclusive to God, a point to be detailed later. Second, the claim that Jesus is called 'son' honorifically is a scriptural matter (sam'iy) that will be addressed later; it is not a rational argument.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
إيجاد (ijad)
تشريف (tashrif)
سمعي (sam'i)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That someone other than God can have creative power, or that the title 'son' for Jesus is a rational argument.
Correction: Creation is exclusive to God, and the debate about honoring Jesus as a 'son' is scriptural, not rational.

Checkpoint

A) Rational
B) Scriptural

D) Mystical

Key Takeaway
God alone has power to create, and claims about Jesus' sonship are scriptural, not rational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if God alone is the Creator, or do you sometimes attribute your successes to your own ability and effort? What would change if you internalized this truth more deeply today?
?
When you encounter a belief that is based solely on scripture (like the honorific sonship), does your heart submit, or does your mind demand rational proof? Which is more pleasing to God?
?
If Jesus had no power to create, then all other humans have even less. Does this humble your pride? Will you acknowledge that every breath and thought is a direct gift from God, not your own doing?

Scriptural Argument from Abraham's Statement
Razi cites Quranic verse to argue that change disqualifies divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a scriptural verse (Quran 6:76) is used to argue that anything subject to change cannot be divine.

Core Idea
Change is incompatible with divinity — a principle affirmed by Abraham's own words.

Imam Razi introduces a second proof for divine transcendence, this time drawing from the Qur'an. He cites the verse where Abraham, upon witnessing celestial bodies setting, declares he does not love those that set. Razi explains that 'setting' is a form of change, and anything that changes cannot be a god. Let's examine this concise but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its context
Read the Quranic verse (6:76) where Abraham rejects heavenly bodies that set. Understand the scene: Abraham is reasoning about the nature of divinity.
The verse is part of a larger narrative.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on 'al-aful' (setting) and 'taghayyur' (change). Razi equates setting with change. Define these terms in your own words.
Setting refers to the sun, moon, and stars disappearing below the horizon.
3
Trace the logical implication
Follow Razi's reasoning: setting is change → change implies originatedness → what is originated cannot be divine. Conclude that any being subject to change is not God.
This argument supports the broader kalam principle that God is unchanging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : قول الخليل ما لا أحب الافلين [الأنعام: ٧٦] والأقول : عبارة عن التغير، وهذا يدل على أن المتغير لا يكون إلها أصلا .
The second proof: The saying of al-Khalil (Abraham), 'I do not love those that set' (Quran 6:76), and 'al-uqul' [likely 'al-aful' (the setting)] is an expression of change, and this indicates that the changing thing cannot be a god at all.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents a scriptural argument. Razi cites the Quranic verse where Abraham rejects heavenly bodies that set (i.e., change position). The act of setting (al-aful) is a type of change. Since God is unchanging, anything that changes cannot be divine. This supports the argument that originated attributes (which imply change) cannot belong to God.

Key Terms
الافول
التغير
الخليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that change is inherently negative or that the argument condemns all physical processes.
Correction: The argument is specifically about divinity: change indicates originatedness and dependence, which are incompatible with the necessary, independent nature of God.

Checkpoint

A) Light
B) Setting or declining
C) Rising
D) Motion

Key Takeaway
The act of setting (change) is a sign of contingency and thus incompatible with the nature of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you willing to say, like Abraham, 'I do not love those that set' when it comes to your deepest affections? What would change in your life if you truly internalized that only the unchanging God deserves your ultimate love?
?
The moment you love something that can change, you set yourself up for disappointment. Is there anything in your life right now that you are holding onto as if it will never change? How can you actively reorient your heart towards the One who never sets?

Abraham's Argument Against Change in God
Razi cites Abraham's verse to prove the changing cannot be God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Abraham's rejection of setting celestial bodies to prove that God cannot be subject to change.

Core Idea
Change indicates contingency, and the Necessary Being cannot change.

Razi presents a concise Qur'anic proof for divine transcendence. Abraham's statement 'I do not love those that set' is interpreted as a rejection of anything subject to change. Since change implies dependence and imperfection, such a being cannot be God. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let Razi's compact reasoning come through.
Notice that Razi uses a single Qur'anic verse to build a logical proof.
2
Identify the key term 'al-uful'
Understand that 'setting' (al-uful) is defined as change (al-taghayyur). This definition is crucial for the argument.
In kalam, change encompasses any form of alteration or dependence.
3
Follow the logical inference
Razi links Abraham's rejection of setting things to the conclusion that anything that changes cannot be a god. This is a deductive argument: major premise (change implies non-divinity), minor premise (setting is change), conclusion.
4
Reflect on why change is incompatible with God
Consider that change implies contingency (need for something else) and imperfection, both of which are impossible for the Necessary Being.
This connects to broader kalam principles about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : قول الخليل ما لا أحب الافلين [الأنعام: ٧٦] والأقول : عبارة عن التغير، وهذا يدل على أن المتغير لا يكون إلها أصلا .
The second proof: the saying of al-Khalil [Abraham] 'I do not love those that set' (Qur'an 6:76). And 'setting' (al-uful) is an expression of change (al-taghayyur). This indicates that that which changes cannot be a god at all.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the Qur'an that God cannot be subject to change. Razi cites Abraham's statement in Surah al-An'am, where Abraham rejects the worship of celestial bodies that set (i.e., undergo change). The word 'setting' (al-ufool) implies change. From this, Razi deduces that anything that changes is not fit for divinity. This is a concise argument: if a being can change, it is contingent and dependent, thus cannot be the necessary ground of all existence, i.e., God.

Key Terms
آفلين
أقول
تغير
متغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That change is only physical; but in kalam, any type of change indicates contingency and imperfection.
Correction: Razi's argument covers all forms of change, not just physical. Even conceptual or relational change implies contingency, which is incompatible with the Necessary Being.

Checkpoint

C) That Abraham did not believe in any god.
D) That setting is a natural process.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that change is incompatible with divinity using Abraham's logical rejection of setting celestial bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abraham rejected the setting stars even though they were revered by his people. What 'stars' in your life—things that change and fade—are you still holding onto as sources of security or identity? Will you speak the same rejection today?
?

Where have you placed your ultimate trust in something that is fleeting? What would it mean for you to actually live as if only the Unchanging One is worthy of your devotion?
?
The proof is simple yet demands total reorientation. How would your daily decisions change if you truly believed that any change in a thing disqualifies it from being your ultimate concern? Are you ready to let go of what you love because it sets?

Karramiyya's First Proof and Razi's Response
Razi presents and defers the answer to the Karramiyya's first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the Karramiyya's first proof for accidents in God and why Razi defers his response.

Core Idea
Presence of attributes does not automatically make God a locus of originated events.

The Karramiyya presented a proof that since God has attributes like speech and will, and these originate in time, God must be a subject for accidents. Razi acknowledges the proof but postpones his answer, indicating that the key issue lies in whether those attributes are indeed originated. This passage shows a typical dialectical move.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Notice the two premises: attributes are originated, attributes must inhere in a subject. The conclusion: God is locus of accidents.
The Karramiyya do not assume God has attributes; they argue from established attributes.
2
Examine Razi's response
He does not deny anything yet. He simply says he will answer their proof for origination later. Why might he do that?
This is a common strategy to separate issues.
3
Reflect on the strategy
This deferral is a rhetorical strategy to separate the issues. It does not mean he concedes. The real debate will be about whether divine attributes are created in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الكرامية فقد احتجوا من وجهين : الحجة الأولى : أنهم أقاموا الدلائل على أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بحدوث الكلام والإرادة والسامعية والمبصرية، ثم أقاموا الدلائل على أنه لا بد من قيام الصفة بالموصوف، وحينئذ يحصل من هذين الأصلين كونه تعالى محلا للحوادث. الجواب عن الحجة الأولى: أنا سنجيب عن دلائلكم في حدوث الكلام والإرادة والسمع والبصر.
As for the Karramiyya, they argued in two ways. The first proof: They adduced proofs that it is necessary to acknowledge the origination of speech, will, hearing, and sight, then they adduced proofs that an attribute must inhere in the described subject. Then from these two premises, it follows that He, exalted, is a locus of originated events (mahall li-l-hawadith). The answer to the first proof: I will answer your proofs regarding the origination of speech, will, hearing, and sight.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the Karramiyya sect and Razi's preliminary response. The Karramiyya argue that since God is described as having speech, will, hearing, and seeing, and these are originated attributes (haditha), they must inhere in a subject, making God a locus for originated accidents. Razi's answer is a deferral: he says he will later answer their proofs for the origination of those attributes. This indicates that Razi does not accept that these attributes are originated, but he postpones the detailed refutation. This is a standard move to acknowledge the objection and promise a later response.

Key Terms
كرامية
كلام
إرادة
سامعية
مبصرية
قيام الصفة بالموصوف
محل للحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi's deferral means he accepts the Karramiyya's conclusion.
Correction: Razi is postponing his answer, indicating that he intends to refute the premise that divine attributes are originated.

Checkpoint

A) He refutes it on the spot by denying the premises.
B) He says he will answer their proofs for the origination of those attributes later.
C) He agrees that God is a locus of accidents.
D) He argues that attributes do not need a subject.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya's proof that God is a locus of accidents hinges on the claim that divine attributes are originated; Razi postpones his refutation of that claim.

?
The Karramiyya assumed that God's attributes are originated. Is there an area in your life where you assume something about God that is not yet settled? What would it cost you to suspend judgment?

Answer to the Third Question: God as Creator of All
Razi states that God is creator of all servants, voiding moral attributions to His actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that if God is the creator of all servants, then human moral categories such as good and evil cannot be applied to God's actions.

Core Idea
God's creation of all actions voids applying human moral standards to Him.

Razi now answers a key objection: if God's actions are subject to moral evaluation, then He could be blamed. But Razi argues that because God is the creator of all servants, there is no independent standard of good and evil by which His actions can be judged. This short passage encapsulates a foundational principle of Ash'ari theology: divine transcendence beyond human moral frameworks.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise structure: a claim followed by its consequence.
The passage begins with 'Aʿmāl' (Acts) which is likely a heading.
2
Identify the key premise
What is the main premise that Razi asserts? He says he will establish that God is the creator of all servants. This is the foundation.
The phrase 'khaliqu li-jamīʿ al-ʿibād' is central.
3
Understand the conclusion
If the premise is true, then what follows? The attribution of good and evil to God's actions, and consideration of benefits, becomes invalid. Ask yourself why that is.
The logic: if God creates everything, there is no external standard to judge Him.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
This argument protects divine transcendence. Human moral categories are not applicable to God; He does not act for reasons of benefit or to avoid evil.
This is a key Ash'ari stance in contrast to Mu'tazila who hold that God must act for the best.
5
Test your understanding
Use the checkpoint to verify you have grasped the connection between God's creation and the invalidation of moral judgments on God.

Source Text

Acts. And the answer to the third question: We will establish the proof that He, exalted, is the creator of all servants, and if that is the case, then the attribution of God's actions to good and evil and consideration of interests is invalidated.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a brief answer to a question. It states that the speaker will demonstrate that God creates all servants, and from that it follows that assigning moral values like good and evil to God's actions, and saying they are for the sake of benefits, is invalid. The point is that if God creates everything, then there is no independent standard of good and evil to judge His actions.

Misconception: That God's actions can be judged by human moral standards (e.g., good or evil).
Correction: Razi argues that if God is the creator of all servants and their actions, then there is no independent standard of good and evil beyond God's will. Therefore, God's actions are beyond such categorization.

Second Preliminary and Opponents' Positions
Razi introduces the second preliminary and the debate over God's visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second preliminary regarding God's transcendence and visibility, and how he frames the debate by distinguishing between self-evidence and inference.

Core Idea
Razi argues that denying God's visibility cannot be claimed as self-evident; it requires rational proof.

Razi begins by reaffirming that God is beyond body and place, yet He can be seen. He then identifies three groups: philosophers, Mu'tazila, and Karramiyya/Mujassima, each with different grounds for their view. Crucially, he separates those who claim the denial is self-evident from those who infer it, setting up his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the preliminary statement
Read the first part of the passage where Razi states that God is transcendent but visible.
2
Identify the opponents
Note the three groups Razi mentions: philosophers, Mu'tazila, and Karramiyya/Mujassima. Understand their positions.
Philosophers and Mu'tazila simply deny vision; Karramiyya affirm it only because they believe God is a body.
3
Understand the framing
Recognize that Razi distinguishes two types of claims: self-evident and inferential. This sets up the next lesson's proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية: اعلم أنا بينا أنه سبحانه وتعالى منزه عن أن يكون جسما وجوهرا ومختصا بمكان وحيز، ثم إنا ندعي أن هذا الشيء الموصوف بهذه الصفات يمكن رؤيته، وهذا القول يخالفنا فيه كل من ليس على مذهبنا، أما الفلاسفة والمعتزلة فذلك ظاهر، وأما الكرامية والمجسمة فهم إنما يسلمون جواز رؤية الله تعالى لاعتقادهم أنه جسم وفي مكان، وهم متفقون على أنه تعالى لو لم يكن جسما ولم يكن في مكان فإنه يمتنع وجوده. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: القول بامتناع رؤية هذا الموجود إما أن تدعي فيه أنه معلوم بالبداهة أو تدعى فيه أنه معلوم بالاستدلال. أما دعوى البداهة فباطلة ويدل عليه وجوه: الحجة الأولى: أن البديهي متفق عليه بين العقلاء، وهذا غير متفق عليه، فلا يكون بديهيا. الحجة الثانية: أنا إذا عرضنا على عقولنا رؤية هذا الموجود بالتفسير الذي لخصناه وعرضنا على عقولنا أن الواحد نصف الاثنين لم نجد القضية الأولى في قوة هذه الثانية. الحجة الثالثة: أن حكم الوهم والخيال في معرفة الله تعالى إما أن يكون مقبولا أو لا يكون مقبولاً، فإن كان مقبولا لا يمتنع إثبات ذات منزهة عن الكمية والكيفية والجهة والمعتزلي يسلم أن ذلك باطل، وإن لم يكن مقبولا لم يكن حكم الوهم بأن ما كان منزها عن الجهة كان غير مرئي واجب القبول لأن الوهم والخيال لما صار كل واحد منهما مردود الحكم في بعض الأحكام لم يبق الاعتماد عليها في شيء من المواضع. وبالجملة: فإن كان حكم الوهم حقا كان الحق مع المجسم، وإن كان مردودا كان الحق معنا، أما المعتزلي فإنه يرد حكمه في إثبات التجسيم والجهة، ويقبل حكمه في مسألة الرؤية، فكان كلامه متناقضا. فثبت بما ذكرنا أن من نفى الرؤية بالوجه الذي ذكرناه لا بد أن يعول في نفيها على الدليل لا على ادعاء الضرورة. وإنما قدمنا هذه المقدمة لأن هؤلاء المعتزلة في أول الأمر يلتزمون الاستدلال، وفي آخر الأمر عند العجز عن تمشية الدليل كأنهم يسرعون في ادعاء الضرورة، فقدمنا هذه المقدمة صونا للكلام عن هذا الخبط.
Second preliminary: Know that we have explained that He, glorified and exalted, is transcendent beyond being a body, a substance, or being particularized by place and location. Then we claim that this thing described by these attributes can be seen. And this statement is opposed by everyone who is not upon our school. As for the philosophers and the Mu'tazila, that is apparent. As for the Karramiyya and the Mujassima, they only affirm the possibility of seeing God, exalted, because they believe He is a body and in a place. And they agree that if He were not a body and were not in a place, then His existence would be impossible. When you know this, we say: The statement that it is impossible to see this existent either claims that it is known by self-evidence (bi-l-badāha) or claims that it is known by inference (bi-l-istidlāl). As for the claim of self-evidence, it is false, as indicated by several proofs. The first proof: That which is self-evident is agreed upon by rational people, but this is not agreed upon, so it is not self-evident. The second proof: If we present to our minds the vision of this existent according to the interpretation we have summarized, and present to our minds that one is half of two, we do not find the first proposition to be as strong as the second. The third proof: The judgment of imagination (al-wahm) and fantasy (al-khayāl) concerning knowledge of God, exalted, is either acceptable or not. If it is acceptable, then it is not impossible to affirm an essence transcendent beyond quantity, quality, and direction—and the Mu'tazili concedes that that is false. If it is not acceptable, then the judgment of imagination that what is transcendent beyond direction is not visible is not necessarily accepted, because when each of imagination and fantasy has its judgment rejected in some rulings, reliance upon them is not remaining in any matter. In sum: If the judgment of imagination is true, then the truth is with the Mujassima; if it is rejected, then the truth is with us. As for the Mu'tazili, he rejects its judgment in affirming corporeality and direction, but accepts its judgment in the matter of vision; thus his statement is contradictory. So it is established by what we have mentioned that whoever denies vision in the manner we have mentioned must rely in his denial upon evidence, not upon claiming necessity. And we have only presented this preliminary because these Mu'tazila at first commit to inference, but at the end, when unable to sustain the inference, they rush to claim necessity. So we have presented this preliminary to protect the discussion from this confusion.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative refutation. Razi first states the second preliminary: God is transcendent beyond body, substance, and location, yet we claim He can be seen. He notes that opponents vary: philosophers and Mu'tazila deny visibility outright, while Karramiyya and Mujassima affirm it only because they believe God is a body in a place. Razi then focuses on those who deny visibility and argues that their denial cannot be claimed as self-evident (badīhī). He then provides three proofs against the claim of self-evidence. First, self-evident truths are universally agreed upon, but this is not. Second, the proposition 'God can be seen' does not have the same evident force as 'one is half of two'. Third, the judgment of imagination (wahm) and fantasy (khayāl) is unreliable: if accepted, it would lead to anthropomorphism (which the Mu'tazili rejects); if rejected, it cannot be used to deny vision. Razi exposes the Mu'tazili contradiction: they reject imagination when it supports corporeality but accept it when it denies vision. He concludes that denial of vision must be based on inference, not claimed necessity, and explains that he preempted this argument to prevent the Mu'tazili tactic of retreating to self-evidence when their inferences fail.

Key Terms
بداهة
استدلال
جسم
جهة
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi, which groups oppose the claim that God can be seen?
A) Only the Mu'tazila
B) Philosophers and Mu'tazila, plus Karramiyya and Mujassima
C) Only the philosophers
D) Only the Karramiyya

Key Takeaway
Razi's second preliminary establishes that while God is transcendent, He can be seen, and the debate is over whether the denial is self-evident or requires inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to accept truths about God that your intellect does not immediately grasp as self-evident, or do you insist that everything must be obvious before you believe? What does that demand of your humility before divine revelation?
?
The Karramiyya only affirmed God's visibility because they reduced Him to a body. Are there areas in your own thinking where you subtly limit God to something more 'visible' or tangible, avoiding the awe of His absolute transcendence? What would it take to truly trust in a God who is beyond your senses?
?
Razi forces the opponent to admit that their denial of vision is either self-evident or inferred. In your own doubts, do you sometimes treat your skepticism as self-evident without examining its foundations? How would your faith be strengthened if you instead subjected your doubts to rigorous proof?

Three Proofs Against Self-Evidence
Razi provides three proofs against the claim of self-evidence in denying God's visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's three arguments that demonstrate that the denial of God's visibility is not self-evident, including his exposure of the Mu'tazili contradiction.

Core Idea
Razi uses consensus, comparative evidentness, and the unreliability of imagination to refute self-evidence.

In this passage, Razi presents three proofs to show that the impossibility of seeing God cannot be claimed as self-evident. The first proof cites lack of agreement among rational people. The second compares it to a self-evident truth like 'one is half of two'. The third, and most powerful, critiques the role of imagination and reveals the inconsistency of the Mu'tazila, who accept imagination when it denies vision but reject it when it supports corporeality.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the first proof: if it were self-evident, all rational people would agree, but they don't.
Note that Razi considers the disagreement of the Karramiyya etc. as evidence.
2
Read the second proof
Read the second proof: compare the proposition to a basic truth like 'one is half of two'. The first is not as strong.
This is a test of intuitive certainty.
3
Read the third proof
Read the third proof about the judgment of imagination and fantasy, and how the Mu'tazili is inconsistent.
This is the most complex part; pay attention to the logical options.
4
Understand the conclusion
Read Razi's conclusion that the denial must be based on inference, not claimed necessity, and why he preempted this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية: اعلم أنا بينا أنه سبحانه وتعالى منزه عن أن يكون جسما وجوهرا ومختصا بمكان وحيز، ثم إنا ندعي أن هذا الشيء الموصوف بهذه الصفات يمكن رؤيته، وهذا القول يخالفنا فيه كل من ليس على مذهبنا، أما الفلاسفة والمعتزلة فذلك ظاهر، وأما الكرامية والمجسمة فهم إنما يسلمون جواز رؤية الله تعالى لاعتقادهم أنه جسم وفي مكان، وهم متفقون على أنه تعالى لو لم يكن جسما ولم يكن في مكان فإنه يمتنع وجوده. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: القول بامتناع رؤية هذا الموجود إما أن تدعي فيه أنه معلوم بالبداهة أو تدعى فيه أنه معلوم بالاستدلال. أما دعوى البداهة فباطلة ويدل عليه وجوه: الحجة الأولى: أن البديهي متفق عليه بين العقلاء، وهذا غير متفق عليه، فلا يكون بديهيا. الحجة الثانية: أنا إذا عرضنا على عقولنا رؤية هذا الموجود بالتفسير الذي لخصناه وعرضنا على عقولنا أن الواحد نصف الاثنين لم نجد القضية الأولى في قوة هذه الثانية. الحجة الثالثة: أن حكم الوهم والخيال في معرفة الله تعالى إما أن يكون مقبولا أو لا يكون مقبولاً، فإن كان مقبولا لا يمتنع إثبات ذات منزهة عن الكمية والكيفية والجهة والمعتزلي يسلم أن ذلك باطل، وإن لم يكن مقبولا لم يكن حكم الوهم بأن ما كان منزها عن الجهة كان غير مرئي واجب القبول لأن الوهم والخيال لما صار كل واحد منهما مردود الحكم في بعض الأحكام لم يبق الاعتماد عليها في شيء من المواضع. وبالجملة: فإن كان حكم الوهم حقا كان الحق مع المجسم، وإن كان مردودا كان الحق معنا، أما المعتزلي فإنه يرد حكمه في إثبات التجسيم والجهة، ويقبل حكمه في مسألة الرؤية، فكان كلامه متناقضا. فثبت بما ذكرنا أن من نفى الرؤية بالوجه الذي ذكرناه لا بد أن يعول في نفيها على الدليل لا على ادعاء الضرورة. وإنما قدمنا هذه المقدمة لأن هؤلاء المعتزلة في أول الأمر يلتزمون الاستدلال، وفي آخر الأمر عند العجز عن تمشية الدليل كأنهم يسرعون في ادعاء الضرورة، فقدمنا هذه المقدمة صونا للكلام عن هذا الخبط.
Second preliminary: Know that we have explained that He, glorified and exalted, is transcendent beyond being a body, a substance, or being particularized by place and location. Then we claim that this thing described by these attributes can be seen. And this statement is opposed by everyone who is not upon our school. As for the philosophers and the Mu'tazila, that is apparent. As for the Karramiyya and the Mujassima, they only affirm the possibility of seeing God, exalted, because they believe He is a body and in a place. And they agree that if He were not a body and were not in a place, then His existence would be impossible. When you know this, we say: The statement that it is impossible to see this existent either claims that it is known by self-evidence (bi-l-badāha) or claims that it is known by inference (bi-l-istidlāl). As for the claim of self-evidence, it is false, as indicated by several proofs. The first proof: That which is self-evident is agreed upon by rational people, but this is not agreed upon, so it is not self-evident. The second proof: If we present to our minds the vision of this existent according to the interpretation we have summarized, and present to our minds that one is half of two, we do not find the first proposition to be as strong as the second. The third proof: The judgment of imagination (al-wahm) and fantasy (al-khayāl) concerning knowledge of God, exalted, is either acceptable or not. If it is acceptable, then it is not impossible to affirm an essence transcendent beyond quantity, quality, and direction—and the Mu'tazili concedes that that is false. If it is not acceptable, then the judgment of imagination that what is transcendent beyond direction is not visible is not necessarily accepted, because when each of imagination and fantasy has its judgment rejected in some rulings, reliance upon them is not remaining in any matter. In sum: If the judgment of imagination is true, then the truth is with the Mujassima; if it is rejected, then the truth is with us. As for the Mu'tazili, he rejects its judgment in affirming corporeality and direction, but accepts its judgment in the matter of vision; thus his statement is contradictory. So it is established by what we have mentioned that whoever denies vision in the manner we have mentioned must rely in his denial upon evidence, not upon claiming necessity. And we have only presented this preliminary because these Mu'tazila at first commit to inference, but at the end, when unable to sustain the inference, they rush to claim necessity. So we have presented this preliminary to protect the discussion from this confusion.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative refutation. Razi first states the second preliminary: God is transcendent beyond body, substance, and location, yet we claim He can be seen. He notes that opponents vary: philosophers and Mu'tazila deny visibility outright, while Karramiyya and Mujassima affirm it only because they believe God is a body in a place. Razi then focuses on those who deny visibility and argues that their denial cannot be claimed as self-evident (badīhī). He then provides three proofs against the claim of self-evidence. First, self-evident truths are universally agreed upon, but this is not. Second, the proposition 'God can be seen' does not have the same evident force as 'one is half of two'. Third, the judgment of imagination (wahm) and fantasy (khayāl) is unreliable: if accepted, it would lead to anthropomorphism (which the Mu'tazili rejects); if rejected, it cannot be used to deny vision. Razi exposes the Mu'tazili contradiction: they reject imagination when it supports corporeality but accept it when it denies vision. He concludes that denial of vision must be based on inference, not claimed necessity, and explains that he preempted this argument to prevent the Mu'tazili tactic of retreating to self-evidence when their inferences fail.

Key Terms
بداهة
وهم
خيال
استدلال
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazili position is consistent because they reject imagination altogether.
Correction: Razi shows they accept imagination's judgment on vision but reject it on corporeality, which is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's third proof, what is the result if the judgment of imagination is accepted?
A) It would prove the Mu'tazili correct
B) It would lead to affirming corporeality
C) It would be irrelevant
D) It would support the denial of vision

Key Takeaway
Razi's three proofs establish that the denial of God's visibility is not self-evident, forcing the opponent to rely on inference, thereby exposing the Mu'tazili inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself assuming something about God is 'obvious', ask yourself: is it truly self-evident, or have you accepted it without evidence? Will you examine your own assumptions today?
?
Razi exposes the inconsistency of the Mu'tazila—they reject imagination in one area but accept it in another. Are you consistent in your reasoning about God, or do you accept conclusions only when they suit your prior beliefs?
?
Have you ever claimed something is 'obvious' about God to avoid the effort of deeper reflection? What does this passage teach you about intellectual honesty and the need for evidence in matters of faith?

Three Quranic Verses Negating the Vision of Allah
Imam Razi presents three Quranic verses used to argue against the possibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how three Quranic verses are used by opponents of ru'ya to argue that seeing Allah is impossible, and the reasoning behind their interpretation.

Core Idea
Three Quranic verses show that demanding to see Allah was rebuked, implying impossibility.

Imam Razi presents the strongest scriptural arguments against the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. These three verses from the Quran are cited by those who deny ru'ya. Before Razi offers his own response, he lays out the opposing case. In this lesson, we carefully read these verses and their apparent meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the three verses cited: 2:55, 4:153, and 25:21.
Each verse involves a demand to see Allah and a negative consequence.
2
Understand the context of each verse
For each verse, identify who demanded to see Allah and what happened as a result. This context is crucial for the argument.
The first two involve the Israelites asking Moses; the third involves disbelievers in general.
3
Grasp the argument from 'istiz'am'
The passage ends by stating that the Quran's use of 'istiz'am' (deeming it great/arrogant) indicates that ru'ya is impossible. Reflect on how the tone of rebuke implies impossibility.
This is the key scriptural argument against the vision of Allah.
4
Consider the limitation of the argument
Note that Razi will later respond to these verses, arguing they do not prove absolute impossibility but only that such a vision is not fitting in this world or for disbelievers. Keep this nuance in mind.
This passage presents the objection; Razi's response will come in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة عشر: فأخذتكم الصاعقة وأنتم تنظرون [البقرة: 55] وثانيها: قوله تعالى: ويَسْأَلُكَ أَهْلُ الْكِتَابِ أَن تُنَزِّلَ عَلَيْهِمْ كِتَابًا مِّنَ السَّمَاءِ فَقَدْ سَأَلُوا مُوسَىٰ أَكْبَرَ مِن ذَٰلِكَ فَقَالُوا أَرِنَا اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً فَأَخَذَتْهُمُ الصَّاعِقَةُ بِظُلْمِهِمْ [النساء: 153] وثالثها: قوله تعالى: وَقَالَ الَّذِينَ لَا يَرْجُونَ لِقَاءَنَا لَوْلَا أُنزِلَ عَلَيْنَا الْمَلَائِكَةُ أَوْ نَرَىٰ رَبَّنَا لَقَدِ اسْتَكْبَرُوا فِي أَنفُسِهِمْ وَعَتَوْا عُتُوًّا كَبِيرًا [الفرقان: 21] وهذا الاستعظام يدل على أن رؤية الله تعالى ممتنعة.
The nineteenth issue: 'And the thunderbolt took you while you were looking' [2:55] and the second: His statement: 'The People of the Scripture ask you to bring down to them a book from the sky. They asked Moses for greater than that and said, "Show us Allah clearly," and the thunderbolt took them for their wrongdoing' [4:153] and the third: His statement: 'And those who do not expect the meeting with Us say, "Why were not angels sent down to us, or we see our Lord?" They have certainly become arrogant within themselves and became insolent with great insolence' [25:21]. And this deeming it great indicates that the vision of Allah the Exalted is impossible.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents three Quranic verses that the opponents of ru'ya (vision of Allah) use as scriptural proof that Allah cannot be seen. The first verse is from the story of Moses where the people demanded to see Allah and were struck by lightning as a punishment, implying that such a request was inappropriate or impossible. The second verse similarly mentions that the People of the Scripture asked Moses to bring down a book, and Moses reminded them that they had earlier asked to see Allah and were punished. The third verse describes the disbelievers who arrogantly demand to see their Lord, and the Quran condemns their demand as a great insolence. Imam Razi notes that the Quran's use of 'istiz'am' (deeming it great) indicates that seeing Allah is impossible. However, note that Razi will later respond to these objections, arguing that the verses do not prove absolute impossibility but rather that such a vision is not fitting for this world or for disbelievers.

Correction: Imam Razi later argues that these verses only show that such a vision is not granted in this world or that the demand of disbelievers is arrogant; they do not negate the possibility of believers seeing Allah in Paradise.

Distinguishing 'Idrak' from Vision: Razi's First Answer
Razi begins answering the first scriptural doubt by distinguishing 'idrak' from mere vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi resolves the apparent conflict between the verse 'Sight does not perceive Him' and the possibility of seeing Allah by distinguishing the meaning of 'idrak' from 'ru'ya'.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah does not require encompassing Him—'idrak' means enclosure, not vision.

This lesson tackles one of the most common scriptural objections to the possibility of seeing Allah: the verse 'لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ'. Many have assumed that this verse proves vision is impossible. Imam Razi shows that this is based on a misunderstanding of the word 'idrak', which does not mean sight but rather encompassment. By examining the linguistic and rational distinctions, Razi clears the path for affirming the beatific vision while preserving divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
The second aspect is incomplete; focus on the first.
2
Understand the linguistic argument
Razi shows that 'idrak' means 'reaching' or 'encompassing', not mere sight. He gives examples: attaining puberty, fruit ripening, and the Quranic verse 'إِنَّا لَمُدْرَكُونَ' meaning 'overtaken'.
3
Follow the rational implication
If 'idrak' means encompassment, then negating it for Allah means He cannot be encompassed, not that He cannot be seen. Razi analogises: we know Allah without encompassing, so we see Him without encompassing.
This preserves the possibility of vision (ru'ya) while affirming divine transcendence.
4
Consider the second aspect
Razi begins a second argument: if 'idrak' were synonymous with vision, the verse would contradict the claim that sight perceives Him, and the definite article would imply universality. The passage ends mid-sentence; the full argument is not here.
The second aspect is incomplete in this passage.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
The core insight: negation of qualified vision (idrak) does not negate unqualified vision (ru'ya). This distinction allows the Ash'ari position on seeing Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى وهي تمسكهم بقوله تعالى: {لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ} [الأنعام: 103] من وجوه: الأول: لا نسلم أن الإدراك عبارة عن الرؤية بل هو عبارة عن الوصول، يقال: أدرك الغلام إذا صار بالغا وأدركت الثمرة إذا وصلت إلى حد النضج. قال تعالى: {إِنَّا لَمُدْرَكُونَ} [الشعراء: 61] أي لملحقون. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إن من رأى شيئا ورأى أطرافه ونهاياته، قيل: إنه أدركه - على تقدير أن يكون قد أحاط به من جملة جوانيه - وهذا المعنى إنما يتحقق في الشيء الذي له أطراف ونهايات والباري تعالى منزه عن ذلك. فلم تكن رؤيته إدراكا البتة. فلم يلزم من نفي الإدراك نفي الرؤية. فالحاصل أن الإدراك رؤية مكيفة ولا يلزم من نفي الرؤية المكيفة، نفي أصل الرؤية. وكما أنا نعرف الله ولا نحيط به. فكذلك نراه ولا ندركه. الوجه الثاني في الجواب: هب أن إدراك العين عبارة عن الرؤية، إلا أن قوله تعالى: {لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ} نقيض لقولنا: تدركه الأبصار وقولنا: تدركه الأبصار يقتضي أن يدركه كل أحد لأن الألف واللام إذا دخلا على
And the answer to the first doubt, which is their clinging to His statement: {Sight does not perceive Him} [6:103], is from several aspects: First: We do not accept that 'idrak' means vision; rather it means 'reaching.' It is said: 'adraka al-ghulam' when he reached puberty, and 'adrakat al-thamara' when it reached the stage of ripeness. Allah said: {Indeed we are overtaken} [26:61], i.e., we are caught up. Once you know this, we say: Whoever sees a thing and sees its edges and extremities, it is said that he 'idraka' it—on the assumption that he encompasses it from all its sides. This meaning is only realized for a thing that has edges and extremities, and the Exalted Creator is transcendent above that. So His vision is not 'idrak' at all. Therefore, the negation of 'idrak' does not necessitate negation of vision. In summary, 'idrak' is qualified vision, and negation of qualified vision does not necessitate negation of vision itself. Just as we know Allah but do not encompass Him, so we see Him but do not perceive Him. The second aspect of the answer: Assuming that 'idrak' of the eye means vision, except that His statement {Sight does not perceive Him} is the contradictory of our statement 'sight perceives Him', and our statement 'sight perceives Him' would require that everyone perceive Him because the definite article when it enters
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage begins the response to the first scriptural doubt, the verse 'Sight does not perceive Him' (لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ). The first aspect argues that 'idrak' (perception) does not mean mere vision but rather 'reaching' or 'encompassing'. Linguistic evidence is given from Arabic usage (attaining puberty, ripening fruit, and the Quranic verse 'إِنَّا لَمُدْرَكُونَ' meaning overtaken). Thus, the verse negates that Allah can be encompassed by sight, not that He is not seen at all. Razi analogizes: just as we know Allah without encompassing His essence, so we may see Him without encompassing His whole being. The second aspect (incomplete) suggests that if 'idrak' were synonymous with vision, the verse's negation would be contradictory to the claim that sight perceives Him, and the definite article would imply universality.

Correction: Razi clarifies that 'idrak' does not mean vision but encompassment, so the verse only negates encompassing Allah, not seeing Him.

Defining the Point of Dispute and Rejecting Necessary Impossibility
Razi defines the point of dispute and rejects the claim of necessary impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi sets the terms of the debate on ru'ya and why the claim that Allah cannot be seen is not self-evident.

Core Idea
The possibility of seeing Allah is not settled by intuition; it requires rational proof.

Before engaging with the Mu'tazila's arguments, Razi carefully defines what is actually being disputed: whether an existent that transcends place and direction can be seen. He then dismisses any claim that the impossibility of seeing Him is known necessarily, because that would make the debate pointless. This lesson clarifies the starting point of the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the point of dispute.
Razi states that proofs must be preceded by specifying the point of dispute. Find what he identifies as the core question.
He says محل النزاع: الموجود المنزه عن المكان والجهة. هل تجوز رؤيته؟
2
Understand the rejection of necessary impossibility.
The Mu'tazila might claim that it is self-evident that Allah cannot be seen. Razi labels this false, implying that it is not a necessary truth but requires demonstration.
If it were necessary, there would be no need for argument, but the issue is debated, so it is inferential.
3
Recognize the implications.
By clarifying that the impossibility is not self-evident, Razi opens the door to rational proofs for the possibility of ru'ya.
This sets the stage for the following refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أنا بينا في المقدمة أن ذكر الدلائل لابد أن يكون مسبوقا بتعيين محل النزاع فنقول : محل النزاع أن الموجود المنزه عن المكان والجهة. هل تجوز واينه أم لا ؟
فإن ادعيتم أن العلم بامتناع رؤيته ضروري، فذلك باطل - على ما بيناه في المقدمة - وإن ادعيتم أن هذا العلم استدلالي فلا بد فيه من الدليل وقولكم : " إن كل مرئي، لا بد وأن يكون مقابلاه يقرب من أنه إعادة للدعوى لأن المقابل هو الذي يكون مختصا بجهة قدام الرائي فكأنكم قلتم : الدليل على أن ما لا يكون في الجهة لا يكون مرئيا. هو أن كل ما كان مرئيا في جهة والمنطقيون يسمون هذه القضية الثانية عكس نقيض القضية الأولى.
وفي الحقيقة لا فرق بين القضيتين في الظهور والخفاء فلم يجز جعل أحدهما حجة في صحة الأخرى، بل يقرب هذا من أن يكون إعادة للمطلوب بعبارة أخرى. والوجه الثاني في الجواب : ثبت أن المقابلة شرط للرؤية في الشاهد فلم قلتم : إنه في الغائب كذلك؟ وتقريره ما ذكرناه في الجواب عن الشبهة الأولى. وتمام الكشف والتحقيق أنا ذكرنا في المقدمات : أن المراد من الرؤية أن يحصل لنا انکشاف بالنسبة إلى ذاته المخصوصة، وهو يجري مجرى الانكشاف الحاصل عند إبصار الألوان والأضواء، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فإذن الانكشاف يجب أن يكون على وفق المكشوف، فإن كان المكشوف مخصوصا بالجهة والحيز وجب أن يكون الانكشاف كذلك. وإن كان المكشوف منزها عن الجهة وجب أن يكون انكشافه منزها عن الحيز والجهة . وأما الشبهة الثالثة : فجوابها أيضا على هذا القانون. فالرؤية عبارة عن الانكشاف التام فإن كان الشيء له صورة كان انكشافه بانكشاف صورته ولونه، وإن كان منزها عن الصورة واللون كان انكشافه كذلك أيضا لأن شرط الانكشاف أن يحصل على وفق ماهية المكشوف
وهذا هو الجواب بعينه عن الشبهة الرابعة.
واعلم أن من تأمل في هذه الكلمات على سبيل الإنصاف، علم قطعا أنه ليس للمعتزلة في نفي الرؤية شبهة تحيله البتة. وبالله التوفيق.
First: We explained in the introduction that the mention of proofs must be preceded by specifying the point of dispute. So we say: The point of dispute is: Is it permissible to see the existent that is transcendent of place and direction, or not? If you claim that knowledge of the impossibility of seeing Him is necessary, that is false—as we explained in the introduction—and if you claim that this knowledge is inferential, then a proof is necessary for it. Your statement: 'Every visible object must be in front (muqābil)' is close to restating the claim, because 'in front' is what is specific to the direction in front of the seer, so it is as if you said: The proof that what is not in a direction is not visible is that everything visible is in a direction. The logicians call this second proposition the contrapositive of the first proposition. In reality, there is no difference between the two propositions in clarity and obscurity, so it is not permissible to make one a proof for the validity of the other; rather, this is close to restating the desired conclusion in other words. The second answer: It is established that being in front (muqābala) is a condition for vision in the witnessed world; so why do you say it is likewise for the Unseen? Its explanation is what we mentioned in the answer to the first objection. The complete disclosure and verification: We mentioned in the introductions that what is meant by vision (ru'ya) is that disclosure (inkishāf) occurs to us with respect to His specific essence (dhāt al-makhṣūṣa), and it is analogous to the disclosure that occurs when seeing colors and lights. If this is so, then disclosure must correspond to the disclosed object. If the disclosed object is specific to direction and space, then disclosure must likewise be so. And if the disclosed object is transcendent of direction, then its disclosure must be transcendent of space and direction. As for the third objection: its answer is also based on this principle. Vision is complete disclosure. If the thing has a form (ṣūra), its disclosure is through disclosure of its form and color. And if it is transcendent of form and color, then its disclosure is likewise, because the condition of disclosure is that it occurs according to the quiddity (māhiyya) of the disclosed object. This is the same answer for the fourth objection. Know that whoever reflects on these words with fairness will know with certainty that the Mu'tazila have no objection to negating vision that is at all valid. By Allah is success.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's objections to the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The author proceeds in order: First, he defines the point of dispute: whether the existent transcendent of place and direction can be seen. Then he addresses the claim that the impossibility of seeing Him is known necessarily, stating it is false as explained earlier. He then critiques the opponents' argument that every visible must be in front (muqābil) by showing it is circular—the second proposition is merely the contrapositive of the claim, so it grants no new knowledge. The second answer argues that while being in front is a condition for vision in the witnessed world, this does not apply to the Unseen (ghā'ib). The author then presents the key principle: vision is disclosure (inkishāf), and disclosure must correspond to the nature of the disclosed object. If the object is spatial, disclosure is spatial; if transcendent, disclosure is transcendent. This principle is applied to the third and fourth objections: vision as complete disclosure adapts to whether the object has form and color or is beyond them. The passage concludes that the Mu'tazila have no valid objection at all.

Key Terms
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That it is self-evident (بديهي) that Allah cannot be seen, requiring no proof.
Correction: Razi states that this claim is false; it is not a necessary truth but a disputed issue requiring rational demonstration.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the point of dispute (محل النزاع) in the discussion of ru'ya?
A) Whether Allah is visible with physical eyes
B) Whether an existent transcendent of place and direction can be seen
C) Whether vision requires direction
D) Whether the Mu'tazila are correct

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing Allah is a rational question, not a self-evident truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed something about Allah without evidence, just because it seemed obvious? How does Razi's insistence on rational proof challenge you to examine your own beliefs, even those you consider self-evident?
?
Are there areas of your faith where you rely on 'common sense' instead of seeking deeper understanding? What would it take for you to change that—and are you willing to do it?
?
When you face a theological disagreement, do you first clarify the exact point of contention, as Razi does? How might your discussions change if you followed this method, and what is preventing you from doing so?
