Refuting the Circular Argument of the Mu'tazila (Objection 2)
Razi refutes the second objection by exposing its circular reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi exposes the circular reasoning behind the Mu'tazila's claim that every visible must be in front (muqābila).

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's argument that Allah cannot be seen because He is not in a direction is a restatement of the claim, not a proof.

After setting the terms, Razi turns to the Mu'tazila's first inferential argument: that every visible object must be in front of the seer, and since Allah is not in a direction, He cannot be seen. Razi shows that this argument is logically circular—it merely restates the conclusion in different words. This lesson explores his incisive critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's argument as presented by Razi.
The Mu'tazila claim: كل مرئي لا بد وأن يكون مقابلاه. Identify the premise and conclusion.
They argue that visibility requires being in front, so Allah, being beyond direction, is not visible.
2
Understand Razi's logical analysis.
Razi points out that the statement 'every visible is in front' is logically equivalent to its contrapositive 'what is not in front is not visible'. He argues that the Mu'tazila are essentially using the latter to prove the former, which is circular.
The logicians call this عكس نقيض.
3
Appreciate the refutation.
Razi concludes that there is no real difference between the two propositions in clarity, so one cannot serve as evidence for the other. This undermines the objection.
This critique shows the weakness of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what logical error does the Mu'tazila commit in their second objection?
A) They confuse necessary and contingent
B) They use a self-evident premise
C) Their argument is circular (restates the conclusion)
D) They assume visibility requires color

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's argument that Allah cannot be seen because He is not in a direction is a circular restatement, not a valid proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted an argument about Allah simply because it seemed to follow a logical structure, without verifying that the premises are true? Where in your own thinking might you be guilty of assuming what you are trying to prove?
?
This lesson shows that even intelligent scholars can be misled by circular reasoning. In what area of your faith—such as worship, ethics, or theology—are you tempted to rely on assumptions that you have not critically examined? Will you take a step today to question one such assumption?
?
If the Mu'tazila had admitted that their argument was circular, would it change your view on the possibility of seeing Allah? What does your reaction reveal about whether you approach such matters with intellectual humility or pride?

The Principle of Disclosure and Its Application (Objections 2-4)
Razi presents the principle of disclosure and applies it to objections 2-4.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's key principle that vision is disclosure (inkishāf) and that disclosure corresponds to the nature of the disclosed object, which refutes the remaining objections.

Core Idea
Vision is disclosure, and disclosure mirrors the object—spatial objects yield spatial disclosure, transcendent objects yield transcendent disclosure.

Having dismantled the circular argument, Razi offers a second, deeper answer to the Mu'tazila's objections. He proposes that vision is essentially a form of disclosure (inkishāf) that corresponds to the quiddity of the object. This principle not only answers the second objection but also the third and fourth, by showing that for a transcendent being, vision is also transcendent. This lesson explores this profound metaphysical concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer.
Razi argues that even if being in front is a condition for vision in the physical world, we cannot assume it applies to the Unseen (ghā'ib).
This is a common Ash'ari move: لا يلزم من ثبوت الحكم في الشاهد ثبوته في الغائب.
2
Understand the disclosure principle.
Razi defines vision as disclosure (inkishāf) that occurs to us regarding His specific essence. He likens it to seeing colors and lights.
The key point: disclosure must be according to the disclosed object (على وفق المكشوف).
3
Apply to the third and fourth objections.
The third objection concerns vision requiring form/color. Razi says if the object is transcendent of form and color, disclosure is likewise transcendent. The fourth objection is similar.
This unified principle refutes multiple objections.
4
Reflect on the conclusion.
Razi concludes that anyone who reflects fairly will see that the Mu'tazila have no valid objection at all.
This seals the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the condition for disclosure (inkishāf)?
A) It must occur in a specific direction
B) It must reveal form and color
C) It must correspond to the nature of the disclosed object
D) It must involve physical eyes

Key Takeaway
Razi's principle that disclosure corresponds to the object's nature elegantly refutes multiple Mu'tazilite objections, establishing the rational possibility of seeing Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine ‘seeing Allah’ on the Day of Judgment, do you subconsciously picture it like seeing a physical object? What does that reveal about how your heart still tends to measure the Creator by the standards of creation? Will you commit to training your mind to accept a disclosure beyond imagination, even if it feels abstract?
?
Razi states that disclosure corresponds to the object’s nature. If you approached your knowledge of Allah—His names, His attributes, His actions—with this principle, would you realize how often you impose your own limited categories onto Him? What is one specific assumption about Allah that you now see might be shaped by your experience of created things?
?
Razi ends with certainty that the Mu'tazila have no valid objection. But do you have any lingering doubt in your heart that perhaps a physical direction or form is somehow required for Allah to be seen? Confess that doubt. Are you willing to stand before Allah on the Day of Resurrection with that doubt, or will you uproot it now by embracing the principle of disclosure?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
Why Allah's Essence Cannot Be Conceived
Razi provides the second proof that Allah's essence cannot be conceived due to lack of direct perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that Allah's essence cannot be conceived by the human intellect, and the two exclusive avenues of conception.

Core Idea
Conception requires direct perception or sensation; Allah transcends both, so He cannot be conceived.

How can we know something if we cannot even conceive it? Imam Razi begins with a logical principle: before we can affirm anything about something, we must have a conception of it. He then restricts the possible ways to form a conception to two: internal necessary perception and external sensory perception. Applying this to Allah, he concludes that since Allah does not fall into either category, His reality remains beyond our intellectual grasp.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical premise
Read the first sentence: 'Affirmation (tasdiq) is a branch of conception (tasawwur).' This means we cannot affirm or deny anything about something we have no concept of. This is the foundation of the argument.
Notice how the argument builds on this simple logical rule.
2
Identify the two avenues of conception
Razi lists two ways we can conceive a quiddity: (1) necessary internal perception, like pain or joy, and (2) external sensory perception via the five senses, like seeing colors or hearing sounds.
These are the only two ways according to Razi; no third option is given.
3
Apply the rule to Allah
Ask: Does Allah fall under either category? He is not an internal feeling or emotion, nor is He a physical object perceived by the senses. Therefore, neither avenue applies.
This step requires careful reflection on the nature of Allah.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since both avenues are absent, Razi concludes that Allah's reality is not conceived by intellects. This is the core of the second proof.
The conclusion is necessary given the premises.
5
Reflect on theological implications
Think about how this argument supports divine transcendence (tanzih). If we cannot conceive Allah's essence, then any mental image we form is inadequate.
This connects to broader discussions in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: من البين أن التصديق فرع التصور، فما لا نتصور حقيقته لا يمكننا أن نعلم أنها حاصلة أم غير حاصلة. ونحن لا يمكننا أن نتصور حقيقته إلا إذا أدركناها من أنفسنا إدراكاً ضرورياً كالعلم بالألم واللذة والفرح والغم والغضب، وأدركناها بإحدى الحواس الخمس كالعلم بالألوان فإنه حصل من الإبصار، والعلم بالأصوات فإنه حصل بالسماع، وكذا القول في بقية المحسوسات. وأما الماهية التي ما أدركناها بواحد من هذين الطريقين فإننا نعلم أنها غير متصورة. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: حقيقة الله وكنه ماهيته غير مدركة بأحد هذين الطريقين، فوجب أن لا تكون متصورة عند العقول.
The second proof: It is evident that affirmation (tasdiq) is a branch of conception (tasawwur); so whatever we do not conceive its reality, we cannot know whether it is obtained or not. And we cannot conceive His reality except if we perceive it from ourselves by necessary perception, like knowledge of pain, pleasure, joy, grief, and anger, or we perceive it by one of the five senses, like knowledge of colors acquired through sight, and knowledge of sounds acquired through hearing, and likewise for the rest of the sensible things. As for the quiddity (mahiyya) which we have not perceived by either of these two ways, we know that it is not conceived. If you have understood this, we say: The reality of Allah and the essence of His quiddity are not perceived by either of these two ways, so it necessarily follows that they are not conceived by intellects.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that Allah's essence is unknowable, focusing on the impossibility of conception. The author begins by establishing a logical principle: affirmation (tasdiq) is a branch of conception (tasawwur). That is, before we can affirm or deny anything about something, we must have a conception of it. Therefore, if we cannot conceive Allah's reality, we cannot make any judgment about it. He then explains that conception occurs through two avenues: (1) necessary internal perception, such as direct awareness of pain, pleasure, emotions; and (2) sensory perception via the five external senses, such as sight for colors and hearing for sounds. Any quiddity not accessed through either of these two avenues cannot be conceived. Applying this to Allah, His reality and essence are not perceived internally (since He is not a feeling or emotion) nor externally (since He is not a physical object). Therefore, His reality cannot be conceived by intellects. This argument emphasizes the absolute transcendence of Allah beyond human cognitive faculties.

Key Terms
تصديق
تصور
ماهية
إدراك ضروري
حواس خمس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That conception of Allah is possible through abstract reasoning or intellectual deduction alone.
Correction: Razi insists that conception requires direct internal perception or sensory perception. Abstract reasoning does not yield a true conception of a quiddity.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, how can we conceive a quiddity?
A) Through logical deduction alone
B) Through necessary internal perception or one of the five senses
C) Through revelation and scripture
D) Through accumulated knowledge from teachers

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that because conception requires direct internal or sensory perception, and Allah is beyond both, His reality is inconceivable by the intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to imagine Allah in your mind, what are you actually imagining? How does this passage expose that image as a product of your limited perception rather than His reality?
?
If your intellect cannot conceive Allah's essence, what does that mean for the way you pray and depend on Him? Are you willing to trust a God whose reality you cannot grasp?
?
Have you ever assumed that your understanding of Allah's attributes gives you knowledge of His essence? How does Razi's argument demand you reconsider the limits of your knowledge?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
8 min
Objections to Unknowability and Start of Answer
Razi presents two objections and begins his rebuttal with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main objections against the unknowability of Allah's essence and the beginning of Razi's rebuttal using the analogy of properties of foods and medicines.

Core Idea
Knowing attributes does not require knowing the essence itself.

In the previous lesson, we learned that Allah's essence is ultimately unknowable. Now, Razi presents two strong objections from opponents who argue that we do know the essence. They claim that if we can judge the essence to be unknown, or attribute positive qualities to it, then we must have some conception of it. Razi begins his answer by pointing to a simple analogy: we know the properties of foods and medicines without knowing their true quiddities. This lesson sets the stage for a deeper resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the two proofs offered by the opponents. Notice how each proof builds on the relationship between conception and judgment.
The first proof uses the principle that affirmation (tasdiq) is preceded by conception (tasawwur).
2
Identify the structure of the arguments
Each objection follows a conditional logic: if X were not known, then Y would be impossible. Identify the condition and the consequence in each case.
The second objection lists several positive attributes that we predicate of Allah.
3
Examine the beginning of Razi's answer
Razi says both proofs are refuted by the example of properties of foods and medicines. Ask yourself: how does this example show that one can know attributes without knowing the essence?
The answer is cut off; we will continue in the next lesson.
4
Reflect on the analogy
Think about foods and medicines: we know their effects (e.g., honey is sweet, medicine heals) but we may not know their essences. How does this parallel knowledge of Allah's attributes?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بأن تلك الحقيقة معلومة بوجهين: الحجة الأولى: أن التصديق مسبوق بالتصور، ولو لم تكن تلك الحقيقة معلومة، لامتنع الحكم عليها بأنها غير معلومة. الحجة الثانية: أنا نحكم على تلك الذات المخصوصة بأنها موصوفة بالوجود والقدم والدوام والوجوب والوحدة وصفات الجلال والإكرام، ولولا أن تلك الحقيقة من حيث هي هي معلومة وإلا لما أمكن الحكم عليها بهذه الصفات. والجواب: تنتقض هاتان الحجتان بخواص الأغذية والأدوية، فإنها من حيث
And those who claim that that reality is known argued with two aspects: The first proof: That affirmation is preceded by conception, and if that reality were not known, it would be impossible to judge that it is unknown. The second proof: We judge that that specific essence is described by existence, eternity, perpetuity, necessity, oneness, and the attributes of majesty and honor. And if that reality in itself were not known, it would not be possible to judge it with these attributes. The answer: Both these proofs are refuted by the properties of foods and medicines, for they inasmuch as...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents the objections of those who claim Allah's essence is known, followed by the beginning of Razi's answer. The opponents offer two arguments. First, they use the same principle Razi used earlier: affirmation is preceded by conception. They argue that if the essence were not known, we could not even say it is unknown, because that would require a conception of it. Second, they argue that we do in fact predicate positive attributes to Allah—such as existence, eternity, oneness, etc.—and such predication requires knowledge of the essence. If we did not know the essence, we could not apply those attributes to it. Razi begins his answer by stating that these arguments are refuted by the example of the properties of foods and medicines. The answer is incomplete here, but the analogy likely means that we can know the effects and properties of foods and medicines without knowing their specific quiddities, similar to how we know Allah's attributes without knowing His essence.

Key Terms
تصديق
تصور
حكم
صفات الجلال والإكرام
خواص الأغذية والأدوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we can predicate attributes to Allah, then we must have direct knowledge of His essence.
Correction: Razi argues that we can know attributes without knowing the essence, just as we know the properties of foods and medicines without knowing their quiddities.

Checkpoint
What is the logical principle used in the first objection?
A) Knowledge is infinite
B) Affirmation is preceded by conception
C) Attributes are identical to the essence
D) The unknown cannot be spoken of

Key Takeaway
Razi counters the claim that knowing attributes implies knowing the essence by using the analogy of properties of foods and medicines, a theme he will develop further.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I know Allah,' do you mean you know His attributes and actions, or do you feel compelled to imagine His essence? How does this lesson free you from that burden?
?
The opponents argued that you cannot judge a thing as unknown without some knowledge of it. Yet Razi says you can know properties without knowing the quiddity. How does this apply to your relationship with Allah? Are you content to know Him through what He has revealed about Himself, or do you crave an impossible grasp of His essence?
?
This lesson shows that we can benefit from things (like medicines) without knowing their true nature. How does that change your trust in Allah's names and attributes? Will you now rely on them more, even when you cannot fully comprehend His essence?

Divine Transcendence

The Magnet Analogy: Knowing Effects, Not Essence
First proof: analogy of the magnet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that God's essence is unknowable, based on the analogy of the magnet.

Core Idea
We know God's effects, not His specific essence.

Imam Razi begins with a concrete analogy: we observe the magnet's effect but cannot perceive its specific reality. Similarly, from God's attributes we deduce His distinct reality, but that reality itself remains unknown. This lesson unpacks the logic of that first proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the magnet analogy
Read the Arabic and translation of the first proof. Notice how Razi uses the magnet example to illustrate the distinction between knowing an effect and knowing the essence.
The key phrase is 'أما أن نعلم تلك الحقيقة بعينها، فهذا غير حاصل'.
2
Identify the structure
The proof has two parts: (1) from the effect we know there is a distinct reality, (2) but we cannot know that reality itself. See how he applies this to God.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that knowledge of God's specific reality is not attained. Why does Razi consider this knowledge necessary?
The phrase 'والعلم به ضروري' indicates it is a necessary truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل تعين تلك الحقيقة فهذا غير حاصل وهذا كما أنا لما شاهدنا الأثر المخصوص عن المغناطيس. قلنا : إن له حقيقة مخصوصة مميزة عن سائر الحقائق، فأما أن نعلم تلك الحقيقة بعينها، فهذا غير حاصل. فكذا ههنا لما علمنا اختصاص ذاته بهذه الصفات على أصل الوجوب واللزوم، علمنا أن له حقيقة متميزة عن سائر الحقائق، فأما أن نعلم من هذه الصفات تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة بعينها، فهذا غير حاصل. والعلم به ضروري. فعلمنا أن العلم بحقيقته المخصوصة غير حاصل. الحجة الثانية من البين أن التصديق فرع التصور فما لا نتصور حقيقته لا يمكننا أن نعلم أنها حاصلة أم غير حاصلة. ونحن لا يمكننا أن نتصور حقيقته إلا إذا أدركناها من أنفسنا إدراكا ضروريا كالعلم بالألم واللذة والفرح والغم والغضب، وأدركناها بإحدى الحواس الخمس كالعلم بالألوان فإنه حصل من الإبصار، والعلم بالأصوات فإنه حصل بالسماع وكذا القول في بقية المحسوسات. وأما الماهية التي ما أدركناها بواحد من هذين الطريقين فإننا نعلم أنها غير متصورة. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : حقيقة الله وكنه ماهيته غير مدركة بأحد هذين الطريقين، فوجب أن لا تكون متصورة عند العقول. الحجة الثالثة : أن كل ما نعلمه منه سبحانه وتعالى فإن مجرد تصوره غير ما تبين من وقوع الشركة فيه : ولذلك فإنا بعد معرفة هذه الصفات نفتقر إلى إقامة الدلائل على أنه سبحانه وتعالى واحد وأما ذاته المخصوصة فإنها من حيث إنها هي الشركة. وإذا كان كذلك، وجب القطع بأنه من حيث إنه هو غير معلوم للبشر. وهذا قياس جلي من الشكل الثاني. واحتج القائلون بأن تلك الحقيقة معلومة بوجهين الحجة الأولى أن التصديق مسبوق بالتصور، ولو لم تكن تلك الحقيقة معلومة، لامتنع الحكم عليها بأنها غير معلومة. الحجة الثانية : أنا نحكم على تلك الذات المخصوصة بأنها موصوفة بالوجود والقدم والدوام والوجوب والوحدة وصفات الجلال والإكرام، ولولا أن تلك الحقيقة من حيث هي هي معلومة وإلا لما أمكن الحكم عليها بهذه الصفات. والجواب : تنتقض هاتان الحجتان بخواص الأغذية والأدوية، فإنها من حيث هي هي مجهولة العلم بكونها مستلزمة للآثار المخصوصة. وكذا ههنا. واعلم أن هذا البحث ينتج إشكالات عظيمة، لا يليق ذكرها بهذه المواضع.
The intellect [determining] that specific essence is not attained. This is like when we observe the specific effect of the magnet. We say: it has a specific reality distinct from other realities. But as for knowing that reality itself, that is not attained. Likewise here, when we know the particularity of His essence with these attributes on the basis of necessity and permanence, we know that He has a reality distinct from other realities. But as for knowing from these attributes that specific reality itself, that is not attained. And knowledge of it is necessary. So we know that knowledge of His specific reality is not attained. The second proof: It is evident that assent is a branch of conception. So what we cannot conceive of its reality cannot be known whether it exists or not. And we cannot conceive of His reality unless we perceive it from ourselves by necessary perception, like knowledge of pain, pleasure, joy, sorrow, and anger, or perceive it by one of the five senses, like knowledge of colors obtained by sight, and knowledge of sounds obtained by hearing, and likewise for the rest of the sense-objects. As for the quiddity which we have not perceived by either of these two ways, we know that it is inconceivable. Once you know this, we say: The reality of God and the quiddity of His essence are not perceived by either of these two ways, so it must be that they are inconceivable to the intellects. The third proof: Everything we know of Him—exalted is He—its mere conception is other than what has been shown concerning the occurrence of commonality in it. Therefore, after knowing these attributes, we need to establish proofs that He—exalted is He—is one. But His specific essence, inasmuch as it is He, is the commonality. If this is so, it must be concluded that inasmuch as He is He, He is unknown to humans. This is a clear syllogism of the second figure. Those who claim that that reality is known argued with two points: The first argument: Assent is preceded by conception. If that reality were not known, it would be impossible to judge that it is unknown. The second argument: We judge that specific essence as being described by existence, pre-eternity, perpetuity, necessity, oneness, and attributes of majesty and honor. And were that reality, inasmuch as it is itself, not known, it would not be possible to judge it with these attributes. The answer: Both of these arguments are refuted by the properties of foods and medicines, for inasmuch as they are themselves, they are unknown, [while] knowledge of their being productive of specific effects [is known]. The same applies here. Know that this investigation produces weighty difficulties, which it is not fitting to mention in these places.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs that the essence (ḥaqīqa) of God is unknowable to human intellects, followed by two objections from opponents and an answer. The first proof uses analogy with the magnet: we know the effect but not the specific reality causing it; similarly, we know God's attributes but not His specific essence. The second proof relies on the principle that assent (taṣdīq) depends on prior conception (taṣawwur). Conception occurs either by direct inner awareness (like pain) or by external senses. Since God's essence is not perceived in either way, it cannot be conceived, hence it is unknown. The third proof argues that all we know of God (attributes) are common categories, not the unique essence itself; thus the specific essence remains unknown. This is a syllogism of the second figure. Then two objections are presented: (1) if the essence were unknown, we could not judge it as unknown, since judgment requires conception; (2) we do predicate attributes like existence and pre-eternity to the essence, implying it is known. The answer refutes both by noting that the properties of foods and medicines are unknown in themselves, yet we know their effects; likewise, God's essence is unknown in itself but known through its effects. Razi closes by noting that deeper issues arise but are not addressed here.

Key Terms
حقيقة
أثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the magnet analogy, what do we know about God?
A) We know His essence directly through reason.
B) We know His attributes but not His specific essence.
C) We know nothing about God at all.
D) We know His essence through the senses.

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that we can know God's attributes but not His specific essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that even after knowing God's attributes, His essence remains utterly unknown. When you make supplication or reflect on God, have you ever paused to admit that you are addressing a reality you cannot grasp? What would change in your worship if you truly lived with this awareness—not of what you know about God, but of what you cannot know?
?
Razi uses the magnet to show that effects tell us something exists without telling us what it is. Consider: what are the 'effects' of God in your life? How often do you mistake the experience of these effects—like mercy, provision, guidance—for a direct knowledge of His essence? How might you guard your heart against presuming to have 'figured God out'?
?
The passage ends by calling this truth 'necessary'—meaning it is not optional to accept. Can you honestly say that your inner posture toward God reflects gratitude for what He has revealed and humility before what He has concealed? If not, what is one concrete step you can take today to honor that boundary?

Conception and Assent: The Second Proof
Second proof: conception-assent connection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that God's essence is unknowable, based on the principle that assent depends on conception.

Core Idea
Assent requires conception; God's essence cannot be conceived.

Imam Razi's second proof builds on a fundamental epistemological principle: to affirm something (assent) we must first have a concept of it (conception). Since God's essence is not perceived through inner sense or external senses, it cannot be conceived, and thus remains unknown. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Read the opening statement: 'التصديق فرع التصور'. This is the key premise. Make sure you grasp what 'tasdiq' and 'tasawwur' mean.
Assent is the act of affirming a proposition; conception is forming a mental representation.
2
Identify the two sources of conception
Razi lists two ways we conceive things: inner perception (like pain) and external senses (like sight). Find these in the text.
The Arabic says 'إدراكا ضروريا' and 'بإحدى الحواس الخمس'.
3
Apply to God
Since God's essence is not perceived in either way, it cannot be conceived. Therefore, we cannot have assent about it (i.e., we cannot know it).
The conclusion: 'فوجب أن لا تكون متصورة عند العقول'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل تعين تلك الحقيقة فهذا غير حاصل وهذا كما أنا لما شاهدنا الأثر المخصوص عن المغناطيس. قلنا : إن له حقيقة مخصوصة مميزة عن سائر الحقائق، فأما أن نعلم تلك الحقيقة بعينها، فهذا غير حاصل. فكذا ههنا لما علمنا اختصاص ذاته بهذه الصفات على أصل الوجوب واللزوم، علمنا أن له حقيقة متميزة عن سائر الحقائق، فأما أن نعلم من هذه الصفات تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة بعينها، فهذا غير حاصل. والعلم به ضروري. فعلمنا أن العلم بحقيقته المخصوصة غير حاصل. الحجة الثانية من البين أن التصديق فرع التصور فما لا نتصور حقيقته لا يمكننا أن نعلم أنها حاصلة أم غير حاصلة. ونحن لا يمكننا أن نتصور حقيقته إلا إذا أدركناها من أنفسنا إدراكا ضروريا كالعلم بالألم واللذة والفرح والغم والغضب، وأدركناها بإحدى الحواس الخمس كالعلم بالألوان فإنه حصل من الإبصار، والعلم بالأصوات فإنه حصل بالسماع وكذا القول في بقية المحسوسات. وأما الماهية التي ما أدركناها بواحد من هذين الطريقين فإننا نعلم أنها غير متصورة. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : حقيقة الله وكنه ماهيته غير مدركة بأحد هذين الطريقين، فوجب أن لا تكون متصورة عند العقول. الحجة الثالثة : أن كل ما نعلمه منه سبحانه وتعالى فإن مجرد تصوره غير ما تبين من وقوع الشركة فيه : ولذلك فإنا بعد معرفة هذه الصفات نفتقر إلى إقامة الدلائل على أنه سبحانه وتعالى واحد وأما ذاته المخصوصة فإنها من حيث إنها هي الشركة. وإذا كان كذلك، وجب القطع بأنه من حيث إنه هو غير معلوم للبشر. وهذا قياس جلي من الشكل الثاني. واحتج القائلون بأن تلك الحقيقة معلومة بوجهين الحجة الأولى أن التصديق مسبوق بالتصور، ولو لم تكن تلك الحقيقة معلومة، لامتنع الحكم عليها بأنها غير معلومة. الحجة الثانية : أنا نحكم على تلك الذات المخصوصة بأنها موصوفة بالوجود والقدم والدوام والوجوب والوحدة وصفات الجلال والإكرام، ولولا أن تلك الحقيقة من حيث هي هي معلومة وإلا لما أمكن الحكم عليها بهذه الصفات. والجواب : تنتقض هاتان الحجتان بخواص الأغذية والأدوية، فإنها من حيث هي هي مجهولة العلم بكونها مستلزمة للآثار المخصوصة. وكذا ههنا. واعلم أن هذا البحث ينتج إشكالات عظيمة، لا يليق ذكرها بهذه المواضع.
The intellect [determining] that specific essence is not attained. This is like when we observe the specific effect of the magnet. We say: it has a specific reality distinct from other realities. But as for knowing that reality itself, that is not attained. Likewise here, when we know the particularity of His essence with these attributes on the basis of necessity and permanence, we know that He has a reality distinct from other realities. But as for knowing from these attributes that specific reality itself, that is not attained. And knowledge of it is necessary. So we know that knowledge of His specific reality is not attained. The second proof: It is evident that assent is a branch of conception. So what we cannot conceive of its reality cannot be known whether it exists or not. And we cannot conceive of His reality unless we perceive it from ourselves by necessary perception, like knowledge of pain, pleasure, joy, sorrow, and anger, or perceive it by one of the five senses, like knowledge of colors obtained by sight, and knowledge of sounds obtained by hearing, and likewise for the rest of the sense-objects. As for the quiddity which we have not perceived by either of these two ways, we know that it is inconceivable. Once you know this, we say: The reality of God and the quiddity of His essence are not perceived by either of these two ways, so it must be that they are inconceivable to the intellects. The third proof: Everything we know of Him—exalted is He—its mere conception is other than what has been shown concerning the occurrence of commonality in it. Therefore, after knowing these attributes, we need to establish proofs that He—exalted is He—is one. But His specific essence, inasmuch as it is He, is the commonality. If this is so, it must be concluded that inasmuch as He is He, He is unknown to humans. This is a clear syllogism of the second figure. Those who claim that that reality is known argued with two points: The first argument: Assent is preceded by conception. If that reality were not known, it would be impossible to judge that it is unknown. The second argument: We judge that specific essence as being described by existence, pre-eternity, perpetuity, necessity, oneness, and attributes of majesty and honor. And were that reality, inasmuch as it is itself, not known, it would not be possible to judge it with these attributes. The answer: Both of these arguments are refuted by the properties of foods and medicines, for inasmuch as they are themselves, they are unknown, [while] knowledge of their being productive of specific effects [is known]. The same applies here. Know that this investigation produces weighty difficulties, which it is not fitting to mention in these places.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs that the essence (ḥaqīqa) of God is unknowable to human intellects, followed by two objections from opponents and an answer. The first proof uses analogy with the magnet: we know the effect but not the specific reality causing it; similarly, we know God's attributes but not His specific essence. The second proof relies on the principle that assent (taṣdīq) depends on prior conception (taṣawwur). Conception occurs either by direct inner awareness (like pain) or by external senses. Since God's essence is not perceived in either way, it cannot be conceived, hence it is unknown. The third proof argues that all we know of God (attributes) are common categories, not the unique essence itself; thus the specific essence remains unknown. This is a syllogism of the second figure. Then two objections are presented: (1) if the essence were unknown, we could not judge it as unknown, since judgment requires conception; (2) we do predicate attributes like existence and pre-eternity to the essence, implying it is known. The answer refutes both by noting that the properties of foods and medicines are unknown in themselves, yet we know their effects; likewise, God's essence is unknown in itself but known through its effects. Razi closes by noting that deeper issues arise but are not addressed here.

Key Terms
تصديق
تصور
الحواس الخمس
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what are the two ways we can conceive something?
A) Inner perception and external senses.
B) Reason and revelation.
C) Intellect and intuition.
D) Analogy and induction.

Key Takeaway
Because God's essence is not perceived through inner awareness or the senses, it cannot be conceived, and therefore remains unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you cannot conceive of God's essence, how do you actually know Him? Does this humbling truth push you to seek Him through His names and actions, or do you secretly assume you have figured Him out? What will you change today in your worship to acknowledge your mind's limits?
?
Have you ever relied on a feeling or a sensory experience as proof of God, only to find it fleeting? How does Razi's argument challenge your own attempts to grasp God intellectually? Can you accept that true knowledge of His essence is impossible, or will you keep striving in vain?
?
The passage says assent depends on conception. If God's essence is inconceivable, on what basis do you give your assent to His existence? Examine your faith: is it built on human proofs or on divine revelation that transcends conception? Which foundation will you trust more from now on?

Third Proof and Refutation of Objections
Third proof, objections, and answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that God's essence is unknowable, along with two objections and their refutation.

Core Idea
What we know of God are common attributes, not the unique essence.

Imam Razi presents a third proof based on syllogism: the attributes we know are shared concepts (like existence, oneness), not the specific essence. Then he addresses two objections from those who claim essence is known, refuting them with an analogy from foods and medicines. This lesson covers the complete dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the Arabic and translation for the third proof. It is a syllogism in the second figure. Identify the conclusion that 'He, inasmuch as He is He, is unknown to humans.'
The key phrase: 'وهذا قياس جلي من الشكل الثاني'.
2
Study the two objections
The opponents argue (1) that judgment requires conception, so we must conceive essence to judge it unknown, and (2) we do predicate attributes to essence, implying it is known. Understand each.
Objection 1: 'لولا أن تلك الحقيقة معلومة، لامتنع الحكم عليها بأنها غير معلومة.' Objection 2: 'لولا أن تلك الحقيقة من حيث هي هي معلومة وإلا لما أمكن الحكم عليها بهذه الصفات.'
3
Analyze the answer
Razi refutes both objections with the example of foods and medicines: their specific properties are unknown in themselves, yet we know they produce certain effects. Similarly, God's essence is unknown in itself, but known through effects.
The analogy: 'تنتقض هاتان الحجتان بخواص الأغذية والأدوية...'

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That we cannot know anything about God if His essence is unknown.
Correction: We can know God's attributes and effects; only the specific essence remains unknown.

Checkpoint
How does Razi refute the objection that we predicate attributes to God's essence, implying it is known?
A) He denies that we predicate attributes.

C) He argues that attributes are not really predicated.
D) He says the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The third proof, together with the refutation of objections, solidifies that God's essence is unknowable even though we know His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
After reading this passage, are you content with not knowing God's essence? Or do you feel a restless desire to know Him directly—and what does that desire reveal about your heart's posture before the unknowable?

Three Types of Knowledge: Razi's Classification
Razi enumerates three types of knowledge with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's threefold classification of knowledge: inferential, essential, and visionary.

Core Idea
Knowledge comes in three ranks: inferential, essential, and visionary.

Razi begins by establishing that not all knowledge is equal. Before discussing whether we can know God directly, he clarifies three types of knowledge we experience: knowledge by inference, knowledge by direct inner awareness, and knowledge by vision. This classification sets the stage for the theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the three types of knowledge through examples.
Pay attention to the examples: burner, pain/joy, and color/vision.
2
Identify each type
Label each example as either inferential (bi al-tabaʿ wa al-ʿaraḍ), essential (bi al-dhāt wa al-ḥaqīqa), or visionary (ru'ya).
Notice that Razi ranks them: inferential weakest, visionary strongest.
3
Reflect on the ranking
Ask yourself why visionary knowledge is considered the highest and what that implies about knowing God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في بيان أن كنه حقيقة الله تعالى وإن لم يكن الآن معلوما فهل يصح أن يصير معلوما للبشر؟ اعلم أنا إذا رأينا الاحتراق حاصلا في الجسم، علمنا أنه لابد لذلك الاحتراق من محرق. وذلك المحرق معلوم، لكن علمنا يكون بالتبع والعرض. فإنا لا نعلم في هذا الوقت أن ذلك المحرق ما هو ؟ بل إنه شيء ما، معين في نفسه، مجهول التعين عند العقل. ومن لوازمه حصول هذا الاحتراق. وأيضا إذا وجدنا في أنفسنا ألما ولذة وغما وفرحًا . فهذه الماهيات معلومة علما لا بالتبع والعرض، بل بالذات والحقيقة. وهذه المرتبة أقوى وأجلى من المرتبة الأولى . وأيضا إذا أدركنا بالعين لونا وضوءا، ثم غمضنا العين فإنا ندرك تفرقة بديهية بين الحالتين مع أن العلم بذلك اللون المخصوص حاصل في الحالتين وتلك التفرقة هي المسماة بالرؤية. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنا إذا استدللنا بوجود الممكنات على وجود الصانع فقد حصل النوع الأول من العلم والمعرفة . أما النوع الثاني والثالث فهل هو ممكن الحصول ؟ قال بعضهم : هذا غير ممكن . لأنه غير متناه والعقل متناه، وإدراك غير المتناهي بالمتناهي محال. ومن المحققين من توقف في جوازه وامتناعه وقال : لا سبيل للعقل إلى معرفة هذه المضايق، بل السمع لما دل على أن المؤمنين يرون الله تعالى يوم القيامة، دل ذلك على كون هاتين المرتبتين ممكنتين. ولا شك أنه لا حال للبشر أشرف من هاتين الحالتين. فنسأل الله أن يجعلنا بفضله أهلا لها والله الموفق والمعين.
The second chapter: On explaining that the quiddity of God's reality, even if it is not known now, can it become known to humans? Know that when we see burning taking place in a body, we know that there must be a burner for that burning. That burner is known, but our knowledge is by way of consequence and accident. For we do not know at that time what that burner is; rather, it is something, determined in itself, unknown in its determination to the intellect. And from its concomitant properties is the occurrence of this burning. Also, when we find in ourselves pain, pleasure, sorrow, and joy, these quiddities are known by knowledge not consequential and accidental, but by essence and reality. This rank is stronger and clearer than the first rank. Also, when we perceive by the eye a color and light, then close the eye, we perceive an intuitive distinction between the two states, even though knowledge of that specific color is obtained in both states, and that distinction is what is called vision (ru'ya). Once you know this, we say: If we infer from the existence of contingent things to the existence of the Maker, then the first type of knowledge and cognition is obtained. As for the second and third types, are they possible of attainment? Some said: This is not possible, because He is infinite and the intellect is finite, and perceiving the infinite by the finite is impossible. And among the verifiers (muḥaqqiqīn) are those who suspended judgment concerning its permissibility and impossibility and said: There is no way for the intellect to know these straits; rather, since revelation indicates that the believers will see God Most High on the Day of Resurrection, that indicates that these two ranks are possible. There is no doubt that no state of humans is nobler than these two states. So we ask God to make us, by His grace, worthy of them. God is the Giver of success and the Helper.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and discussion about whether the quiddity (kunh) of God's reality, though currently unknown, can become known to humans. Razi first explains three types of knowledge: (1) Knowledge by inference (bi al-tabaʿ wa al-ʿaraḍ), like knowing a burner from the effect of burning—we know it exists but not its specific identity. (2) Knowledge by direct inner awareness (bi al-dhāt wa al-ḥaqīqa), like pain or joy—these are known essentially and are clearer. (3) Knowledge by vision (ru'ya), like seeing a color with the eye and then distinguishing the state after closing the eyes—this is the strongest. Then Razi applies this: inferring the Maker from contingent things gives the first type. The second and third types involve direct awareness or vision of God. Some argue they are impossible because God is infinite and the finite intellect cannot grasp the infinite. However, some verifiers suspended judgment, relying on revelation: the promise that believers will see God on the Day of Resurrection shows such knowledge is possible. Razi notes that no state is nobler than these, and asks God to grant them.

Key Terms
كنه
علم بالتبع والعرض
علم بالذات والحقيقة
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowledge is the same kind.
Correction: Razi shows there are three distinct ranks: inferential (weak), essential (stronger), and visionary (strongest).

Checkpoint
According to Razi, which type of knowledge is the strongest?
A) Inferential knowledge
B) Essential knowledge
C) Visionary knowledge
D) All are equally strong

Key Takeaway
Razi identifies three distinct kinds of knowledge, with visionary being the highest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these three kinds of knowledge do you most rely on when thinking about your Lord—inferential reasoning, direct experience of your soul, or a deeper intuitive vision? And what does that reliance say about where you truly stand with Him?
?
When you feel pain or joy, do you merely acknowledge it, or do you use that essential self-knowledge to recognize that your soul is a sign of a greater reality—the One who feels without limitation? What would change if you saw every internal state as a window to the Divine?
?
You have likely heard of the vision of Allah in Paradise. But are you seeking that vision now through reflection and purity? Or are you content with secondhand knowledge, satisfied with 'knowing that He exists' without truly longing to see Him? Make a concrete intention today to move from inferential to visionary knowledge.

6 min
Can We Know God Directly? Possibility of Higher Knowledge
Razi applies the three types to knowledge of God and discusses the possibility of higher knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether direct knowledge or vision of God is possible, and how revelation resolves the issue.

Core Idea
Inferential knowledge of God is certain, but direct knowledge and vision require revelation.

Razi now applies his classification to the question of knowing God. He affirms that inferential knowledge of God is achieved through reflection on creation. But can we have essential awareness or vision of God? Some say no, due to God's infinity. Others suspend judgment and rely on revelation. Razi leans toward possibility, citing the promise of seeing God on the Day of Resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Read the application passage
Read from 'Once you know this, we say' to the end. Notice how Razi applies the three types to knowledge of God.
2
Identify the first type achieved
Razi says inferring the Maker from contingent things gives the first type of knowledge. This is inferential knowledge.
This is the knowledge we have now.
3
Understand the objection
Some argue that the second and third types are impossible because God is infinite and the intellect is finite. The finite cannot grasp the infinite.
4
See the role of revelation
The verifiers (muhaqqiqun) suspend judgment and rely on revelation: since the Quran promises believers will see God on the Day of Resurrection, that shows these higher ranks are possible.
Razi does not decide via reason alone; he points to revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في بيان أن كنه حقيقة الله تعالى وإن لم يكن الآن معلوما فهل يصح أن يصير معلوما للبشر؟ اعلم أنا إذا رأينا الاحتراق حاصلا في الجسم، علمنا أنه لابد لذلك الاحتراق من محرق. وذلك المحرق معلوم، لكن علمنا يكون بالتبع والعرض. فإنا لا نعلم في هذا الوقت أن ذلك المحرق ما هو ؟ بل إنه شيء ما، معين في نفسه، مجهول التعين عند العقل. ومن لوازمه حصول هذا الاحتراق. وأيضا إذا وجدنا في أنفسنا ألما ولذة وغما وفرحًا . فهذه الماهيات معلومة علما لا بالتبع والعرض، بل بالذات والحقيقة. وهذه المرتبة أقوى وأجلى من المرتبة الأولى . وأيضا إذا أدركنا بالعين لونا وضوءا، ثم غمضنا العين فإنا ندرك تفرقة بديهية بين الحالتين مع أن العلم بذلك اللون المخصوص حاصل في الحالتين وتلك التفرقة هي المسماة بالرؤية. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنا إذا استدللنا بوجود الممكنات على وجود الصانع فقد حصل النوع الأول من العلم والمعرفة . أما النوع الثاني والثالث فهل هو ممكن الحصول ؟ قال بعضهم : هذا غير ممكن . لأنه غير متناه والعقل متناه، وإدراك غير المتناهي بالمتناهي محال. ومن المحققين من توقف في جوازه وامتناعه وقال : لا سبيل للعقل إلى معرفة هذه المضايق، بل السمع لما دل على أن المؤمنين يرون الله تعالى يوم القيامة، دل ذلك على كون هاتين المرتبتين ممكنتين. ولا شك أنه لا حال للبشر أشرف من هاتين الحالتين. فنسأل الله أن يجعلنا بفضله أهلا لها والله الموفق والمعين.
The second chapter: On explaining that the quiddity of God's reality, even if it is not known now, can it become known to humans? Know that when we see burning taking place in a body, we know that there must be a burner for that burning. That burner is known, but our knowledge is by way of consequence and accident. For we do not know at that time what that burner is; rather, it is something, determined in itself, unknown in its determination to the intellect. And from its concomitant properties is the occurrence of this burning. Also, when we find in ourselves pain, pleasure, sorrow, and joy, these quiddities are known by knowledge not consequential and accidental, but by essence and reality. This rank is stronger and clearer than the first rank. Also, when we perceive by the eye a color and light, then close the eye, we perceive an intuitive distinction between the two states, even though knowledge of that specific color is obtained in both states, and that distinction is what is called vision (ru'ya). Once you know this, we say: If we infer from the existence of contingent things to the existence of the Maker, then the first type of knowledge and cognition is obtained. As for the second and third types, are they possible of attainment? Some said: This is not possible, because He is infinite and the intellect is finite, and perceiving the infinite by the finite is impossible. And among the verifiers (muḥaqqiqīn) are those who suspended judgment concerning its permissibility and impossibility and said: There is no way for the intellect to know these straits; rather, since revelation indicates that the believers will see God Most High on the Day of Resurrection, that indicates that these two ranks are possible. There is no doubt that no state of humans is nobler than these two states. So we ask God to make us, by His grace, worthy of them. God is the Giver of success and the Helper.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and discussion about whether the quiddity (kunh) of God's reality, though currently unknown, can become known to humans. Razi first explains three types of knowledge: (1) Knowledge by inference (bi al-tabaʿ wa al-ʿaraḍ), like knowing a burner from the effect of burning—we know it exists but not its specific identity. (2) Knowledge by direct inner awareness (bi al-dhāt wa al-ḥaqīqa), like pain or joy—these are known essentially and are clearer. (3) Knowledge by vision (ru'ya), like seeing a color with the eye and then distinguishing the state after closing the eyes—this is the strongest. Then Razi applies this: inferring the Maker from contingent things gives the first type. The second and third types involve direct awareness or vision of God. Some argue they are impossible because God is infinite and the finite intellect cannot grasp the infinite. However, some verifiers suspended judgment, relying on revelation: the promise that believers will see God on the Day of Resurrection shows such knowledge is possible. Razi notes that no state is nobler than these, and asks God to grant them.

Key Terms
كنه
غير متناه
متناه
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason alone can prove whether direct knowledge or vision of God is possible.
Correction: Razi shows that reason cannot decide; revelation is needed to confirm possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the verifiers (muhaqqiqun), what is the basis for the possibility of the second and third types of knowledge?
A) Rational demonstration
B) The consensus of philosophers
C) The revelation that believers will see God on the Day of Resurrection
D) The analogy of knowing a burner from burning

Key Takeaway
The possibility of direct knowledge and vision of God is settled not by reason alone but by revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that inferential knowledge of God is certain, but direct knowledge and vision are only possible in the afterlife. Are you content with knowing God only through His signs and proofs, or do you long for a deeper, more direct encounter? How does this longing shape your hope for the Hereafter and your efforts to prepare for it?
?
The passage shows that reason alone cannot settle the possibility of seeing God; revelation must be trusted. In your own life, where do you find yourself relying more on your intellect than on divine guidance when it comes to matters of faith? Will you commit to submitting to revelation even when your reason questions it?
?
Razi prays to be made worthy of the highest states of knowledge and vision. Do you truly desire these states with the same intensity? What concrete changes in your worship, character, or priorities would reflect that desire?

The Second Argument: Impossibility of Two Effects from One Aspect
Razi presents the second sophistical argument against multiplicity from one cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that a single simple cause cannot produce two distinct effects from exactly the same consideration.

Core Idea
One cause, one aspect, one effect — two effects would contradict the oneness.

The philosophers' second argument formalizes a simple intuition: if a cause produces A, then from that same aspect it cannot produce B, because A and B are different. This is not a trivial point; it establishes a key principle in causality that affects arguments about divine simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the second argument in Arabic once. Notice the use of 'alif' and 'ba' as placeholders.
The argument is structured as a conditional.
2
Identify the key components
Identify the single cause (شيء واحد), the single consideration (باعتبار واحد), and the two distinct effects (الألف والباء).
3
Follow the logical contradiction
Trace the reasoning: from the aspect that yields alif, ba is not yielded, and vice versa. Conclude that both cannot proceed from the same consideration.
This relies on the principle of non-contradiction.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument challenges the idea of a simple divine essence producing multiple attributes without distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : إذا صدر عن الشيء الواحد باعتبار واحد الألف والباء - والألف من ذلك لا شك أنه غير الباء - فمن حيث إنه صدر عنه الألف لم يصدر عنه الباء؛ لأنه الحيث صدر عنه الباء، والباء ليس بالألف. ومن حيث إنه صدر عنه الباء، لم يصدر عنه الألف.

الشبهة الثالثة : العلة لا بد أن تكون ملائمة للمعلول، والشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد، لو أوجب الشيئين المختلفين لزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد ملائما لشيئين مختلفين والملائم للشيئين المختلفين مختلف فيلزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد، مخالفا لنفسه محال.

الشبهة الرابعة لما شاهدنا أن تأثير النار هو التسخين، وتأثير الماء هو التبريد، استدللنا بهذين الأثرين على أن طبيعة الماء مخالفة لطبيعة النار، وإذا كان اختلاف الأثر دالا على اختلاف المؤثر، فبأن يدل على تعدد المؤثر أولى.
The second sophistical argument: If from one thing by one consideration proceed alif and ba - and alif is undoubtedly not ba - then insofar as alif proceeds from it, ba does not proceed from it, because insofar as ba proceeds from it, and ba is not alif. And insofar as ba proceeds from it, alif does not proceed from it.

The third sophistical argument: The cause must be suitable for the effect, and if one thing by one consideration were to necessitate two different things, it would necessitate that one thing by one consideration be suitable for two different things, and that which is suitable for two different things is different, so it would necessitate that one thing by one consideration be opposed to itself, which is impossible.

The fourth sophistical argument: Since we observe that the effect of fire is heating and the effect of water is cooling, we infer from these two effects that the nature of water is different from the nature of fire. And if difference of effect indicates difference of the effecter, then it is even more indicative of multiplicity of the effecter.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents three separate objections from the philosophers. The second argument uses the example of A and B: if a single simple cause produces both by the same consideration, then from the aspect that gives A, B is not given, and vice versa—so they cannot both proceed from the same aspect. The third argument relies on the principle of suitability: a cause must be suitable for its effect; if it were suitable for two different effects, it would have to be different from itself, a contradiction. The fourth argument is empirical: different natural effects (heat from fire, cold from water) indicate different causes; similarly, different effects from one cause would argue for multiplicity in the cause.

Key Terms
شيء واحد
باعتبار واحد
علة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is trivial and doesn't apply to God.
Correction: The argument is a logical principle that applies to any simple cause; it challenges the notion that a single divine essence could be the source of multiple distinct attributes or effects without distinction in the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the logical outcome of the second argument?
A) Two effects can proceed from one cause in different respects.
B) One cause cannot produce two different effects from the same aspect.
C) A cause must be composite to produce multiple effects.
D) The cause's simplicity allows multiple effects.

Key Takeaway
The second argument forces the theologian to consider the aspect (اعتبار) when attributing multiple effects to a single cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever treat God's actions as if they come from multiple 'aspects' that could conflict? How does this argument challenge you to see everything that happens as coming from one simple divine will?
?
Consider a time you attributed two different outcomes to God - one you liked and one you disliked. Did you unconsciously divide God's causality into good and bad aspects? How must you repent from such thinking?
?
This argument shows the logical impossibility of two effects from one aspect. If you truly believe God is one and simple, can you still harbor doubts that He is the sole source of every event in your life? What specific doubt do you need to surrender to this truth today?

The Third Argument: The Suitability Principle
Razi presents the third sophistical argument based on the principle of suitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that a cause must be suitable for its effect, and how this rules out one cause producing two different effects.

Core Idea
A cause must fit its effect; one cause cannot fit two different effects.

The third argument appeals to the intuitive notion that a cause must be appropriate or 'suitable' (ملائمة) to its effect. If one thing were to necessitate two different effects, it would have to be suitable for both, which would require it to be different from itself—an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the third argument in Arabic. Pay attention to the term 'ملائمة' (suitability).
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the suitability requirement
The cause must be suitable for its effect; this is a basic causal principle.
3
Analyze the contradiction
If one thing produces two different effects, it must be suitable for both. But that which is suitable for two different things must itself be different, leading to a contradiction that the one thing is both itself and different from itself.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Thus, it is impossible for a single simple cause to produce two different effects.
This strengthens the case against multiplicity in the divine essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : إذا صدر عن الشيء الواحد باعتبار واحد الألف والباء - والألف من ذلك لا شك أنه غير الباء - فمن حيث إنه صدر عنه الألف لم يصدر عنه الباء؛ لأنه الحيث صدر عنه الباء، والباء ليس بالألف. ومن حيث إنه صدر عنه الباء، لم يصدر عنه الألف.

And insofar as ba proceeds from it, alif does not proceed from it.

Key Terms
ملائمة
علة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The suitability principle is arbitrary and not required.
Correction: The principle is foundational in causality: effects bear a specific relation to their causes; otherwise, any cause could produce any effect, leading to chaos.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the third argument draw?
A) A cause must be suitable for its effect, so one cause can produce many effects.
B) One cause cannot produce two different effects because it would have to be suitable for both, leading to a contradiction.
C) Suitability is irrelevant to causality.
D) Two different causes can produce the same effect.

Key Takeaway
The third argument establishes that the principle of suitability between cause and effect prohibits a single cause from producing two different effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there been a moment when you assumed that a single intention of yours could only produce one outcome, but then you saw multiple effects from it? Does this argument make you doubt the validity of those effects, or does it remind you that your intentions are not simple like the divine essence? How does that distinction affect your trust in Allah's creative power?
?
This argument presents a logical impossibility if a simple cause produces multiple effects. When you think of Allah's creation—vast and diverse—does your heart ever feel a tension between His oneness and the multiplicity of what He creates? What verse or hadith settles this for you? If you cannot recall one, will you search for it today and let it become a shield against such doubts?

The Fourth Argument: Empirical Inference from Nature
Razi presents the fourth sophistical argument from empirical observation of natural effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the empirical argument that different effects in nature indicate different causes, and how this is used to argue against a single cause producing multiple effects.

Core Idea
Different effects point to different causes; sameness of cause leads to sameness of effect.

The fourth argument moves from observation to principle: we see that fire heats and water cools – different effects from different natures. From this, we infer that difference in effect indicates difference in cause. Therefore, if we observe different effects, they must come from different causes, not one single cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the fourth argument in Arabic. Note the empirical examples: fire and water.
2
Understand the empirical premise
We observe that fire causes heating and water causes cooling. These are different effects.
3
Follow the inference
We infer that the nature of fire differs from the nature of water because their effects differ.
This is an inductive generalization.
4
Apply the principle
The principle: difference in effect indicates difference in cause. Thus, if there were multiple effects from one cause, that would imply multiplicity in the cause, contradicting its unity.
The argument uses 'first' (أولى) to emphasize that the principle holds even more strongly for different effects from one cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second sophistical argument: If from one thing by one consideration proceed alif and ba - and alif is undoubtedly not ba - then insofar as alif proceeds from it, ba does not proceed from it, because insofar as ba proceeds from it, and ba is not alif. And insofar as ba proceeds from it, alif does not proceed from it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that all different effects require completely separate causes.
Correction: The argument uses 'أولى' meaning 'more fitting', but it does not prove absolute separation; it only suggests that many effects from one cause would imply multiplicity in the cause.

Checkpoint
What is the key inference drawn from the example of fire and water?
A) Fire and water have similar natures.
B) Different effects imply different causes.
C) The same cause can produce both heating and cooling.
D) Observation is irrelevant to causality.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument uses empirical observation to establish the principle that different effects point to different causes, challenging the possibility of a single cause producing multiple distinct effects.

?

?
Is there an area of your life where you have assumed that a particular outcome must have a specific worldly cause, forgetting that Allah can bring about any effect directly? What would change if you truly believed that every effect is from Him alone?

First Proof Against Soul as Body: Self-Persistence
Razi presents the first proof against identifying the soul with the observable body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against identifying the soul with the observable body, based on the persistence of self-awareness despite constant bodily change.

Core Idea
You are not your body — your body changes, but you remain the same self.

Razi begins refuting the view that the soul is the sensible body by appealing to immediate self-knowledge. He argues that we all recognize ourselves as the same person over decades, yet our bodies undergo constant transformation. This simple observation decisively separates the self from the physical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice how Razi builds from self-evident knowledge to a conclusive separation.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بداهة عقلي' (self-evidence of intellect).
2
Identify the key intuition
Razi claims we know by immediate rational intuition (badāhat ʿaqliyya) that we are the same person over time. This is the anchor of the argument.
This intuition is presented as undeniable, not needing proof.
3
Understand the bodily change argument
Razi lists ways the body changes: fatness, thinness, sweat, waste, and the need for food to replace dissolved parts. Since the body is not constant while the self is, they cannot be identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول الأول: هو اختيار طائفة عظيمة من المتكلمين إلا أنه ضعيف ويدل على ضعفه وجهان : الأول : أني أعلم ببداهة عقلي أني الآن هو الذي كنت موجودا قبل هذا اليوم بعشرين سنة وهذا الهيكل المحسوس الموجود ليس هو الذي كان موجودًا قبل ذلك بعشرين سنة ؛ لأن أجزاء هذا الهيكل متبدلة تارة بالسمن والهزال، وتارة بسائر أنواع التحللات من العرق وانفصال الفضلات من الدماميل وغيرها ولأنه محتاج إلى الغذاء والغذاء عبارة عما يصير بدلا لما تحلل من البدن وكيف لا يكون كذلك، والبدن حار رطب والحار إذا عمل في الرطب أصعد عنه الأجزاء البخارية وحللها ؟ وإذا ثبت أن هذا الإنسان هو الذي بعينه كان موجودا قبل هذا اليوم بعشرين سنة، وثبت أن هذا الهيكل ليس هو الذي كان موجودا قبل ذلك، ثبت قطعا أن الإنسان ليس عبارة عن هذا الهيكل.
The first doctrine: it is the choice of a great group of theologians (mutakallimūn), except that it is weak, and there are two proofs for its weakness: The first: that I know by the self-evidence of my intellect that I now am the one who existed twenty years before this day, and this sensible existing structure is not the one that existed before that twenty years ago; because the parts of this structure change, sometimes by fatness and emaciation, and sometimes by other types of dissolution (taḥallulāt) from sweat and separation of wastes from abscesses and others, and because it needs nourishment, and nourishment is what replaces what has dissolved from the body. How could it not be so? The body is hot and moist; when heat acts upon moisture it raises vaporous parts and dissolves them. So if it is established that this human being is the very same one who existed twenty years before this day, and it is established that this structure is not the one that existed before that, it is decisively established that the human being is not identical with this structure.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the view that the soul is the observable body. Razi states that the body (haykal) undergoes constant change through fatness, thinness, sweating, loss of wastes, and the need for food, which replaces what dissolves. In contrast, the person remains the same self over time, as known by immediate rational intuition. Since the persisting self cannot be identical with a perpetually changing body, the soul is not the physical structure.

Key Terms
بداهة عقلي (badāhat ʿaqliyya)
هيكل محسوس (haykal mahsūs)
تحلل (taḥallul)
غذاء (ghidhāʾ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the body also persists because it remains recognizable.
Correction: Razi argues that the body persists only through replacement of parts, not identity. The self, by contrast, is known to be strictly identical over time.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of Razi's first proof against the soul being the body?
A) The body does not change over time
B) The self is known to be the same, while the body changes entirely
C) The soul is made of fire
D) The body is corruptible

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof establishes that the soul cannot be the body because the self remains identical while the body is in constant flux.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body has changed completely since you were a child, yet you know you are the same person, what is it in you that remains? How does that affect your understanding of who you are in front of Allah right now?
?
Razi says you know by self-evidence that you are the same self. But do you live as though you are more than your body? When you sin, do you blame your body or your soul? What does that reveal about your true belief?
?
What would it mean for your accountability on the Day of Judgment if you were nothing but your physical body? How does this proof change how you view the intentions and actions that arise from your true self?

Everything Besides God Is Subject to Annihilation: Al-Razi's Proof from Contingency
Al-Razi states that everything besides God is subject to annihilation and gives a proof based on contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's rational proof that every created thing inherently possesses the possibility of annihilation.

Core Idea
Every originated thing carries within its essence the permanent possibility of not existing.

Al-Razi now takes up the question of whether anything besides God can be annihilated. The philosophers held that certain entities like human souls and celestial bodies, though originated, are eternal. Al-Razi counters with a clear argument rooted in the nature of contingency: whatever is created has an essence that is equally open to existence and non-existence. This capacity is inseparable from it, so annihilation is always a possibility for everything other than God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the philosophers' claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Observe what the philosophers assert about human souls, celestial intellects, bodies, and prime matter.
The philosophers admit these are originated but claim they are not susceptible to annihilation.
2
Identify the two premises of al-Razi's argument
Al-Razi's proof has two key premises: (1) everything besides God is originated, and (2) every originated thing has a reality that is susceptible to existence and non-existence. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the concept of 'necessary concomitant' (lāzim)
Al-Razi says the susceptibility to non-existence is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the quiddity (māhiyya) of every originated thing. Consider what it means for something to be inseparable from an essence.
Necessary concomitants are always present as long as the essence exists.
4
See how the conclusion follows
Since the susceptibility to non-existence is always present, every originated thing is always capable of being annihilated. Therefore, it is possible for everything besides God to cease to exist.
This does not mean they will be annihilated, only that it is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في بيان أن ما سوى الله تعالى فإنه يجوز الفناء عليه : قالت الفلاسفة : الأرواح البشرية وإن كانت محدثة إلا أنها أبدية غير قابلة العدم، وأيضًا العقول الفلكية والنفوس الفلكية والأجرام الفلكية والهيولى غير قابلة للعدم. لنا: أنا بينا في مسألة حدوث العالم أن كل ما سوى الله تعالى فهو محدث، وكل محدث فإن حقيقته قابلة للعدم والوجود وهذه القابلية من لوازم الماهية وكل ما كان من لوازم الماهية فإنه واجب الدوام في جميع زمان دوام الماهية، فإذن قابلية العدم من لوازم ماهية كل ما سوى الله تعالى وهذا يقتضي جواز العدم على كل ما سوى الله تعالى.
The second chapter: On explaining that everything besides God, exalted, is subject to annihilation. The philosophers said: human souls, although originated, are eternal, not susceptible to non-existence; likewise the celestial intellects, celestial souls, celestial bodies, and prime matter are not susceptible to non-existence. Our [proof]: we have explained in the question of the temporal origination of the world that everything besides God is originated (muḥdath). And every originated thing, its reality (ḥaqīqa) is susceptible to non-existence and existence, and this susceptibility is one of the necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of its quiddity (māhiyya). And whatever is a necessary concomitant of quiddity is necessarily perpetual throughout the entire duration of that quiddity. Therefore, the susceptibility to non-existence is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity of everything besides God, and this entails the possibility of non-existence for everything besides God.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This lesson introduces a new topic: the possibility of annihilation (fanā') for everything other than God. Al-Razi first states the position of the philosophers (falāsifa): they hold that human souls, celestial intellects, souls, bodies, and prime matter, although originated (muḥdatha), are eternal and cannot cease to exist. Al-Razi then presents his proof: everything besides God is originated (as proven in the earlier discussion on the temporality of the world). Every originated thing has a reality (ḥaqīqa) that is equally capable of existence and non-existence. This capacity (qābiliyya) is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of its quiddity (māhiyya). Necessary concomitants of quiddity are always present during the quiddity's duration. Therefore, the capacity for non-existence is always present in every created thing, meaning that non-existence is always a possibility. Thus, everything besides God is subject to annihilation.

Key Terms
الفناء
الحدوث
الحقيقة
الماهية
اللوازم
القابلية
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that being originated automatically entails being perishable at all times, meaning annihilation is necessary.
Correction: Al-Razi's argument is about inherent capacity (qābiliyya), not actual annihilation. The possibility exists, but actual annihilation depends on God's will.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the basis for the possibility of annihilation of everything besides God?
A) Because God is omnipotent and can destroy anything.
B) Because everything other than God is originated and its essence entails susceptibility to non-existence.
C) Because the philosophers admit that human souls are originated.
D) Because the quiddity of all things changes over time.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of annihilation is inherent in the very quiddity of every created thing because its essence is equally open to existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your quiddity carries the permanent possibility of non-existence. What would change in your daily choices if you truly lived with that awareness—not as a depressing thought, but as a clear truth?
?
Al-Razi proves that nothing besides God has inherent permanence. Where in your life are you relying on something other than God to give you security or identity? How will you realign that trust today?
?
If your own existence is always balanced on the edge of being and non-being, what does that say about your need for the Self-Subsisting (al-Qayyūm)? What one specific act of dependence on Him can you commit to right now?
