Everything Other Than God is Subject to Annihilation
Razi asserts that everything other than God is subject to annihilation and provides a rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam proof that everything besides God is contingent and possible for annihilation.

Core Idea
Everything other than God is inherently susceptible to ceasing to exist.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi here presents a crucial argument in Islamic theology: that all created things, including celestial souls and intellects, are inherently possible for annihilation. He first states the opposing view of the philosophers, then provides a rational proof based on the nature of originated things and necessary concomitants of essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the overall argument.
Notice the structure: first the philosophers' claim, then Razi's proof.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers argue that certain entities (human souls, celestial intellects etc.) are not subject to annihilation.
These entities are considered eternal in some philosophical traditions.
3
Analyze the proof structure
Break down Razi's proof into its premises and conclusion. Identify the key terms: محدث (originated), قابلية (susceptibility), لازم (necessary concomitant).
The proof relies on the nature of originated things and the permanence of essential concomitants.
4
Understand the concept of necessary concomitant
A necessary concomitant (لازم الماهية) is something that necessarily accompanies the essence and cannot be separated from it as long as the essence exists.
Here, susceptibility to both existence and non-existence is such a concomitant.
5
Apply the proof to all created things
Since everything other than God is created, it follows that annihilation is possible for all of them, not just some.
This counters the philosophers' exception list.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في بيان أن ما سوى الله تعالى فإنه يجوز الفناء عليه : قالت الفلاسفة : الأرواح البشرية وإن كانت محدثة إلا أنها أبدية غير قابلة العدم، وأيضًا العقول الفلكية والنفوس الفلكية والأجرام الفلكية والهيولى غير قابلة للعدم. لنا: أنا بينا في مسألة حدوث العالم أن كل ما سوى الله تعالى فهو محدث ، وكل محدث فإن حقيقته قابلة للعدم ،والوجود وهذه القابلية من لوازم الماهية وكل ما كان من لوازم الماهية فإنه واجب الدوام في جميع زمان دوام الماهية، فإذن قابلية العدم من لوازم ماهية كل ما سوى الله تعالى وهذا يقتضي جواز العدم على كل ما سوى الله تعالى.
The Second Chapter: On the Explanation that Everything Other than God, the Exalted, is Subject to Annihilation. The philosophers said: The human souls, though originated, are eternal, not susceptible to non-existence. Also, the celestial intellects, the celestial souls, the celestial bodies, and prime matter are not susceptible to non-existence. Our [argument]: We have demonstrated in the issue of the creation of the world that everything other than God, the Exalted, is originated, and every originated thing is such that its reality is susceptible to non-existence and existence, and this susceptibility is among the necessary concomitants of the essence. Everything that is among the necessary concomitants of the essence is necessarily permanent throughout the entire duration of the essence. Therefore, the susceptibility to non-existence is among the necessary concomitants of the essence of everything other than God, the Exalted. And this necessitates the possibility of non-existence for everything other than God, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a declaration and proof of the position that everything besides God is subject to annihilation (الفناء). The author first reports the opposing view of the philosophers, who claim that human souls, celestial intellects, celestial souls, celestial bodies, and prime matter are not subject to annihilation despite being originated. Then he gives his own proof: It was established earlier (in the chapter on the creation of the world) that everything other than God is originated (محدث). Every originated thing has a reality (حقيقة) that is susceptible to both existence and non-existence. This susceptibility is a necessary concomitant (لازم) of the essence. Since a necessary concomitant is permanent for the duration of the essence, it follows that susceptibility to non-existence is a necessary concomitant of every essence other than God. Therefore, non-existence is possible for everything other than God.

Key Terms
الفناء
الحدوث
الماهية
اللازم
القابلية
الوجود
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that everything other than God will inevitably be annihilated.
Correction: The proof only establishes the possibility (جواز) of annihilation, not its actual occurrence.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the proof that everything other than God is subject to annihilation?
A) Every originated thing's essence includes susceptibility to non-existence as a necessary concomitant
B) The philosophers admit it
C) It is proven by scripture
D) Annihilation is observed in the world

Key Takeaway
The susceptibility to non-existence is a necessary concomitant of all created essences, making annihilation possible for everything besides God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What are you holding onto in your life that you treat as if it could never cease to exist? How would your priorities change if you truly believed it could vanish tomorrow?

If you knew that the thing you rely on most—your health, wealth, or relationships—could be taken away at any moment, would you still cling to it? What does your attachment reveal about your belief in God's sole permanence?
?
When you face a loss or disappointment, do you feel angry at the world? Or do you recognize that only God is eternal and that everything else was always subject to annihilation?

The Thirteenth Proof: Preponderance of Promise over Threat
Imam Razi presents the Ash'ari argument that specific verses of pardon outweigh general verses of threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi's thirteenth proof uses the principle that specific verses override general verses to argue that verses of promise are preponderant over verses of threat.

Core Idea
Specific pardon verses tip the scale over general threat verses.

Imam Razi presents the Ash'ari argument that when general verses of promise and threat conflict, the specific verses indicating divine pardon give preponderance to the verses of promise. This is based on a well-established hermeneutical principle: when a specific text contradicts a general text, the specific is given precedence. Here, we see how this principle is applied to settle the theological debate on promise and threat.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
The passage is concise; note the logical connectors.
2
Identify the two premises
Find where Razi states that verses of pardon must be specific and verses of punishment must be general. Understand why this is necessary for their intended meaning.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Recognize the hermeneutical rule
Note the principle: when a specific and a general text conflict, the specific takes precedence. This is a standard rule in usul al-fiqh.
This rule is applied here to weigh verses.
4
Trace the conclusion
See how the rule leads to the conclusion that the general promise verses (supported by specific pardon verses) outweigh the general threat verses. The Mu'tazila position collapses.
The conclusion is a blow to Mu'tazila theology.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument is important for Ash'ari theology: it provides a textual basis for prioritizing God's mercy over punishment.
This is not about God's actual will but about scriptural interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث عشر : أن عمومات الوعد والوعيد لما تعارضت فنحن نرجح عمومات الوعد بالآيات الدالة على العفو والغفران وهذا ترجيح يجب المصير إليه، وذلك لأن آيات العفو لا بد أن تكون خاصة وآيات العقوبة والوعيد لا بد أن تكون عامة، وإلا لم تدل على مقصودهم، والعام والخاص إذا تعارضا ترجح الخاص على العام. فثبت بمجموع هذه الوجوه أن عمومات الوعد راجحة على عمومات الوعيد، فسقط كلام المعتزلة بالكلية.
The thirteenth: that when the general texts of promise and threat conflict, we give preponderance to the general texts of promise by means of the verses indicating pardon and forgiveness, and this is a preponderance that must be adopted. This is because verses of pardon must be specific, and verses of punishment and threat must be general; otherwise they would not indicate their intended meaning. And when the general and the specific conflict, the specific is given preponderance over the general. Thus, by all these considerations, it is established that the general texts of promise are preponderant over the general texts of threat, so the statement of the Mu'tazila falls entirely.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari argument for the preponderance of verses of promise over verses of threat. It is a classificatory argument based on textual principles. The author first states the premise: when general verses of promise and threat conflict, we give preponderance to the general verses of promise by appealing to the specific verses that indicate divine pardon and forgiveness. This preponderance is necessary because verses of pardon must be specific (otherwise they wouldn't refer to a particular group), while verses of punishment and threat must be general (otherwise they wouldn't serve as a deterrent). If a specific text conflicts with a general text, the specific is given precedence. Therefore, on this principle, the verses of promise (supported by specific pardon verses) outweigh the general threat verses. The conclusion is that the Mu'tazilite position collapses.

Key Terms
عمومات الوعد
عمومات الوعيد
العام والخاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument claims that God must forgive people because there are specific verses of pardon.
Correction: The argument is not a moral claim about God's obligation but a hermeneutical rule about scriptural interpretation: the specific verses of pardon outweigh the general threat verses in establishing what is more likely from the texts.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi say that verses of pardon must be specific?
A) Because God's mercy is limited to a few people.
B) Otherwise they would not indicate their intended meaning.
C) Because the Mu'tazila claim they are general.
D) Because they are contradicted by threat verses.

Key Takeaway
The specific verses of pardon make the general promises of reward more likely than the general threats of punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses of threat in the Quran, do you genuinely feel the weight of accountability, or do you dismiss them with a vague hope in mercy? This lesson shows that specific pardon verses tip the scale toward hope. In your own life, do you lean on those specific promises enough to change your actions? What one verse of pardon will you memorize and live by this week?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted on strict justice, but the Ash'aris argue that scripture itself leads us to hope. Have you ever judged someone as beyond redemption—including yourself—based on general fears? How does the specific nature of pardon verses in this passage challenge your unforgiveness? Write down one person you need to extend hope to because of what you learned.
?
If this argument is true—that God's scripture deliberately tips the scale toward mercy—then your default attitude should be hopeful, not anxious. Honestly, is your daily mindset dominated by fear of punishment or hope in forgiveness? What would it look like for you to live today with 'preponderant hope'? Commit to one specific action that reflects this trust.

Two Examples of Perfection and Deficiency in God's Essence
Al-Razi gives two examples of perfection/deficiency in essence: transcendence and the visibility of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the same rational principle—resembling deficiency is deficiency—can be used by opposing theological schools to argue for their positions on divine transcendence and the vision of God.

Core Idea
The same premise can lead to opposite conclusions on God's essence.

Imam al-Razi now gives two concrete examples of how the premise of perfection/deficiency applies to God's essence. These examples show that rational arguments often depend on what one considers 'deficient.' Both the proponent of transcendence and the anthropomorphism claim that the other's view implies resemblance to something deficient, yet they reach opposite conclusions. As you read, notice how each side uses the same logical rule.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Notice that al-Razi presents two numbered examples, each with two opposing positions.
2
Identify the two examples
The first example is about transcendence vs. anthropomorphism. The second is about whether God can be seen. Note how each argument uses the idea that resembling what is deficient is itself a deficiency.
3
Analyze each argument's structure
For each example, see how each side defines deficiency differently. In the first example, one side says a body is deficient; the other says non-existence is deficient. In the second, one says invisibility implies non-existence (deficient), the other says visibility implies direction (deficient).
The premise remains the same: 'resembling the deficient is deficiency.'
4
Reflect on the lesson
Ask yourself: how can the same premise yield two opposite conclusions? This shows that rational arguments alone may not settle theological disputes without additional agreed premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الكمال والنقصان في الذات فلنذكر فيه مثالين:
الأول: ما يتعلق بالتنزيه والتشبيه، فصاحب التنزيه يقول : لو كان جسما وجوهرًا، أو أو في مكان لكان مشابها لهذه المخلوقات وهي ناقصة ومشابهة الناقص نقصان فيلزم تنزيه الله تعالى عنه، والمجسم يقول : لو لم يكن متحيزا ولا مشارًا إليه بحسب الحس ولا في المكان لكان مشابها للعدم وهذا غاية النقصان تعالى الله عنه.
والثاني : ما يتعلق بالرؤية فنقول : الشيء الذي لا يرى يكون معدوما - وتعالى الله عنه - والمعتزلي يقول : الشيء الذي لا يرى يكون في مقابلة الرائي . وتعالى الله عنه.
As for perfection and deficiency in the essence, let us mention two examples regarding it: The first: what pertains to transcendence and anthropomorphism. The proponent of transcendence says: 'If He were a body, a substance, or in a place, He would resemble these creatures, and they are deficient, and resembling the deficient is deficiency, so it is necessary to transcend God Most High above that.' The anthropomorphism says: 'If He were not a body nor perceptible by sense nor in place, He would resemble non-existence, and this is the utmost of deficiency—far exalted is God above that.' The second: what pertains to vision. We say: 'A thing that is not seen is non-existent—far exalted is God above that.' The Mu'tazili says: 'A thing that is not seen is opposite to the viewer—far exalted is God above that.'
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage provides two concrete examples of how the premise of perfection/deficiency is applied to the essence of God. The first example concerns transcendence (tanzih) versus anthropomorphism (tashbih). The proponent of transcendence argues that if God had a body, substance, or location, He would resemble creatures, which are deficient, so that resemblance would be a deficiency; therefore God must be transcended of such attributes. The anthropomorphism (mujassim) counters that if God were not a body, perceptible, or in place, He would resemble non-existence, which is the utmost deficiency. Both sides use the same premise of perfection/deficiency but reach opposite conclusions. The second example concerns the vision of God (ru'ya). One position claims that an unseen thing is non-existent, so for God to be seen would be a perfection; the Mu'tazili claims that an unseen thing is opposite to the viewer, implying that if God were seen, He would be subject to a direction, which is a deficiency. Again, the same premise yields different positions.

Key Terms
التنزيه
التشبيه
الرؤية
المتحيز
المجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the passage is a simple proof for one theological position, e.g., that God must be transcendent or that God must be seen.
Correction: Al-Razi is not advocating for either side; he is showing how the same premise of perfection/deficiency can be employed by opposing schools, demonstrating that rational arguments alone may not resolve these debates.

Checkpoint
In the first example, what logical rule do both the proponent of transcendence and the anthropomorphism rely on?
A) Resembling a creature is a deficiency
B) Resembling something deficient is itself a deficiency
C) Non-existence is the ultimate deficiency
D) God must be a body

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that the same rational premise can lead to opposite theological conclusions depending on what one considers deficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a firm belief about God's nature, do you ever consider that your reasoning might mirror exactly the same structure as an opponent's? How does that possibility affect your intellectual humility before Allah?
?
If the great scholars of Islam could look at the same premise of 'perfection' and arrive at opposite conclusions, how can you be sure your current understanding of God is not missing a deeper dimension? Will you seek to understand both sides before settling on your view?
?
The passage shows that what one calls 'perfection' another calls 'deficiency'. What does this reveal about your own tendency to label ideas as 'obviously true' or 'obviously false' when talking about Allah? Are you willing to sit with the tension of this uncertainty and ask Allah for guidance?

Divine Transcendence
beginner
3 min
The Necessary Being Is Not a Locus or Located
Razi states and proves that God cannot be a locus or located.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Being cannot be a locus or located, based on the principle of independence from dependence.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being depends on nothing—so it cannot be a container or contained.

This brief passage from Imam Razi's Arba'in establishes a fundamental rule of divine transcendence. The Necessary Being, by definition, cannot be subject to the kind of dependency implied by being a locus (something that supports accidents) or a located object (something that inheres in another). Let's unpack this concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi packs an entire argument into one sentence.
The Arabic is very concise; each word carries weight.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the terms ḥāll (locus) and maḥall (located). A locus is that in which something inheres (like an accident), and a located is what inheres. Both entail dependence.
These terms are central to discussions of divine transcendence.
3
Grasp the proof
Razi says: if the Necessary Being were either a locus or located, then dependence (iftiqār) would 'recur'—meaning it would depend on something else. This contradicts its necessary existence.
The phrase 'otherwise dependence would recur' is the logical engine.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: why must the Necessary Being be free from all forms of being a locus or located? How does this protect tawḥīd?
This affirms God's absolute independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : كون واجب الوجود لذاته لا يكون حالا ولا مجلاً، وإلا لعاد الافتقار.
The second: that the Necessary Existent in essence is not a locus (ḥāll) nor a located (maḥall); otherwise dependence would recur.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This is a brief proof that the Necessary Being is neither a locus (that in which something inheres, like an accident in a substance) nor a located object (that which inheres in something else). If it were a locus, it would support accidents and thereby depend on them; if it were located, it would inhere in something else, also implying dependence. Since the Necessary Being is independent by essence, both possibilities are impossible. The phrase 'otherwise dependence would recur' means that if it could be a locus or located, it would be dependent on something else, contradicting its necessity.

Key Terms
حال (locus)
محل (located)
افتقار (dependence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God is 'everywhere' in a literal spatial sense, meaning He is located in places.
Correction: Razi's argument denies that God can be located anywhere, since location implies dependence on the place.

Checkpoint
What does 'lā yakūnu ḥāllan wa lā maḥallan' mean?
A) He is not a body nor a substance.
B) He is not a locus nor located.
C) He does not change nor move.
D) He is not seen nor perceived.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being is completely independent; therefore it can be neither a container nor contained.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever unconsciously imagine God in a specific direction (e.g., above the sky) as if He is located there? How does Razi's proof challenge that habit? Will you consciously train your mind to not locate God spatially, even in worship?
?
If God is completely independent and needs nothing from you, why do you still act as if your good deeds earn you a place with Him? In what area of your life do you rely on created things (money, status, people) as though they were necessary for your well-being? Write down one specific dependence you will repent from today.
?
The word 'dependence' (iftiqār) is used to prove God's transcendence. But everything in creation is dependent. How does recognizing your own total dependence on God change the way you face your biggest fear this week? Commit to one action that acknowledges this dependence.
